"THE BULLETIN" BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
Some Books Worth Reading.
In the Midst of Life, by Ambrose Bierce.
Boards, 2s 6d. Some wonderfully fine
short stories.
The White Company, by Conan Doyle,
Paper. 2s lOd. A good historical novel.
Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.
Adventures of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle, Paper, 2s lOd
A Study in Scarlet, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
Is 3d.
The Sign of Four, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
2s lOd. First-class detective stories.
The Woman in White, by Wilkie Collins.
Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is 3d. The anchor’s
best book, well printed.
The Cloister and the Hearth, by Charles
Reade. Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is 3d.
Tess of the D’Urbervilles, by Thomas Hardy.
Paper, 2s lOd. A masterpiece of English
fiction.
Crime and Punishment, by Fedor Dostoieff-
sky. Paper, 3s. One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.
The Anarchist Peril, by Felix Dubois.
Cloth, 6s 6d. A startling exposure.
Lorna Doone, by R. D. Blackmore. Cloth,
3s. A charming English romance.
Looking Backward, by Edward Bellamy.
Paper, Is 3d. The bane or the antidote.
Pictures of the Socialistic Future, by Eugene
Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or
the bane.
Studies in Pessimism, by Schopenhauer.
Paper, Is. 3d. Delightfully cynical.
The Green Carnation. Paper, 45.; cloth,
6s. 6d. Witty and amusing.
Virginibus Puerisque, by R. L. Stevenson.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Essays perennially charm-
ing.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d. A book chief among ten thousand
and altogether lovely.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s. Sketches, verses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
Pmse Fancies, by Richard Le Gallienne.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. A recent book of idyllic
essays.
Post-Prandial Philosophy, by Grant Allen.
Cloth, 4s. Readable essays.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table. The
Professor at the Breakfast Table. The
Poet at the Breakfast Table. By Dr.
O. W, Holmes. A beautifully-printed
edition ; neatly cloth-bound, 2s. lOd. each.
Also, pocket size, paper covers, 9d. each.
The Manxman, by Hall Caine (cloth, 45.).
A really fine novel, epic in breadth and
intensity.
The Tales of John Oliver Hobbes (including
“Some Emotions and a Moral,” “AStudy
in Temptations,” “A Bundle of Life,”
and “The Sinner’s Comedy”). Piquant
and clever. A charming edition, with
portrait of author ; cloth, 6s. -fid.
Aut Diabolus Aut Nihil and other stories.
Cloth, 4s. Clever and strong.
A Child of the Age, by Francis Adams.
Cloth, 4s 4d.
Kenelm Chillingly, by Bulwer Lytton. His
best novel; cloth, 4s Id (recommended) ;
also, .cloth, 2s 6d.
John Bull and Co. Cloth, 3s. lOd. Max’s
new book about Australasia j and South
Africa.
The Story of Francis Cludde ; cloth, 3s lOd.
Under the Red Robe, by Stanley Weyman.
Paper 2s JOd.
A Gentleman of France, by Stanley Wey-
man. Paper, 2s lOd.
My Lady Rotha, by Stanley Weyman.
Paper, 2s lOd.
Four clever and interesting new historical
novels.
Ships that Pass in the Night, by Beatrice
Harraden. Paper, 2s lOd; cloth, 4s.
Novel and clever.
Paradoxes, by Max Nordau. Paper, 3s.
Acute, fresh, thoughtful essays on many
interesting subjects.
National Railways, by James Hole. Cloth,
5s fid. A full, instructive and interesting
discussion of the State railway question.
Papa, Mamma, and Baby, by Gustave Droz
(English translation). A witty, naughty,
delightful book, which should be first upon
every list of wedding presents. Cloth,
2s 9d.
Labour and the Popular Welfare, by W. H.
Matlock. A clever and instructive essay,
from the anti-socialist standpoint. Limp
covers, Is 4d.
Isis Very Much Unveiled, by Edmund Gar-
rett. A scathing exposure of the Mahatma
forgeries ; paper, Is 3d.
The Yellow Book, the new unconventional
magazine, established for the express pur-
pose of giving Mrs. Grundy fits. Yols. 1,
2,3, 4 ; cloth, 6s fid each.
Songs of the Army of the Night, by Francis
Adams. New edition, with portrait and
memoir. Paper, Is 3d ; cloth, 3s 9d.
Vivid, intense, thrilling; a book to be
prized by every democrat
Esther Waters, by George Moore ; cloth, 6s.
6d. A strong and remarkable story.
The Woman Who Did, by Grant Allen.
Cloth, 4s. A strong statement, in tale-
form, of strong thoughts about marriage.
Memoir of George Higinbotham, by Prof.
Morris. Cloth, 9s 6d.
Degeneration, by Max Nordau. Cloth, 2s 6d.
William Watson’s Poems. Cloth, ss. 6d.
John Davidson’s Poems. Cloth, 5s 6d.
Australian Books.

lian novel.

Paper, 2s 9d ; Cloth, 3s 9d. The second-
best Australian novel.

Bs.

We wish it to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything w'e' sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few-
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, butit the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
IINIVEICSAI. I'KOVIDEKS.

Editor Bulletin, —“ Why are bulls more
savage in England ?” Answer : Because they
aren’t. In England they are allowed less liberty,
and the country is more crowded. In Australia a
herd-bull will hardly see a human being except
the stockman in twelve months, and the stockman
is always on his guard, and doesn’t mind a charge;
while in England people who are not on their
guard may any day come in the way of a farmer’s
bull.—R.
Gov. Hopetouu kept in his scrap-book
every paragraph or picture he could find in any
Australian paper, either about himself or Lady
Hopetouu, including “ the one referring to Lady
H.’s accident, and congratulations on the safety of
her little Irish nose.” That was the par. in which
The Bulletin expressed its felicitations on the
fact that the bandages upon the dainty little
“organ” relieved her from the eternal insistanoe
of the predominant characteristic of marvellous
Smellbourne.
As a young barrister, Higinbotham sank
to a penniless condition in Melbourne, for the
attorneys did not believe in him, evidently regard-
ing him as a visionary. His luck came round
through a mighty barrister getting drunk, where-
upon five briefs devolved on Higinbotham within
two days. He rose to the occasion and was a
made man. Higinbotham’s conscience prevented
him from ever making a very first-class Bar
income, and he gained financially by being hoisted
to a judgeship.
Newspaper reporters help to inflate the
gallant captains, and pursers, and other profes-
sional lady-killers of the mercantile marine. A
ship’s officer is, apparently, something to he
greased down by an admiring press, after the
passengers have thanked him all round for a most
delightful voyage, and left him in a puffed-up con-
dition. The other day a Melb. bird - fancier
received two pairs of choice canaries by a certain
steamer, and the local evening journal screamed,
“ Too much praise cannot bespoken of the manner
in which the birds were cared for on the way
out by Mr. Blank, the- chief steward.” Why con-
fine all the adulation to the gentleman who fed
the four canaries ? Probably the captain, his sub
ordinate mates, and the leading engineers deserve
public gratitude for their courtesy in saying,
“ Chirp, chirp !” every time they visited the birds
of passage.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Bucalypte Extract.
The writer of “Fugitive Notes” in S.M.
Herald naturally dislikes literary brilliancy.
What we want is “ a novel in which there is not a
clever saying in the whole book.” Now, if
“ A.8.C.” would only write a novel ——! The
Herald, now but a penny, is still tuppenny at
heart. In fact, it is now so dull that it is becom-
ing threepenny. Once, there were 'oases in
the desert—when the clever, cynical Dr. Neild
wrote a “Melbourne letter,” and Nugent Robert-
son contributed “As you Like it,” and Francis
Myers occasionally wrote an eloquent column ; but
now all the brightmen—theborn journalists, not the
dull clergy and the dry-as-dust lawyers—are on the
Daily Telegraph : Farrell, and Courtney, and
Peters, and Blackwell and TaperelK
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Oolemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
The Chinese of Sydney, some months ago,
collected amongst themselves £25, in Is. sub-
scriptions, as prizes fora “poetry-contest.” The
head of the movement was a Celestial fancy-goods
dealer, and competitive verses were received from
all parts of.N.S. Wales. The compositions were
forwarded to a Chinese literary society at Hong-
kong, and the awards of the judges have now
reached Sydney. The principal prize-winner is a
litterateur named Lee Choy Chong, who was
living in modest retirement in a suburban garden,
and it came as a surprise to the local Chinese that
a person possessing so much talent should not
have received earlier recognition. The gifted Mr.
Lee Chov Chong has now abandoned cabbagee
pursuits," and has been installed on the literary
staff of the Sydney Chinese newspaper, Quong
Yik Wah 80. He is perhaps the only poet in
Australasia who is.getting a living at the game.

Ed. Bulletin, —
Trenwith’s grievance is the attempt to form a Liberal
Association in' Victoria while there is already the Labor
and Land League, which, he contends, is doing all the
work.
Thrice is he armed, etc. Is Trenwith justified?
The trail of the hob-nailed superstition is over
the Labor League, and the taint on all its utter-
ances is that the manual laborer and his union are
the two objects for which the democracy exists.
Prominent among these men are certain J.’sP.
who adopt the Fat Man policy of packing benches
when semi-political trials are on strike oases,
etc. This putting of new wine into old bottles—
of compromising the new ideal with the old injus-
tice—is not a necessary adjunct of the Labor
League; hut it is typical of that class of men who
wage war on oppression without a reasoned plan
of aotion. Trenwith himself does not suffer this
blame, but the League does. The proposed Liberal
Association would fight for government by the
people for the people. The League fights for
government by the “ working man ” for the
“ working man.” This distinction may be a little
too broad, hut it indicates the difference. This is
not written in any spirit of disrespect for the
“ working man ”as popularly defined —he has his
place and value in the democracy.—Fluffy.

NOTICE.
By sending direct : to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note , or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing ivith Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that

SATURDAY, MAY 11, 1895.
Prisons and Criminals.
Herbert Spencer has spoken the last word
on the ethics of prison discipline. He has
laid it down that “ such portion of a crimi-
nal’s right is only justly taken away as can-
not be left to him without danger to the
community.” This implies that the term
“ penology ” is a misnomer, and that any
punishment attendant upon the treatment of
offenders should be purely accidental. Pro-
tection is the end aimed at by society :
punishment follows or not according to the
disposition of the offender and the nature of
the means taken to achieve the end. Further,
it implies that the better the means employed
towards the end the less punitive will be
their effect. It is a question of achieving a
maximum of cure with a minimum of pain.
# # •
Australasian prison systems are concerned
neither with science nor with ethics. They
are plants with their roots in convictism and
their branches in the fog of penal tradition.
They suck in the sap of barbarism and draw
in the blight of miscalled civilisation. Every
province has been content to stick to the old
order of things, grafting on occasionally an
unsuitable sprig of something new. There
has never yet been a prison administrator in
Australasia who put his heart into his work
with enthusiasm, and his head into it with
scientific method. Each has been content
to take up his task like any ordinary civil
servant, and perform it in mechanical red-
tape fashion. Now and again a Commission
has sat and digested several British Blue-
books, embodying the result in a more or
less satisfactory report. Sometimes the
recommendations have been adopted ; some-
times not. Often they have been partially
carried out, and consequently botched. Any-
how, it doesn’t matter, because uncompre-
hending imitations of English or any other
methods are useless. There is no prison
system in the world that can be exactly
copied with advantage. What Australasia
needs to do is to eclectically construct her
own system, and set an example to the world
as she has done with the ballot-box, woman’s
suffrage, the larrikin and the fighting kan-
garoo.
# # «
In Australasia, as elsewhere, the Judges
create difficulties at the very outset by in-
congruous sentences ; and, so long as periods
of imprisonment are left under the influence
of judicial fads, fancies, and prejudices, the
difficulties will never grow less. This subject
is stale, yet as important as ever. A single
recent example will serve to show the
ludicrous irrationality of the system. Two
men were arraigned before different Judges
for perjury. In passing sentence on the first
the ermined dignitary solemnly declared,
“ This is one of tiie worst cases of perjury I
have ever tried. Your sentence will be 12
months.” The other was hapless enough to
fall into the cruel clutches of Mercy. To him
the presiding Solomon said : “ I fully concur
in the verdict of the jury, but, as there seem
to be some extenuating points in your case, I
shall only sentence you to two years’ im-
prisonment.” Each Judge may be deemed
right according to his own views. But that
such a farce can be daily played shows that
the crude principles of our criminal laws are
hopelessly wrong.
• * #
When the victim of the law enters a
“ first-class ” Australasian prison the sterile
farce is continued, tie is usually first sub-
jected to a course of separate and silent
treatment ; he is known only by a number,
and when he marches out to exercise in the
radiating yard he wears a canvas mask. He
works only in his cell, picking oakum or
plaiting straw. His food is plain and scanty.
He is only visited by the parson, who fires
into him periodical doses of premeditated
piety. This continues for three months at
least, and nine at most, according to the length
of sentence. It is fatuously intended to
achieve various objects. First, it is sup-
posed to encourage remorseful meditation ;
secondly, to impress the victim with all the
horrors of prison life ; and, thirdly, to pre-
vent old offenders from mixing at once with
old-time companions and giving them a
budget of news fresh fiom the world ; while
there are many more alleged benefits con-
ceived with equal guilelessness.
* • *
At the end of his edifying seclusion the
prisoner is passed on to a gang. His num-
ber is dropped and his name disclosed ; the
rest of his history comes out by degrees. No
matter what his offence, venial or cardinal ;
no matter what his career ; he is usually
associated with the worst of the province’s
criminals. He may be only a first offender,
and a mild one at that; but in Victoria, for
example, it is all a matter of chance whether
he is sent to a first offenders’ prison or to a
place like Pentridge. The chances are
against him, for no Australasian province has
sufficient gaol accommodation to keep the
first offenders apart. All that the p isoner
has to do at this secondary stage is to keep
straight before the eyes of the officials, so
that he will get his full complement of
marks, and the consequent remission in sen-
tence. That ensured, he can traffic ; frater-
nise with the heroes of the establishment;
indulge in nameless immoralities ; do, in
short, whatever he is tempted and inclined
to do. The authorities are utterly indif-
ferent. All they expect is that their charges
shall conform to the prison rules. Official
concern begins and ends with the prisoner’s
good behaviour in gaol. It is callous as to
his future career when out, or as to his
prospects of coming back. Up to the last
hour, the prisoner is associated with his com-
panions in bond, so that, if inclined, he can
carry out all kinds of messages to free
friends. Hardly a prisoner liberated from
an Australasian gaol fails to bear some mes-
sage or messages of this kind. Thus is kept
compact and complete the confraternity of
crime.
* # #
Very often, if offenders were liberated at
the end of their preliminary separate and
silent dose, they would re enter the world
not worse, even if not absolutely better men.
The subsequent association, soul-petrifying
as it is, spoils all. This is not meant to be
an argument for solitary as against associated
treatment. It means simply this : that
where, as at Pentridge and other large Aus-
tralasian prisons, a short course of separate
is followed by a long course of associated
treatment without classification, little, except
evil, can result. The process is simply one
of developing into matured criminals the
large number of moral weaklings who
undergo it. The authorities will say, of
course, that any other system is “im-
possible.” “Impossible” is the “blessed
word ” of officialdom ; and, to the official
mind, it merely signifies that a suggested
reform is not in accordance with traditional
routine. If the reform is “visionary” as
well—that is, if its goal is admittedly one not
easy to reach in the present imperfect state
of human appliances, then its official
epitaph is finally written. The atti-
tude is as stupid as most official atti-
tudes are. It is admitted, of course, that
however high our ideals we must be limited
by wliat is expedient—our utmost ethical
obligation being that we shall make the ex-
pedient conform as far as possible to the
equitable. But the Australasian penal
authorities have probably, like the Austra-
lasian public, never given the equity of their
business a thought. Their adoration of the
expedient wholly excludes the ethical. They
are hopelessly possessed with the fallacy that
imprisonment is merely a process of punish-
ment and revenge.
Without a disturbance of the present
slumbrous system greater than that involved
in a re-arrangement ..of its Bed-clothes, an
effective reform could be achieved. Every-
body is agreed—intelligent criminals and all
{vide criminal literature from “ Vaux’s
Memoirs ” onwards) as to the evils of in-
discriminate association. These would be
considerably diminished if prisoners were
subjected to separate treatment at the end as
as at the beginning of their sentences. Were
they suddenly withdrawn from the gangs
without notice, the eggs of many plots would
be addled in the hatching. Michael Da vitt,
for different reasons, recently suggested such
an alteration in English prison discipline.
A few years ago Victorian authorities con-
demned the proposal as impracticable, mainly
because it had not been adopted in England.
# # •
Again, the Court, higher and lower, is too
often the ante-chamber to the sraol. Re-'
vised statutes are the only remedy. At
present there is in our laws too much of the
phrase “at the discretion of the Court.” It
would not matter so much if the Court
displayed even the average amount of dis-
cretion ; but it too often exhibits none what-
ever. The classification and sentencing of
offenders is too great a tax on the discretion
and liver of any individual or institution.
The laws conferring the powers are archaic ;
and the time for careful and scientific
revision by Parliament is over-due. The
weak and friendless too easily become classed
as criminals : the influential scoundrel has
too many opportunities to escape scot-free.
• # #
The jurisdiction of the lower (magisterial)
court should be more sharply defined. It
should have no power to imprison, still less
to flog, but only to summarily dispose of all
venial offenders by fine, or other means yet
to be mentioned. These offenders should be
classified by scientifically-inspired and care-
fully-constructed law, not only according to
offences and social positions as now, but also
according to age and the number of previous
convictions. A certain number of previous
convictions would, upon a fresh prima-facie
case being made out, pass the offender out-
side the jurisdiction of the lower and into
that of the higher (judicial) or Criminal
Court.
* * *
The prison should be solely a place for
criminals, and no tribunal except the higher
court should be allowed to wield the grave
power of declaring a citizen to be a criminal
within the meaning of the law. The pro-
vince of the Judge should be confined
strictly to seeing fair play and to applying
the verdict of the jury. Every safeguard
should be erected against justice-by-Jiver-
complaint. Therefore, the Judge should not
have the power to determine sentences, but
only authority to simply commit the offender
to a prison.
• * #
The period of a prisoner’s detention in
prison should be almost as indeterminate as
that of a patient in a lunatic-asylum. It
would require to be regulated by a minimum
to be served before release or probation
became allowable, and a maximum beyond
which the prisoner could not be detained
without further authority obtainable from
the court by the prison department, if justi-
fied. The principles upon which sentences
should be determined would obviously require
exhaustive deliberation before being em-
bodied in law.
* * *
The gradual consequence of preliminary
classification of offenders by the law would be
that the community would soon have its actual
criminal class reduced to correct and compact
proportions. As adoption of the indeter-
minate sentence would mean that a greater
number would be detained for long periods,
it would be more than ever necessary that
prison discipline should be relaxed. Ihe air
of dismalness in which the prison is shrouded
must be dissipated : cells should be less like
tombs; and social recreation should not be so
diligently suppressed. It is not necessary—
nor, indeed, is it just—that prisoners should
be hideously attired, or be miserably and
monotonously fed. Also, there is no decent
reason why the pipe or harmless games such
as draughts, chess, dominoes, etc., should be
forbidden. >
•' • «
Meanwhile, venial and infrequent minor
offenders would never enter prison at all.
Their offences never justify such a drastic
and complete restriction of their rights.
There are other means (alreadyin operation in
some shape elsewhere) of keeping them under
sufficient supervision. Among such means
are : (1) The probation system, coupled with
a scheme of restitution, whereby the petty
larcenist, for example, might retain his
freedom under supervision, and expiate his
offence by restoring the amount stolen with
a small sum as a fine. The supervision
would be best entrusted to special confi-
dential officers unconnected with the police.
(2). A system of military discipline, under
which all human waifage and wreckage not
probationable could be furbished up and
drilled into an appreciation of clean, decent,
industrious and regular life. Under this
scheme the Melbourne, or any other city
prison, would become an open barracks, the
occupants of which would be as restricted,
and, at the same time, as free as soldiers.
# * * '
With the worst of the venials grouped in
barracks and the criminals safe in gaol, the
troublesome question of employment would
remain to be settled. So far as the prisoners
are concerned, the problem would lie solved
by the reduction in their number by
classification. With the prison to keep
in order, and a little Government work
besides, there would seldom be any sur
plus labor' availab’e for fulfilment t) f
orders for civil servants’ boots, &c
As for the army of venial*, they would bo
engaged at drill and fatigue duty, the latter
to consist chiefly of useful public work, which
would not be done at all were it not done by
the employment of such labor. At first, the
cost of the new system here proposed would
be comnaratively great ; but gradually, when
the right principles were got into good goiuo-
order, the expenditure on prisons, police'’
&c., would probably fall much below that at
present incurred by any province. Anyhow
the immediate simplification of the terrible
tangle in which so-called Wine statistics are
tied, would in itself be worth some extra
expense.
* # *
The criminal has to be rescued from the
Pecksniffs of piety and pseudo-philanthropy;
and science is attempting the task. The
Italian school, led by Lombroso, has done
and is doing, much to give crime its proper
place in the student’s curriculum. Perhaps
the Italian method is carried too far. There
is too much measuring of brains ; too much
analysis of faces: altogether too much
picking to pieces of specimens. The
social failure is neither a victim to be
bailed up in a ■ cell and bombarded with
Bible-readings by the leisured snufflebuster ;
nor a peculiar kind of insect, to be dissected
and vivisected, measured and weighed, stuck
through the head with a pin, and generally
made up in a parcel and put under a micro-
scope by the scientist. He is to be trea’ed
justly by human methods, aided by the
highest dispassionate, yet sympathetic, in-
telligence. tie is to be restored to society
where possible, to be restrained by society
where necessary, but tortured by society not
at all.
# # #
So absolutely wrong are present prin-
ciples, so rotten is the administration of
them, that only revolutionary reconstruction
can set things right. Admittedly the crimi-
nal and social-failure problems are difficult;
but that is no reason why they should be, as
they have been, persistently shirked. Upon
their satisfactory solution must follow a
material diminution in crime and vice.
Society’s safety, as well as its conscience,
calls for a change.
The Gospel according to St. Jeamos.
It isn’t long since Paul Bourget remarked that
Americans gravelled for lack of occupation could
happily put in their spare time trying to
find out who their grandfathers were. A good
many Australians—on the evidence of “Burke’s
Colonial Gentry,” the second volume of which
has just been published—have been engaged
upon a similar problem, with such unexpected
success that the welcome discovery of the re-
quired relative appears to have sent them
flapping boldly up the ages upon the wings of
imagination—the general result being aptly
symbolised by the fact that these two volumes
of vain-glory are gilt-edged only upon top.
# * *
Searching the preface of this elegant work of
fiction for extenuating circumstances, The Bul-
letin respectfully notes that “many distin-
guished families at home have sent their younger
sons to win their spurs in the colonies. ” The
Bulletin remembers more than one “cadet,” at
all events, who was winning, with his spurs, the
modest remuneration of a stockrider; but it has
known a good many more who “added new
lustre to the family-name ” by boozing indus-
triously from remittance to remittance, and it
has more than once beheld the haughty scion of
a “ house ” entitled to “ supporters ’’ provided :
therewith in the shape of “ two Hibernian ,
salvages grasphaut, azure, boutonny argent,
helmeted sable.”
* • #
Humanity—or most of it—is a flunkey, when ■,
it isn’t a snob! Even Walter Scott could
make a fetish of a glass which had been used i
by that mangy rascal, George the Fourth..
But Scott, in the tradition of his education !
and environment, had an excuse which will not
fit the squalid rank-worship that fathers the :
eagerness of so many Australians to be deemed I
“the tenth transmitter of a foolish face.”’
There are, of course, men amongst us who can i
establish as undeniable a descent from those ■
robbers and murderers of the 11th century who ■
wore iron all over them as certain others of us ;
can from those of the 18th, who wore it only,
round their ankles. But it is to a third class—
loftily contemptuous of the second, and yet
feverishly anxious to identify itself with the
first—that the examiner of this Gospel of St..
Jeames will be chiefly indebted for an exposi-
tion of the genealogic feats that can be per-
formed with the aid of the four simple little 1
words “alleged,” “reputed,” “ presumed,” and!
“ supposed.”
* # •
In the matter of “arms,” however, the 1
compiler of this precious record has been
inconveniently careful to discriminate between,
“bearings legitimately used” and “those,
which, the editor regrets to say, are mere
assumptions from families with which no.
relationship can be proved.” Unholy trifles,
with the august blazonic mystery, however,,
may soothe their aspiring souls with the reflec-
tion that amongst the arrnorially orthodox are.
such nouveaux arrives as “ Cooper, of Wool-
lahra,” “ Wilson, of Ercildoune,” “ Clarke,.
of Rupertswood.” The would-be feudal twang,
of which style of nomenclature 1 , by the way,,
turns the laugh badly against itself when, the.
mere fancy-name ofa'house is made vaguely to.
masquerade as a territorial designation..
“Sargood, of Rippon-Lea !” “ Ueakin,. of.
Llanarth !” As who should say “ Egerton,.
of Tacton,” or “Stanley, of Alderley.”'
Most notable amongst colonial “coats” is the 1
modest quartering of the arms of France, Eng-


land, Scotland and Ireland, with which one
lofty-souled individual commemorates the trans-
cendent privilege of descent from that bastard
son of Charles 11. to whom Thurlow referred
when he stigmatised another descendant—the
Duke of Grafton— as “the accident of an
accident.” Not that in bastardy itself inheres
the disgrace, but in the slavish eagerness with
which a circumstance which would otherwise
be anxiously concealed is advertised to the
world when it happens to make connection with
a crown —even when worn by such a des-
picable animal as George IV. —to whom a con-
tributor to the corrigenda in one of the volumes
appears nakedly anxious to refer the illegitimate
parentage of his own mother ! Surely, surely ,
someone must have been hoaxing Burke ! Said
the late Professor Jowett to a tuft-hunting
student —“ This miserable rank-worship is the
social leprosy of England ! Keep clean, young
man ! keep clean ! ” Let every Australian
paste the words in his hat, and look forward to
that not impossible time when some conscience-
striken culprit, charged with wilfully and mali-
ciously making use of a title, shall be heard to
murmur from the dock—“Oh, my offence is
rank 1 it smells to Heaven ! ”
# • *
For the present, however, the number of
hitherto unsuspected persons who—according
to their own confession—are “ entitled to write
themselves armigeri in any bill, quittance,
warrant or obligation,” is most democratically
.discouraging, though, on the other hand, many
, reasonably suspected individuals turn out not
.even to have committed themselves as far as a
trumpery little crest. The New South Wales
(Chief Justice, for instance, who goes back only
five generations, makes no pretence to armorial
.distinction any more than does Judge Innes,
who modestly contents himself with two re-
imoves. And Chief Justice Madden, of Vic-
itoria, also seeks to question antiquity no further
.than a grandfather.
# * #
The ancestral gorgeousness of the Australian
(Bench, however, is well propped-up by Noel,
Foster, Molesworth and Holroyd— Judge
(Noel being a “ certified Gainsborough,” and
.Judge Foster reaching boldly out for Edward
Hl. apparently upon much such evidence as
<would also establish his relationship with
Ptolemy Psammetichos or Timour the Tartar.
’The Molesworths, however, go two better,
-and fall exhausted, after six pages of “line-
rage,” upon the bosom of Edward 1., while the
•“ cake ” for pedigree goes to an otherwise un-
iimportant Victorian person, who, with nine
pages of evolution, achieves the proud dis-
tinction of getting nearly level with Carbine.
The antidote to which style of thing is supplied
by Railway-Commissioner Eddy, who —by
being, like the doughty Marshal Junot, “his
own ancestor”—occupies by far the most dig-
nified position in the x’ecord— unless, as in
certain other cases, the lineal exposition is
perforce withheld, “pending reconstruction.”
* # *
Little Dr. Renwick, as a matter of course,
staggers under a mountain of ancestral magnifi-
cence, and the Sydney medicine-men —as wit-
ness their weakness for double-barrelled names
—put on a genealogic “frill” which is badly
discounted by Dr. “ Plevna ” Ryan’s record of
what he did “per se,” and not “per proyealtores
■ stios.” The ecclesiastical cranium, also, is
, quite large enough for its reverent hat, as re-
gards conceit of extraction, though the most
(Unimpeachably blue-blooded cleric in Australia
.tootles much more mildly on his ancestral
(trumpet than the episcopal person who is
; gravely set forth as belonging to “ the ancient
; and distinguished house of Smith” —a statement
v.which almost induces The Bulletin to
:suspect the hierophant of this fantastic
<cult of poking occasionally at the vota-
ries thereof a solemn and respectable kind
«)f “ borak,” as when, for another instance, he
dumps down the genealogically-naked “0. J.
Roberts, Esquire,” right in front of a six-page
procession of Egerton-Warburtons. There’s
a flavor of cold joke, too, about the insinuation,
per italics, that a certain efflorescent journalist
has very possibly no right to the arms he arro-
gates —even after that distinguished person has
been allowed to expand his chest about the pos-
session of a family whisky-mug, reputed to have
assisted at Caledonian coronations. The
Bulletin, as far as that goes, gives itself no
airs over the ownership of a beaker which can't
be proved not to be the veritable article out of
which the late Mr. Noah drank, not wisely,
but too well.
* * *
In connection with which prototypical vig-
uieron and boozer it is to be noted that the
wine-growing De Castellas, with a porten-
.tous list of forbears celebrated chiefly for being
knocked on the head, make only a bad second
ito a whilom Victorian beer-builder, who—
ihaving meandered through 30 generations from
the brass-bound marauder who played “ second
ruffian ” to Strongbow— links thus happily
together the aristocracies of pillage and swill-
age. Yet, what is such poor, fag-end twinkling
of distinction to the brand-new effulgence with
which “ Hereditary-Roman-Marquis-Sir Pat
Jennings ” outshines a batch of mere guberna-
torial earls, about whom the only thing
blazonically remarkable is that John Adrian
Louis, of Hopetouu, is “ supported ” by “ two
females in loose garments, with dishevelled
hair,” for whom it is apparently considered
necessary to vouch promptly as “ all proper ?”
* * *
But enough ! Too much fustian, like too
much arsenic, produces salutary emesis, and
The Bulletin, as it joyously closes this dismal
evangel of cockadoodle-ntni-grovel, wants to
go forth, like Diogenes, and look for a Man !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Alleged Australian Squadron.
Australian Parliaments, over-persuaded by
Jingo-bribed and Jingo-bewitched politician s,
vote £126,000 a year for the up-keep of the
“Australian” Defence Squadron, over which
they have no control whatever. The Australian
people pays for the gold lace of every officer, the
blue jacket of every seaman, and has not the
smallest voice in appointing or governing the
servants whose wages come straight out of its
pocket. The squadron is nominally for the
defence of Australia, and we have the pleasant
assurance that the Admiral (whose screw we
have to find) doesn’t care a brass button for the
wishes of his employers, and can steam away to
“ defend Australia ” in the Red Sea or at Vladi-
vostok the moment it suits his real masters at
the British Admiralty (who pay not a cent
of his wages). The bargain, apart from
its infamous unpatriotism, is the most irra-
tional Australia ever entered into. What
Bulletin reader, in his individual capa-
city, would dream . of paying men for some-
body else to boss about, without him or his
representatives having the right to lift a finger
in approval or remonstrance ? But the congre-
gation of Bulletin readers, in their capacity of
Australian taxpayers, must perforce sit still
and see their money spent at the nod of a clerk
in an office at the other end of the earth.
The most glaring irregularities, the utmost
brutality, the silliest tomfoolery, on the part of
the naval gentry who get their living out of
Australia, find Australia helpless to protest.
So, when the Mildura stokers were all but
roasted alive in their floating purgatory, we
could not stir to protect them. When a man
walking an Australian street is pounced on,
and shipped off to England, away from his Aus-
tralian wife and children, no Australian court
dare interfere. The man may be charged with
desertion : is he, or is he not, a deserter? No-
body knows. It may have been years since his
alleged desertion took place ; identification is
uncertain ; but pleading is useless. Habeas
corpus is a myth; liberty of the subject a fable:
the man is transported then and there without
the vestige of adequate trial. And Australia
suffers that ! She also suffers mockeries like
the recent Ringarooma court-martial in Sydney,
when a captain and a doctor—both in receipt of
Australian salaries—quarrelled as to their
respective rights and duties under the
naval regulations ; and a jury of captains
—some of them also paid by Australia—
defended their comrade, censured the doctor’s
chief witness, excluded the doctor’s most
material evidence, and finally dismissed him
with contumely from the service, after an inves-
tigation in which bias and iucompetency stuck
out 10Q yards. Naturally the captains, how-
ever well-intentioned they may be, had a bias in
favor of their pal. No doubt a jury of doctors
would have sided with their pal. But what is
wanted is not partisanship, but justice. The
doctor put the captain on the sick-list, as he is
empowered to do at any time he sees fit. The
captain refused to go on the sick-list, and
ordered him to go under arrest. The
doctor refused to go under arrest, and
the court cashiered him for it. Not a
word was said about the captain’s refusal
to go on the sick-list, an almost equally grave
offence under the regulations ; and the court
actually refused to permit the doctor to bring
evidence to justify his action. Thus, to take
an imaginary case, if a naval warrior suddenly
goes off his head, and the ship’s doctor, doing
his duty under the regulations, puts that
warrior on the sick-list, he will be quite
warranted in refusing to go, and may play
old Harry with his ship in perfect con-
fidence that an appreciative jury of
brother officers will hold him guiltless.
As to the real merits of the Ringarooma
dispute The Bulletin knows little, seeing that
only half the evidence is before it. But it
knows that the alleged trial of an “Australian”
officer on an “ Australian ” ship was a mere
burlesque of justice, and that the alleged Aus-
tralian nation, having bound itself hand and
foot and delivered itself over to the enemy, can
only look on stupidly and do nothing. The
suspicion grows upon this paper that Australia
is an ass.
Exit N.S.W. Local Government Bill.
New South Wales Local Government Bill has
fallen with the same old thud into the same old
gutter; and Parkes is glad. He can’t get
the credit of passing it; but, at all events,
there is every probability that nobody else
will. That Local Government Bill has
been about to pass ever since Parkes
was a raw Parliamentary recruit, and Parkes
(as he was careful to inform a reporter, the
other day) has been saving the country for 41
years now. Every prominent politician has
tried to pass it, or has boasted that lie tried to
pass it; but the Bill beat the lot of them. It
bested Parkes; it bested Dibbs ; it bested
half-a-score of others ; and now it has bested
Reid. The Bulletin cheerfully anticipates
that the Bill will see every member
of the present generation of politicians
safely in the land of shadows before it ever
dreams of leaving the land of shadows itself.
Its most recent opponent, Premier Reid, is not,
however, disgraced by his defeat. “ The gods
themselves fight in vain against stupidity,”
says Schiller ; and it is chiefly to the stupid
obstinacy of the Labor-party that the wreck of
New South Wales Local Government Bill is
due. They insisted on applying the One-man-
one-vote principle to municipal suffrage. This
paper is not prepared to say it should not be
applied to municipal suffrage, but the point
seems doubtful. r l he association of citizens to
govern a community rests on a different basis
from the association of ratepayers to govern a
municipality. The community is given autho-
rity over the lives and liberties of its members,
anil one man’s life and liberty arc as good
as those of another, hence all have an
equal right, and should have equal powei,
to decide how life and liberty shall be
dealt with by the community. These are
“stakes in the country” which outweigh all
others. The possession of property can no
more be put in the scale against them than a
leaf can be put against a forest. One-man-one-
vote for representation in Parliament is no less
just than expedient. Hut the municipal council
exercises not a direct but a delegated authority.
It governs a restricted area, fixed by Parlia-
ment, to a restricted extent, also fixed by Par-
liament. Its chief concern is, subject always
to tl>3 oversight of Parliament, to promote the
local welfare of those who reside within
the limits of its authority. It expends
funds derived from local contributions,
and the contributors naturally expect, and
generally obtain, control of the expendi-
ture in proportion to the extent of their
contributions. And this seems reasonable.
The ratepayers’ lives and liberties are protected
by the State, and, as equal voters under the
State, they have an equal voice in deciding how
far the municipality shall deal with their con-
venience. But the State does not undertake
to secure their convenience, and surely the
measure of their contribution should be the
measure of their right to influence the way in
which the contributions of all the ratepayers
are expended to promote the general conveni-
ence. If the man who pays ss. in rates should
have, on the ground of his equal manhood, the
same voice in controlling expenditure as the
man who pays £5, then the man who pays
nothing is as much entitled to a voice as either.
Endowment by Government does not complicate
the question. The large ratepayer is just as
much entitled to spend Government endowment
as the small one, and the non-ratepayer as
much as either—that is to say, none
of them has any logical claim to
spend it. The State which endows municipal
councils should have direct representation
on municipal councils. But whether the Labor-
party was right or wrong on the point of prin-
ciple. it was manifestly wrong on the ground of
policy. Half a Local Government Act is better
than no bread ; and it was a matter of cer-
tainty that the Leg. Council would not grant
the whole loaf. The Labor-party pushed its
obstinacy to the climax of a division ; some
Government supporters were treacherous; many
Opposition members deliberately and disgrace-
fully voted against their professed convictions
in order to weaken the Govt, and wreck the
bill; and the Govt, was defeated. Naturally,
it dropped the Bill ; there was no other course
open consistent with self-respect. And, as
before said, The Bulletin stands ready to
welcome Local Government in New South
Wales somewhere about 1955.
The “ Meejum of Exchange.”
A Maoriland paper succeeded the other day
in casting a new light on the old, tired problem
of bi-metallism. The purpose of bi-metallism,
as a good many people know, and a much
greater number, probably, do not, is to make
silver coin legal tender, in the same way as
gold, to any extent, so that the man who owes
£lOOO shall be able to pay it with 1000
sovereigns or 20,000 shillings as suits him best.
Bi-metallists claim that there is not enough of
gold to serve for purposes of buying and sell-
ing, and that owing to its scarcity the price of
gold has gone up, and it takes twice as much
tea or wool to buy a sovereign as it used to do ;
in other words, that a gold standard has
reduced prices and is making the world stone-
broke. Mono-metallists claim that prices have
gone down because more railways, more and
faster ships, and better machinery have made
production easier, and have increased supplies ;
and point out that most of the world’s gold coin
lies idle in banks while business is conducted by
means of cheques and notes; that there is
nearly twice as much silver in use as
there was in the bi-metallic days, much
more gold and paper, and about 60 per
cent, more currency altogether. Also, they
demand aggressively why the world wants
more coin, either gold or silver, when it doesn’t
use half the coin it already possesses. As no
one can get together enough reliable statistics
to prove the case on either side the combatants
claw at each other to very little real purpose.
Most silver-producing countries want bi-
metallism because it will raise the price of
silver and make things boom in their particular
line of business, and most countries which
don’t produce silver view the subject with
gloomy indifference. The combatants on either
side are dreadfully in earnest, and the subject
itself remains vei-y much where it was. Maori-
land, as a land which produces little silver, takes
little interest in the subject. But the island
province is blessed with vast deposits of copper,
and one Maoriland editor raises the very
reasonable question why not have tri-
metallism instead of bi-metallism, and make
copper also legal tender to any extent?
If this were done, he points out, copper
would go up enormously, and Maoriland
would grow so rich that she could, in
a few years, pay off her public debt of
£39,000,000 by shipping to England in bulk
9,360,000,000 coppers. And The Bulletin, after
much serious consideration, doesn’t see any
reason why tri-metallism shouldn’t be as good
as bi-metallism, and it isn’t quite sure that
mono-metallism isn’t as good, or as bad, as
either of them, and it is still trying, without
much success, to arrive at a definite opinion as
to the merits of the w'hole conflict. Of one
thing, however, it is certain —that the introduc-
tion of an iron coinage, which would also be
legal tender to any amount, would help along a
hitherto neglected Australian industry, and
that an iron coin weighing 2cwt. and valued at
20s. would be an inspiriting spectacle. The
“meejum of exchange” is a subject which grows
more perplexing the more it is considered.
Inter-Ausbraliaa Reciprocity.
Editor Bulletin,— On a former occasion I sub-
mitted a plan for a measure of inter-provincial
Freetrade—it provided that all tariff-identities
should be taken advantage of without further
parley. Numerous instances of present agree-
ment were pointed out, and it was also sug-
gested that every tariff revision in Australia
should be carried out with an eye to assimila-
tion with one or more of the other provinces.
A draft report of the Vic. Tariff Board has
been published, and I submit notes thereon
from the reciprocity standpoint:
Agricultural Implements.—The proposed reduction on
taxed implements from 20 per cent, to 15 per cent, ad
ral. will brin/ Vic. into line with S.A., and is, therefore,
to be commended. N.S.W. charges 10 per cent. Biscuits
are at present taxed 2d. in every province. The board
proposes to diversity this agreement by altering the Vic.
dutytoljd. . ~
Candles.—The proposed reduction from 2d. to Id. per
lb. makes a complication. At present N.S.VV. (with IJd.)
is the only exception to the 2d. rate, and the proposed
Vic. Id. rate, therefore, seems unfortunate.
Chemicals.—Certain medical poisons are put on the free
list, presumably for the benefit of anti-federalists
Fruits.—Green fruits, which came into Vic. free for
years, are to be charged 6d. per bushel. This is a tax
exclusively on iuterprovincial trade, and is to be con-
demn'd.
Hats.—Tlie Australian duties on hats are now almost
as tangled as the timber tariffs ; but the board proposes
some brand-new varieties.
Mustard.—The duty on this in Q. and S.A. is 3d. —in
S.A. and Vic. it is 2d. The new proposal for Vic. is Id.
per lb. This is a considerable error in kind, though per-
haps not in degree.
Paper bags.—The proposed reduction will place the
duty somewhere about the average of N.S.W., Q., and
S.A., which is praiseworthy.
Rice.—The duty on rice is treated in an extraordinary
fashion. Hitherto there has been an advantage of 2s, per
cental to dress rice in bond ; this advantage is now to be
almost obliterated. The alteration destroys it as a pro-
tective duty—and as a revenue duty it should be swept
out of existence.
Soaps.—The reduction of Id. per lb. will make the Vic.
duty fall into federal line.
Starch.—The Vic. 2d. per lb. on starch agrees with Q. and
S.A. Id. per lb. would agree with N.S.W. and Tas. ; the
proposed IJd. per lb. agrees with no one.
Spirits—perfumed. —The free port to “spirits of wine
for perfumery ” made from Vie. distilled spirits is a first-
class idea—but it does not come into the federal question.
Saddlery.—The present Vic. duty is 45 per cent, ad val.
—a reduction of 10 per cent, is recommended. S.A. is
25 p.e. ;Q. is 15 p.c.; N.S.W. is 10 p.c. ; certainly Vic.
could not come down to the highest of these before
Federation.
“ Stereo-plates.—Duty to be charged as in N.S.W.” This
is proper talk. Every alteration should be made with an
eye to assimilation.
Timber.—Australian timber duties are the chaotic con-
sequences of independent action—their diversity is magni-
ficent, titanic. The Vic. Board proposes more wooden
differences.
Woollens.—Vic. could not possibly make a Federal
adjustment at present. The highest other (Q.) is 25 per
cent. The board recommends a reduction to 35 per cent
from 40 per cent.
It will be seen that no considerable amount of
attention has been given to adjusting the Vic.
tariff on an Australian basis. With several of
the important duties it would be impossible.
But I believe I have indicated other directions
in which the Vie. Tariff Board might have done
more towards the Federal tariff.— Fluffy.
Howard Willoughby (“Timotheus,” of the
Argus) wrote, some time since, an article —
“State Socialism: Its Coming Failure ” —and
it has been thought worth while to cable the
foregone conclusion that it pleases London
Times. Willoughby’s prime argument was
that State Socialism abolishes a desirable form
of repudiation. A joint-stock co. which built a
railway failure could go insolvent and the debt
would cease. Under the same circumstances a
State would have to continue payments on a
dead concern which ought to be buried. Thus
Willoughby. Nothing could be more worthy
of admiration than the cynical courage which
enables “Timotheus” to tell Australia at the
present time that joint-stock repudiation doesn’t
matter. The ’93 “Reconstruction” involved
£103,000,000 in “legitimate banks” —and
about £15,000,000 in illegitimate banks—and
Australia is still reeling from the wholesale
repudiation of that time, whereof Individualism,
not Socialism, stands accused. Socialism and
Individualism may both be on their trial; but
it is Individualism that is now in the dock.
Editor Bulletin, —I was surprised to-day at
finding the enclosed letter returned me by the
N.S.W. postal authorities. The letter, ad-
dressed to Mr. Geo. Adams, contained no money
and no application for shares in any illegal lot-
tery or sweep, but simply asked a question in re
the V.D.L. Bank Lottery, which is legalised by
Act of Parliament in Tasmania, where the
lottery is being held. It appears to me that in
refusing to deliver the letter in question the
Sydney authorities are, first, assuming a censor-
ship over the legislative acts of a neighbor-
ing province ; and, secondly, assuming a dicta-
torship over the citizens of their own region in
re the particular speculations in which they
may or may not invest their money outside
N.S. Wales. Surely the New South Wales
postal authorities must be, or imagine them-
selves to be, possessed of very highly-developed
moral and intellectual faculties when they
undertake so big a contract in a single line.
We may expect to hear that orders for Sun-
light Soap and Lightning Pills are being
returned from the G.P.O. to the senders, or
that applications for shares in Coolgardie mines
will not be delivered to the brokers in Sydney.
—F.M. April 27, 1895.
Pearson’s Weekly (London), March 16, revives
a venerable friend :
The characteristic climate of Australia and the pug-
nacity of the kangaroo make hunting the hides danger-
ous. Winter starts in May and ends in December in that
country. The rest of the year the heat is intense, the
thermometer frequently reaching 140 degrees.
Eight men hunt together for kangaroos. They are
culled a “see.” When brought to bay the kangaroo jumps
like a flash for a hunter’s breast, and tries to crush it in
with his fore feet. To prevent this each man wears across
his breast a square of matting.
Armed with a spear, with a club attached to the other
end, they ride upon swift horses into a herd. With the
agility and balance of circus-riders they stand erect upon
their liorses and use their spears and clubs.
The kangaroo is able to jump clean over a horse.
Our contemporary makes several serious mis-
takes. The hunting of the kangaroo was long
since stopped by the Government owing to the
loss of thousands of lives. In 1893, 6000 men were
killed in hunting the green kangaroo, which
chiefly depends on its poisonous breath for its
defence. This daring animal suffers from de-
cayed teeth, and the hunter who goes within a
mile of it when it is picking its teeth forfeits
his life. At one time, hunters habitually
carried a tooth - brush and a pot of tooth-
paste, on the principle that if they could get
near enough to clean its teeth there was some
chance of capture, but the theory failed in
practice. The blue kangaroo was hunted suc-
cessfully until recently, but it is now extinct,
except in the remote forests north of Torres
Straits ; and it is to be feared that its dis-
appearance is fraught with much danger to
Australia, as it was the only indigenous animal
able to wage successful war on the rabbits.

and expensive tradition; the offices will be

why not defy tradition, save money, and hasten
Federation by combining the offices wic’-Why
not? Because the billets are handy for vain

The success that Amykos has met with,
and the universal favour it enjoys both in the Old
and New Worlds, is a sufficient guarantee of the
advantages resulting from its use as a mouth-
wash, gargle, cosmetic, &c. It is unanimously
acknowledged to possess a mild and, at the same
time, energetic efficacy as an antiseptic water,
and is superior to all antiseptics at present known
to science, as expressed by Professor Sir Joseph
Lister, of E linBurgh, in the Lancet— which alone
ought to make Amykos a household word for
the Australian, who esteems cleanliness. Price
Is. 6d. per bottle at all chemists, perfumers, &c.

The reading-lessons in Australian schools are
seldom a credit to the authorities. The absurd
“ Royal Readers ” are typical handbooks of the
drivel of our fathers—with this further defect
that the drivel is diluted for the sake of the
young person. Victoria is so enamoured of
this sort of illiterature in its schools that it pro-
poses to buy certain copyrights in England to
perpetuate the same nefarious assaults on inno-
cent scholars. The schoolbooks of Australia
might advantageously be made a matter for in-
terprovincial action. That each province should
devise and maintain a separate series is waste-
ful and ridiculous excess. Concerted action
between the several departments should result
in a creditable standard of lessons being
reached, and thenceforward the production of
the books could be proceeded with economic-
ally, and with good literary results. But the
miserable jealousy of the departmental authori-
ties is the lion in the path.
The prosecution of the English Jockey Club, for
keeping enclosures on Newmarket Heath to be
used as gaming-places, was doomed to failure. In
the first place, the Anti-Gambling League had
to take proceedings on the spot, and an appeal
to a Newmarket Bench for the conviction of
Jockey Club stewards couldn’t expect to suc-
ceed. Also, the Bench was packed, anyway.
But if the Bench had consisted merely of the
parson who acted as chairman and the other
reverend gentleman who assisted his spiritual
brother’s search for the terevvth,.it would have
been all the same to the A.G. League. Parsons
in that district subsist on horse-racing. Jockeys,
trainers, owners and betting folk of various
descriptions are the Church’s only supporters —
at Newmarket. After the Bench had con-
sidered the case for an hour in private the pre-
siding vicar delivered a characteristic ver-
dict. They couldn’t say the betting rings
were not common gaming places, but they
said that they (the Bench) didn’t feel satisfied
that the Jockey Club “ permitted the enclo-
sures to be used for the purposes of betting
within the meaning of the Act of 1853.” A
further prosecution of bookmakers for betting
in a public place was to have followed, but
the Bench prudently resolved to fix a day
“later on” for that tough job. A crowd of
horsey individuals, mostly with straw in their
mouths, gave three cheers for the local law,
and there the matter ended for a time. Only
for a time. What The Bulletin said before
this farcical event was said in the Referee, by
G. R, Sims, afterwards—“ Thanks to the action
of the Anti-Gambling League, we are within
measurable distance of a thorough reform of
the laws relating to betting, and gambling and
turf speculation generally.”

My Dear Mookabinda—
A paragraph published in this column on
March 30 appears to have been taken by some
people as applying to, and consequently reflecting
on, the character of Mr. Chas. Seymour Allen
(inventor of the submarine torpedo boat of which
so successful a trial was recently made before the
Admiral of the Australian station); and from
enquiries I have since made it appears there
was some show of reason for their misappre-
hension. A misapprehension it undoubtedly was ;
for nothing was further from my thoughts
than that either the paragraph or the material
upon which it was founded could be taken as re-
ferring in any way to anybody in Australia-let
alone to Mr. Seymour Allen, of whom I had never
heard anything that was not entirely favorable,
and with whom I much sympathise in his en-
deavors to trace the authors of certain utterly un-
founded and malicious statements which he
warmly resents.
An entirely new and original horror invests the
complicated position of station-overseer inN.S.W.
The occupier thereof (if a bachelor) generally falls
a victim to the charms of the governess (who is
always single), and is actually sometimes called
upon to engage her ! Imagine a bronzed and
bearded youth, just down from the edge of the
horizon, having to run the blockade of the gover-
ness-agencies—catechising beauty in search of a
job after the manner of Peter Amos Dunn in
“Niobe” (with very similar results, I should
think), and travelling back with his choice and
all her luggage !
And yet that is the burden some station-holders
bind upon the souls of their helpless he-hirelings.
One squatter, whose “ supers ” invariably marry
the governesses and leave his employ, always sends
the new men to Sydney, with instructions to look
out a new governess for Mrs. Saddlebags, The
last overseer who underwent what might be called
the ordeal by governess - agency, expressed his
surprise to a friend (named Bill, I expect) that he
should have been called upon to perform such an
unusual task. “Oh,” said Bill, “ old Saddlebags
always does that. His last three supers married a
governess apiece, so he thinks it only fair to the
newcomer to give him a chance to suit himself!
Anyway, after a man has beguiled a girl
on to a back-block run he feels, in a measure,
responsible for her, and pays her more attention
than he otherwise would. The result is, they both
stop longer on the place.” Quite like a glimpse of
Arcadia, isn’t it ? But if this untrammelled state
of affairs grows into a vogue, I should advise all
instructresses-of-youth on the shady side of 40 to
take in their signboards and retire from business.
A woman wrote to a Sydney paper recently
deploring the absence of an Australian substitute
for the London Harley-street institute which
Walter Besant sodrearily describes in “ Katharine
Regina.” As tilings are, country people certainly
buy a pig in a poke when they engage a governess
in the happy-go-lucky way peculiar to Australia—
through registry-offices or in answer to advts. —so
nobody is surprised when high-priced finishing
governesses turn up spelling pumpkins without
the intermediary “p ” and translating “ Jones v.
Smith” as “Jones via Smith.” There is also a
great deal to be said about the present careless
system from the governesses’ point of view. Many
girls reply to a “squatter’s” advt., to find that
the “squattage” is a bush hotel or something
worse, whence no escape is possible until they earn
enough money to pay their passage back to
civilisation. 1 think that a governesses’ home on
a scientific basis really is a long-felt want. An
attempt was made three or four years ago to set
such an institution moving, but it fell through.
Perhaps the time is ripe for another attempt. So
many “leading” parents have seen tlrir daughters
forced to take up teaching as a means of bread-
winning that the movement would probably obtain
much more cordial support nowadays.
Vice-regal governesses (who are, in Australia,
at least, the little tin-goddesses of their calling, I
supoose) don’t seem to be very lucky folks as far
as matrimony goes. One of them certainly mar-
ried a well-placed swain, and was given away by
her vice-regal employer, with her ladyship much
moved in the front pew; and I suppose she creates
a rosy and glorious precedent most encouraging to
the others. But it must not be forgotten that she
is the exception that proves the rule. I remember
one gouvernante whose slender figure, vivacity,
pearly complexion, and absolute bravery in sport-
ing the same frock times out of number ought to
have rendered her attractive to men, but she re-
turned a spinster as in the first chapter. E located
women who think that attachment to a kawlonial
Governor’s sweet is a passport to the heart of the
Australian eligible, are advised to look up the
ordinary fate of their predecessoresses before sign-
ing articles, as the sailors say.
What an operatic patchwork we’ve been having
lately at Sydney Lyceum ! “ The Mountebanks ”
will be raging when these lines appear, but, all
the same, I want to congratulate the firm on a
most radiant revival of “ The Mikado ” last Satur-
day. Tapley topped the record as Nanki-Poo,
althoug i lie dodged the high-ascending notes in
“ Here’s a how-de-do ” (the “ with a passion that’s
intense I worship and adore” part of it), which
ordinarily give such a skylarkv effect to the trio ;
but Mr. Vi. Varley’s legs, clothed in salmon-pink
fleshings, were most edifying. Altogether, I con-
sider Nanki-Poo one of “ the gentlemanly tenor’s”
very best parts. Nellie S., as Yum-Yum (one of
her pet roles), was not quite so lovely to look upon
as usual—for one thing, the exigencies of Japanese
art necessitate a pair of tomato-red cheeks which
look as if the lady had been frost-bitten, but
otherwise I found no flaws. Yum-Yum must be
a very easy part as far as dressing goes. The
creamy, pink-flowered silken wrapper, edged with
yellow bands, and the big yellow pack-saddle,
were worn bang through the operetta. Marriage
doesn’t much agitate the wardrobe of a Mdlle.
Chrysanthibme, apparently; though Nanki-Poo
broke out into a violent toga, like an ecclesiastical
window-ourtam, when he was going to be married,
Ida Osborne was Pitti-Sing, with her front hair
trimmed in a manner that suggested curl-papers,
at a little distance; and Florence Young played
Katisha, so the playbill said, but she was so
hideously got up that I don’t believe I would have
recognised her a bit—at any rate, not until she
began to talk about showing her left elbow on
Tuesdisand Fridis. The melodious Miss Young
has a fatal habit of clipping her words that would
enable one to discover her even under burnt-cork,
I think the chorus ladies always look very fetch-
mg as part of the Japanese crowd. And very
convincing, too, until you get a broadside view of
their feet.

Wallace Brownlow made an excellent Mika, and
was in mellow voice; but still (I venture to speak
as the mouth-piece of d.c. damseldom) it did seem
a pity that this popular youth should come forth
with his twinkling shanks bundled up in black and
gold millinery, and his fatal countenance to match
—it seemed such a waste of good material, don’t
you know. Apropos ugliness—Vernon looked
absolutely hideous as Ko-Ko lie was as ugly as a
Chinese paper-knife; but yet he has the impudence
when ordered to marry Katisha to talk in an
aggrieved manner about a “caricature of a face.”
But it’s quite true to nature, all the same. The
only thing that would make me work for sex-
equality would be the hope that, by so doing, we
might gain the invaluable masculine prerogative
of being as ugly as possible ourselves, and yet
privileged to talk scornfully about the absence of
facial attraction in somebody of the opposite sex.
Talking of “The Mikado” somehow reminds
me that Dr. and Mrs. On Lee gave a very enjoy-
able “ at home ” at Wynyard-square last Friday,
at which the members of the Esty-Marsh Concert
Co. turned up, and were, of course, the lions of
the evening. Mrs. Marsh (Miss Alice Esty) wore
a gown of heliotrope brocade with fuzzy bodice of
black chiffon, diamond brooches and a big bouquet
completing a successful turn-out; and Madame
Enriquez (who is rather too meditative a per-
sonality to be a drawing-room success) sported
pink and blue with crystal headings, and carried
two bouquets, spoils from that evening’s concert.
Mr. Tenor Cunningham, of the easy-going appear-
ance, turned up smiling as usual; and talkative
Herr Vollmar, with his aureole of ginger hair,
and Mr. Van Vleet, who certainly possesses the
smallest male mouth at present appearing in pub-
lic, were also on deck.
So, also, was “ the greatest baritone who has
ever appeared on the ’Stralian concert platform ”
—Mr. Alec. Marsh.. Mr. Marsh is loud in praises
of Our Organ, Our Harbor, and Our Town Hall,
even going so far as to find something pic-
turesquely appealing about the naval storbs on
Garden Island. „
“In Town,” my Melb. correspondent writes,
does not lend such scope for gowning as its pre-
decessor, “ A Gaiety Girl.” In the first act
simple walking-dresses—or the stage-land equiva-
lent for them—are worn, and in the second most
of the principals disclose their very charming
forms in tights. Maude Hobson, in her first-class
dress of celestial blue moirb antique with fixings
of black velvet and yellow lace, as usual makes
the girls round her look almost insipid, and in the
Romeo costume of black velvet with peaeh-satin-
lined cloak, she shows that her splendid figure
stands undressing quite as well as it does dressing.
Blanche Massey, whose little-head-running-over-
with-curls style of head-dress gives her a some-
what childish and forlorn appearance, wears an
innocent-looking white frock begrilled and be-
tucked in a way strongly reminiscent of an infant’s
long-clothes. With this she dons a bewitch-
ing black-plumed hat, tied under the chin with
broad pink satin ribbons, and balanced on the
head at an alarming angle. Grace Palotta’s first
dress reminded one of an agitated French hen,
covered as it was from neck to hem with absurd
tiny flounces. When she asserted that it had cost
a “very pretty figure” every woman in the audi-
ence leant forward and examined it critically, and
doubted her statement.
Tiny Decima Moore, continues my informant,
affected pale-blue, and looked like a forget-me-
not from the bunch which she carried through
the first act. Her Juliet gown in the second act
is of bespangled white chiffon. The, men in the
piece appear to be a cold-blooded lot, for, with the
exception of Kitty Heatherdon (who is beloved by
the fascinating though stony Capt. Caddingtnn),
not one in this group of beauteous maidens is provl-
ded with an admirer. Certainly the “Ambiguity"
girls chatter a great deal about adorers, who pro-
vide them with diamonds, broughams, and trifles
of that sort; but they don’t bring them on, to let
us have a look at them. They have always “just
said goodbye to them,” or “expect to meet them
at the stage-door at half-past 11,” or have some
excuse or other ; and, as boom-prosperity has not
returned suddenly to this city, and as the only
man at the stage-door was a gentleman with a
basket and a tallow-candle, selling trotters, we
Melb. girls take these inflated statements for what
they are worth, and opine that the “ Ambiguities”
are just “giving us guff” (as the rude little boys
say).
Under the thin disguise of “a grand fete," or
words to that effect, the supporters of what Mdme.
Sarah Grand calls a “ fire insurance office ” (they
call it All Saints Church on its notepaper) really
held a common or garden bazaar at Norwood
Park, Stanmore, on Monday last, in aid of the
bag as usual. The Acting-Governoress set the
affair afloat in the afternoon, but I went to hear
the moonlight concert at night, for which a great
success was locally anticipated. I approve of
moonlight concerts, brass band music, and red
umbrellas as things for which Australia
has plenty of room ; and so (although
rather late in the season for evening pro-
menading) this moonlight concert to which I
allude would have made “ one great effect,” as the
musicians say, if only the moon hadn’t winked
such a watery eye on it. Though, mind you, the
singing was occasionally very local. One lady
sang in a reedy voice, and another rendered con-
tralto music in mezzo-soprano, and the artistic
truth was not in them. The Titan of the pro-
gramme was the baritone, Dr. Maguire, who sang
“The King’s Champion.” in an even and cultivated
voice, yet with lots of color and no exaggeration
of force. I believe that the doctor is to be one of
the singers at next Sydney Liedertafel smoke oh.
The birth at last of a son and heir to the Car-
ington estates arouses much interest in Australia
where the parental experiences of Lord and Lady
C. were a source of universal solicitude. The
aristocratic matron who fails to furnish a boy to
keep the direct succession intact cannot consider
herself in any other light than a scorching failure
from her husband’s point of view, although I sup-
pose a matron who has a quiverful of daughters is
a dear and cherished object to the next-of-
kin or heir-at-law, or whatever it is proper
to call the earth-hungry relative who exists
in castles-in-the-air in constant terror of having
his family nose put out of joint. Not being the
heir-at-law, I am rather glad the Caringtons have
a boy at last. People of such laudable perseverance,
even in the teeth of what looked like a very
forlorn-hope, always deserve to succeed. By the
way, Premier Georgey Porgey Reid is also the
happy father of a bouncing babe. I have noticed a
tendency on the part of our Premier to go without
his eyeglass of late. He has even been photo-
graphed without it, and I think the appearance
of Reid, junior, explains the phenomenon. It has
been given to the baby to play with.
A leading Melb. tea - room is run by the
nieces of two very popular Melb. Clubsters of
old standing. By the way, why don’t women
start a restaurant where men might feed— i.e,,
where men might like to feed ? People are tired
of spooning-places, founded on buns and tea, into
which masculinity plunges with variegated needs,
and takes, lamblike for the nonce, whatever soggy
scone or washy liquor is set before it, provided it
be served by the right person ? That is the only
phase of tea-room “mashing ” that almost touches
the heroic—the determined battle a man will wage
with hide-bound pastry, laying his digestion, so
to speak, at the feet of his divinity. But men (I
don’t use the word in a generic sense merely) are
not built that foolish way, and usually avoid
femininely-run tea-rooms (I am not writing in a
Japanesy sense) as they would church-bazaars and
other one-sided places, where they don’t get the
worth of their money and are expected to smile
at grief, as it were.
When you come to reflect about him, man really
seems to be a greatly-suspected person if he only
stirs one inch out of his beaten track, or else how
is it that he cannot buy a pair of gloves in a
hosier’s shop—especially women’s small sizes—
without becoming an object of speculative interest
for every woman within cooee of him ? Men have
been known, I believe, to run wives and daughters
and such like expensive luxuries, but Society has
arbitrarily decided that these ladies superintend
their own outfits, merely trusting the old man
to foot the bills; therefore a youth cannot hope to
keep above suspicion who buys ladies’ kid gloves
in the open market. If he is a bachelor (though
even bachelors sometimes have sisters and maiden
aunts who presumably don’t go about bare-
handed), a first-class prima-facie case is made out
against him, but if he is a married man (who
might be allowed to dabble a little in such things,
one would think), the culprit is tried, condemned
and morally executed before he has made up his
mind what color he’ll take. It must be a curious
thing for a man to reflect that the unwritten
laws of his country absolutely forbid him to pur-
chase a pair of women’s gloves. What would
happen if he branched out personally and bought
a French corset, or a pair of silk stockings, is
beyond my calculation!
Women who seem to find so much to revolt
against cannot at least quarrel with their freedom
to shop. They have an absolute liberty to buy
whatever they choose, from a pin to a pair of
pants. For instance, nobody suspects a girl buy-
ing a necktie in a man’s shop of any baser inten-
tion than wearing it herself, or making an<
allegorical slip-knot of it for some matrimonially-
doomed young man’s neck. The only thing
women are suspected of in the market-place isj
ordering down bales of the goods from near the
roof, arid going out ultimately with a pair of shoe-
laces.

“ Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of the Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “a terrible
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland. where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and F. It is certainly the funniest Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “Killaloe” or
“ Ballyhooley.” It is just the thing tor an aspir-
ing oomique, pro. or amateur.*
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback-the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
