quarter of a yard at one penny. And the white
slave behind the counter had to make out an ac-
count, and get it signed by one of the partners of
the firm I Shopping must be at a low ebb in Mar-
vellous Melbourne.
I read the other day that a “ pretty ceremony
was performed in the hall of the Stewards of
Truth, .Tatter-sail's Chambers, Hunter - street,
styled a dedication service, when two children,
the sons of the president of the body, were dedi-
cated to the service of God anil humanity.” The
hall was trimmed witli masses of white flowers,
and white roses were showered <m the little young-
sters, and white wreaths placed on each as emblems
of purity! It is all very bathetic. Such things
niay be well enough to give the Australian boy
the moral, mystical tilt he would be somuch the
better for possessing within certain Well-defined
limits—but accounts of them sh mid never be pub-
lished. Think of the result. The eyes of a cyni-
cal world will follow these two young angels right
up to, and even past, the time when they become
aldermen or company-promoters.
It is somewhat astonishing to see how a “great”
daily, whose penlady always was wont to minutely
describe the outward and visible raiment of the
firm’s lady relatives in all their ramifications, took
no sort of heed at the recent A.J.C. meeting as
to what all these interesting people wore ! Simply
a calm statement that Mrs. Su-and So “ was pre-
sent ” not looking nice as usual ; and the result
was a more seemly and modest paper than ever
before issued from that particular office. But the
gowns of the ladies in question got there just the
same, for the rival pen woman had them all down
to the very shoe-laceis in the opposition news-
paper. Which strikes me as being a much more
decent way of doing things.
The way one wealthy woman (who could
import half Baris if she liked) turned out
at a public function the other day—black
skirt, plum-pudding blouse and a brown hat—
seemed to point the moral that every woman is at
heart a guy. Nearly every woman thinks she
would dress to the ends of her nails if her banking-
account would let her ; but directly your critics
know for an indisputable fact that you can be clad
in silk oap-k-pie, then you lose interest in the game
and come out in calico. There’s method in it,
after all. Miss Kilmansegg makes far more of a
sensation and is infinitely more talked about in a
giinea suit than ever she would be in jewelled
brocade. It’s only beggars who mustn’t wear rags.
In Cosmos for April, the photo, is a very faith-
ful one of Essie Jenyns and her child, and an in-
terview with the lady is given by Mrs. Charles
Bright, under the heading “Juliet Married—and
Happy.” Juliet, however, doesn’t seem to have'
very much of interest to say for herself, and her
diction is at times unearthly—like choice pickings
from “Pamela.” Fur example, re the new
dramatists: “Ask me not,” she says with impul-
sive scorn, “ what I think of them.” And again:
“ Bernhardt we saw in Paris. Of her I must nob
speak. She is beyond words to express.” I
wonder if she always speaks in that “sirrah ” tone
of voice? The best thing in Cosmos is a sketch
of “Mucus Clarke,” by Charles Bright, in which
the subject is freely quoted; but I cannot con-
gratulate the management on the sequence of the
items in the latest number of Sydney’s baby mag.
For instance, a dissertation on “Headache,” by
Dr. Sehwartzbaoh, is placed immediately before
“ Musical Notes,” by Henri Kowalski.

I have received a copy of Madame Weigel’s
Journal of Fashion, and I must confess that I
haven’t fellowship with quite all the novelties
this up-to-date periodical springs on us. For one
thing, it is alleged that a good many ladies are
wearing “ bodices with the pouch effect.” Just
think of that as a fashion for Millie. Kangaroo,
who already hangs her hands d la marsupial, and
who, in a long, narrow-trained frock and one of
the “new pouch bodices,” would almost come
under the operation of the Marsupial Act, I should
imagine.
I see that the talented Sydney Amateur Or-
chestral Society is advertising for new subscribers.
The A.O.S. (which mourns Signor Hazon, who
brought the Society along more than nicely)
looks more like the nucleus of a really suc-
cessful long-lived musical society than a great
many of its Australian contemporaries. The
average short-haired person rather pins his
musical faith to choral societies, where the words
enlighten his darkness as to the meaning of the
composer, and the V-shaped soprani appear now
and then; but the A. 0.5., which is purely instru-
mental, shows every sign of overcoming the com-
mon prejudice in favor of human voices. Apropos
of music, I hear that the handsome and dark-eyed
Mrs. Vauderveer-Greene is havinganextraordinary
success in London on the ballad-concert platform.
Mine. Emily Soldene, who is on the eve of
departure for London, where she purposes pub-
lishing a volume of theatrical reminiscences, is
to be tendered a complimentary benefit at Sydney
Lyceum on Wednesday afternoon, sth June. A
strong committee has the affair in hand.
I have lately received two waltzes, “The Bell”
waltz, by George Castledean, and “The Lobelia,”
by M. J. Hogan, and I really must say I don’t
know what I have'done to deserve two such com-
positions at one blow. Both are irritatingly tum-
tum and without sequence. '
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Sydney policemen, headed by a brass band
of brother “cops,” will parade Sydney streets in
full fig, including revolvers, on Queen’s birthday.
The general public don’t know how often
accused persons give themselves clean away.
Murderer Archer, executed for the slaying of
the little Burton-st. dressmaker, “ put himself
away ” in certain “ stiffs ” (letters) which he sent
out of gaol through prisoners who were purposely
“ put on to him ” by the police. By the way. a
lady’s watch and ring were last week found planted
on a beam in the ’bus-stables where Archer was
employed. They are believed to have belonged to
the victim and to have been put there by Archer,
and wore handed to the police. "

“ Work and Wages,” at Sydney Majesty’s,
is a good specimen of the noble British drama
which takes the bit in its jaw and steeplechases
through five acts of attractive incohereneies to a
judge’s box in front of which some of it dies
abruptly of heart-disease, while the remainder is
led away to marry a millionaire. Rignold is. of
coarse, the millionaire, and pretty and pathetic
Agnes Knight is the lady who shares his hand-
some features and fortunes after desperately
endeavouring to hnry herself out of sight with a
guilty secret which isn’t guilty. At the beginning
there is a strike at a steel-works, with an
agitator haranguing the men until Ids throat is
dry, when they march into a pub. behind the
scenes to get beer. It is quite obvious, however,
from the dispirited look of the men when they
come out into the green limelight of starvation,
that the pub, is a mere dummy, and The Bulle-
tin denounces it accordingly. Harry Plimmer,
the wicked employer, walks round and allows the
men to cheek him sociably ; and D’Orsay Ogden
is a blackleg whom everybody knows at once for
a foreigner from Ids way of saying “ Ze ” and
spreading out his hands with bony emphasis. It
seems that the wicked employer has ruined the
blackleg’s daughter Adele (Athena Claudius);and,
later on, he shoots her as well. Before this, how-
ever, George Rignold, as foreman of the steel-
works, matures a great invention, and about the
same time his wicked employer (who hates him
virulently) matures an explosion, which comes
with a boom and a flash and a roar, and nearly
kills George—but not quite. Meantime Scot
Inglis is a handsome lord who stands round and
makes copy-book reflections, and Mr. Sweeney is
a comic Irish personage who stands round and
makes jokes. Stirling Whyte, Frank Harcourt.
and a vast horde of others take part, and when
Plimmer is killed off at 11 p.m. everybody goes
away loaded with aggressive precepts and impres-
sive maxims.
* * * *
“ Aladdin Junior,” which draws near its
close at Sydney Royal, is chiefly a brightly-acted
excuse for vehemently applauding Maggie Moore
in the name-part, and H. R. Roberts as his
or her historically male mother. The difference
between one burlesque and another burlesque now-
adays is that they are botli the same burlesque;
and, if there is a third burlesque, then they are that
burlesque also. The actors and singers get out of
burlesques precisely what they put in; and, as
much ability and energy are put into ‘‘ Aladdin
Junior,” the public have also extricated much
profit. Particular notice is due to the pretty
dancing of Miss Nellie Osgood Moore, the little
11-year-old (so she says) maiden from Yankeeland,
whose portrait appears on page 10 of this issue.
Nellie (otherwise Aileen) is the daughter of
Maggie’s sister in Murka, and first appeared at
Hobart the other day, playing Bertie (the little
boy), in “Meg.” She proves conclusively that her
aunt has not absorbed all the family talent and
vivacity.
sit * * *
The revival of “ The Mikado,” at Sydney
Lyceum, was welcome, for Gilbert’s humor and
Sullivan’s music are both evergreen. Howard
Vernon, as usual, was the mainstay of the per-
formance, and one’s pleasure is none the less
because familiarity with his methods brings
admiration in its train. There is an intellectual
quality in Vernon’s rendering of rdles like
Ko-ko and Bunthorne which rarely shines
in his coadjutors, and the delicious fun
of his wooing of Katisha (including the
“tit-willow” song) reached on Saturday night
a very high artistic level. The other performers
did not let themselves go, and the opera as a
whole lacked dash. A six weeks’ season, with a
part every night, and a change twice a week, is
indeed enough to damp enthusiasm and hoarsen
throats. “The Mountebanks ” was to be played
on Wednesday.
¥£ * * *
Of other Sydney shows, “ Fun on the
Bristol” is creditably played to popular prices at
the School of Arts by a hard-working company ;
and the Tivoli’s patrons show by their numbers
their gratitude for the excellent programme. 1 he
Brough-Boucicault company are getting good
attendances at Brisbane Opera House, this week s
bill including “The Masqueraders” and “Re-
bellious Susan.” Altogether, the theatrical tide
of Eastern Australia is just now about high-water
mark.
“ In Town,” the second of three items on
Mm George Edwardes company’s list, struck Mel-
b mrno as violently as “ A Gaiety Girl ” would
liave struck that thick headed city if tluyiopula-
tion had been quite ready for it. “In Town ”
was the pioneer musical-comedy in London, where
“ A Gaiety Girl” is presumably reckoned an im-
provement on the original pattern. A reversal of
this order of things has happened in Melbourne,
and the result is worth remembering. An entirely
new kind (leaving the poorly-acted “ Morocco
Bound ” out of the question) of light entertain-
ment was offered to the public. They got the best
brand Hist, and 50 per cent, of them criticised the
unusual tipple ignorantly. But their palates were
becoming educated the while, and when they
came to sample a rather inferior article it was
voted nectar for the gods—by the gods, also by
the stalls and dress-circle. “In Town ” was
an emphatic success on Saturday night. If
the management were able to reproduce “ A
Gaiety Girl ” presently for the final week of the
season, it would no doubt achieve a much more
howling triumph than before. This remark is
made in justice to the clever ladies of the com-
pany, who formed the body and soul of the former
comedy, whereas they are merely the arms and
legs of the subject under discussion.
“In Town,” like everything associated
with the name of Arthur Roberts, the king of
Cockney humor, is mostly run by one man. Louis
Brad field is the Roberts of the piece, and nobody
else goes for very much. He impersonates an
insolent captain, a whirlwindy child of pleasure,
who is responsible for a rush of stage ladies to a
fashionable hotel in the first act, and a rush of
sassiety people to the greenroom of a theatre in
the second act. The captain represents the plot;
the rest is padding—dancing, songs, bustle, and
spectacle. In respect of satire, wit, and incidental
music this airy nothing is not equal to “ A Gaiety
Girl but no matter. It makes a flying start,
and travels faster in the estimation of the audience.
# * •
Mr. Brndfield rejoices in his great chance.
He is an easy, happy, droll comedian, a capital
dancer, a sufficiently good singer of the cheap
melodies provided for this show, and a fertile pan-
tomimist. Moreover, he takes the cake modestly,
and is by no means a hot, aggressive mummer.
Mr. Fred Kaye, as a love lorn call-boy at the
theatre; Mr. Bantock, as a private chaplain with
a comic hair-tuft ; and Mr. Ryley, in the serious
(not to say heavy) capacity of a British dock, fill
places in the middle distance. The importance of
the radiant ladies is collective, rather than indi-
vidual. Miss Decima Moore, whose small voice
was somewhat “off” on Saturday, plays a re-
spectable prima-donna in her own pretty little
way. A duchess and her daughter are played by
Mrs. Phelps and Miss Blanche Massey, but after
opening with a neat duet this blue-blooded pair
do very little. The other angels are actresses,
coming and going in clusters, and Miss Maud
Hobson is perhaps a few degrees lovelier than her
rival heavenly beings. Miss Florence Lloyd
comes to the front in trousers on the present
occasion. Miss Lloyd is the duke’s son, and
The Bulletin, being a democratic journal,
objects to her charming impersonation. If
dukes produced such lineal descendants, the
House of Peers wouldn’t be tottering. Miss
Madge Rossell’s solo-dance falls comparatively
flat, because it isn’t as Frenohy as her “Gaiety
Girl ” effort; but the pas de trois and the dresses
(Misses Hobson and Palotta wear tights occa-
sionally), and the scenery, and many other matters
not already specified, are d lieious.
** * *
“ La Pompadour,” by Sydney Grundy and
the late W. G. Wells, was dumped hastily upon
the stage of Melb. Bijou last Saturday. A his-
torical play, performed by a scratch company who
lose themselves in its rounded periods and bruise
their shins against the historical situations,
is not a thing to be lightly spoken of.
A masterly silence will meet the case. Mrs. G. B.
Lewis did justice to the lines of the name-part, and
on Friday that time-honored artist will positively
appear as Hamlet, for one night only. On Satur-
day, it is said, Frank Clarke intends to set up a
variety show in succession to Shakespeare’s im-
mortal tragedy. Bijou arrangements are hardly
to be depended on, however. As for variety
shows, Melbourne has enough of them to go on
with. The Rickards speculation flourishes at the
Opera House, whilst the Coghill Bros, have
brought the Oxford prices down to one “ bob ”
and sixpence. Also, the Cambridge theatre is
determined to work itself into popularity, so, in
the face of all this opposition, the Bijou had better
pause and consider.
** * *
At the Royal there is no visible falling-off
in the demand for “The Span of Life.’’ Of late
Mrs. Bland Holt has walked across the span
instead of Miss Hilda Spong, and the span looks
grateful for this new arrangement. Miss Spong
had a way of stopping awhile to ruminate upon
the spine of the middle spanner, and the b-tting
against her sudden disappearance down the yawn-
ing chasm was never more than sto 1. Mrs. Holt
makes the perilous journey with characteristic
sprightliness and a reassuring smile. Indeed,
that clever little lady would have to be in a very
tight place ere her disposition to laugh deserted
her.
** * *
Parson Haweis is bettering his Sydney
success at Melb. Athenaeum Hall. The reverend
philosopher and funny man has proved himself
no indifferent comedian, and his two extra
farewell lectures, to-night (Thursday) and Friday,
are expected to produce a state of standing-room
only. The D.aores give two shows at the
Athemeum on Saturday and Monday, and the
Esty-March ooncertists were to start proceedings
in the Town Hall on Wednesday of the present
week.
** * *
Joe Dias’ little room at North Fitzroy,
Melbourne, adjoining the Old Colonists’ Horne,
and within a stone’s throw of Merri Creek, contains
relics of a bygone time, when booms were not. Two
ancient playbills hang upon the walls. One, dated
Feb. 10, 1852, is a very mixed bill, presented at
the Queen’s Theatre, Melb., with Mr. and Mrs.
Young in large type. The Youngs starred in
conjunction with Ethiopian Minstrels; also,
Lachlan M'Gowan and Joe Dias appeared in this
special entertainment, whieh concluded with
“The Eleventh Hour, or The Murder on the
Moor.” Horrors were fully described in those
days. A second bill relates to the Montezuma
Theatre, Ballarat, year 1855. G. V. Brooke
played “The Stranger;” George Coppin, too, is
prominent on this tattered curio, and Joe Dias ia
down for a part in “ The Wandering Minstrel.”
An even more interesting document, framed over
the mantelpiece, is a travelling showman’s license
granted to John Musgrave (lie died awhile ago)
and Joseph Dias, in 1858, by Sir Henry Barkly,
Governor. A letter from Buddy's haydekong,
the Wellington of the period, mentioning that the
permit has been posted, adorns a separate frame.
According to old Joseph, the license aforesaid was
the last one issued in Victoria. It entitled him-
self and partner to give theatrical entertainments
anywhere in the province outside Melbourne and
Geelong. No restrictions were imposed upon the
company. They could produce “ Hamlet ” and
do a tumbling act on the same evening, or, if they
preferred to hold a circus, the license offered no
objection.
» * *
Joe Dias is nob a rippling stream of anec-
dote as a rule, but he begins to unburden himself
of old recollections when you get him in the
kitchen. Light wood for the kitchen-tire is sent
down by W. and M., from the Princess’s, it ap-
pears. Scene-frames and the debr/s of “heavy
sets’’ serve to boil the superannuated mummer’s
kettle, and the sparks turn his thoughts to the
profession as they fly upward. Then lie remem-
bers how he was prompter at the Victoria Theatre,
Sydney, in ’54 or '53, and passed the word occa-
sionally to Mrs. Guering. This sounds interest-
ing, because Mrs. Guering is Mrs. Dick Stewart,
and mother of the immortal Nellie. One feels
glad to hear that Nellie’s papa was not even ac-
quainted with Nellie’s mamma, much less married
to her, in ’54 or ’53, which is convincing evidence
against certain sacrilegious creatures who would
make our youthful vision a good 10 years older than
her birth-certificate. Joe Dias nearly burnt him-
self out of the Dramatic Home a few weeks ago,
but, when the angry flames had licked his couch
and devoured his curtains, and started to consume
the premises, Providence sent along a gardener,
who syringed the conflagration and preserved the
old man from a smoky doom. He scarcely at-
tempts to dissemble his natural pride in this melo-
dramatic incident.

Melb. Trades Hall seceded from the May
Day movement solely because H. H. Champion,
in whom it “hadn’t confidence,” occupied the

thus divided into two discordant factions clawing
at each other’s hair and eyes. Nobody catches

Dooley, the Melbourne savage who chopped
np his wife and mother-in-law with an axe (like
many other celebrities now before the vari-
ous tribunals —he was suffering from syphilitic
brain-disease) took arsenic before his capture, and
died soon afterwards. Thus was the hangman
cheated of his prey. If Mr. Dooley had only per-
formed in Sydney, the law authorities would, of
coarse, have given him lOgrs. of strychnine on top
of the arsenic, and pulled him round—and then
turned him over to “Nosey Hob ” as good as new.
Thanks to its benighted policy of Protection, the
miserable cabbage-garden hasn’t got a grip of the
very first principles of toxicology.
Salomons, Q.C., it is said, was invited to
preside oyer the Dean Commission, but declined—
on the ground of Ins former little unpleasantness
with the Judge concerned. By the way, it will
be a pity if Dean’s case is not conducted before
the Commission by either Salomons or Pilcher,
Q.C’s. With the composition of the Commission
the public is, on the whole, well satisfied, though
it is recognised as anomalous that (the matter
having to some extent assumed a political aspect)
anyone in any way dependent on Ministerial
favor Should take part judicially in the proceed-
ings. Ami, as the issues are of grave moment to a
certain Supreme Court Judge, it would have been
much better had it been found possible to arrange
that the case should not be judged by a practising
barrister of the Court.
The Bulletin has before it a copy of a
disreputable London baronet - conducted thief-
paper called Tit-Bits (visions here arise oi
a scrofulous fat boy eating grease with his
fingers), dated Kith March, 1805, wherein is pub-
lished a three-and-a-quarter-colnmn story headed
“The Little Duchess,” which, a notice, over the
heading states, “has been judged by the arbi-
trators to be the best sent in, and has, therefore,
gained the prize. A cheque of one guinea has
been forwarded to the sender, Mrs. E. Holland,
24 Burnt Oak Terrace, New Brompton, Kent.”
The name of the author is not appended to the
story, and, no notice to the contrary being visible,
it would appear that Mrs. E. Holland, aforesaid,
is the authoress of “The Little Duchess.” Now,
“The Little Duchess” was first printed in last
year’s Xmas number of The Bulletin, and was
the original work of that talented young Aus-
traiienne, Miss Ethel Turner, whom The Bulletin
was glad to have the privilege of paying for her
prettv story. A mail or two ago The Bulletin
got a letter from Sheffield (Eng.) Telegraph
enquiring terms for the rights of “The Little
Duchess ” and any other stories by the same
authoress for syndication in England, whereupon
this paper, not claiming copyright as against any-
one contributing to its columns, referred it to Miss
Turner herself. If Baronet Newnes stole or re-
ceived valuable stolen literary matter taken from
an English paper he would have to pay damages ;
but as Miss Turner happens to be an Australian
writing for Australian papers she cannot claim the
protection of the English copyright law ; for
while English copyright applies to all English
possessions and so runs in Australia, Australian
copyright is not operative in England 1 If the
English thief-papers continue to steal Bulletin
stories without acknowledgment this paper will
take legal means to prevent the issues containing
such stolen matter from being sold in Australia,
anyhow.

Society
Boiled down, the great Australian naval-
court-martial case stands thus. Surgeon Lea, of
H.B.M.S. Ringarooma, sick-listed Capt. Johnson,
of that vessel, on the ground, it would appear, of
alleged mental derangement. Whereupon the
captain charged the surgeon with insubordination,
proved his case (to the satisfaction of a court of
Ids brother captains), and had Surgeon Lea
cashiered. The court refused to hear any evi-
dence as to the captain’s state of mind, say-
ing that what they had to enquire into
was the charge of insubordination only.
Now, the doctor had, under the regulations, full
power to sick-list the captain, and when an officer
is sick-listed (especially on the alleged ground of
mental alienation) he is relieved of duty and is for
all practical purposes just as non-existent as if he
had had his skull sawn open and his brain dropped
into spirits-of-wine. Not that the Court had
to decide whether the captain was mentally
incapable or not; the only question really before
it was whether the doctor acted bona-fide, and
within his duty, in certifying to the eapcain’s
unfitness. If the doctor was able to show full
cause for his action, then the captain, whether
mentally ill or well, had no ground for complaint.
In declining to allow the doctor to attempt to
show cause, the Court acted foolishly and tyran-
nously.

The S.M. Herald’s truculent attitude over
the Dean trial, and its apathy re the flagrant
injustice of the Ringarooma court-martial, prove
that in the paper’s veins now runs only just
enough red blood to keep it alive. “For consti-
tuted authority, no matter how constituted,”
should be the Herald’s motto.

People are amused at the rupture in a
certain literary household caused by the wife’s
persistence in wearing cycling breeks. She is pen-
woman for a weekly paper ; he indites a column
in a more pretentious organ. Having quarrelled
over the Week dispute, she forthwith proceeds to
attack hubby under a thin disguise in her column
of feminine malice. He responds in his column
with a squelching par. on the asses some married
women make of themselves. They were left
lashing one another as we went to press.

deceased woman as a piece of evidence,” says a
press telegram, in re the murder in N.Q. of a gin

done.

* * *
lb is significant that the presiding Judge’s
notes, as summarised in the S.M. Herald, attri-
bute to Dr. Newmarch this blunt and unqualified
statement (the accuracy of which the Dr. is under-
stood to deny):—
Supposing a person took a dose of arsenic, and then
took strychnine, it would be an antidote to it. Arsenic
deadens the pulse, but strychnine would quicken it.
Then comes this ;
Dr. Geo. Rennie, Government Pathologist, agreed that
the symptoms described by Mrs. Dean were those of acute
arsenical poisoning. Strychnine taken with arsenio
would account for the steady pulse described. Arsenio
deadened the heart’s action ; strychnine stimulated it.
As both medicoes allege that they much qualified
the foregoing statements, it is here plain that in
future the evidence in every capital case should be
reported verbatim.

in the near future —“ Prisoner pleaded not guilty,

* * *
Apropos the Dean case. In London, years
ago, Edward Layton and his wife were unhappily
bonded. The wife died of arsenical poisoning, a
quantity of the drug being found in her bedroom.
Layton narrowly escaped the rope, and was on
the point of execution when it was established
that the wife had been an habitual arsenic-eater.

The latest thing in Australian private de-
tection is the ingratiating would-be parson, who

invites the wife to join in a brief prayer. A
Sydney man has successfully played that game in
fully half-a-dozen cases.

“ The solicitors of Sydney ” (says a country
paper) “are suffering more severely than other
professional men. One runs against a solicitor at
every street corner, and the cry is nothing to do.”
Which accounts for a lot of things. Amongst
others, for the fact that a person libelled in
Sydney the other day was interviewed by no less
than eight law-sharks by 2.30 p.m. of the day
after which the libel appeared. They all wanted
to conduct the case.
• * *
The perennial rascal who advertises for a
clerk (with cash security) still flourishes. Last
week one of these gentry was tripped up in a Syd-
ney Police-court on two charges of misappropria-
tion, and although in both cases the accused had
aimed at securing =Bl5, and was only partially
successful, the miserably inadequate fine of ,£3O,
or one year’s imprisonment, was all that was
inflicted—a sentence about equal to that of a man
who takes a pair of boots from a shop-front.
* * #
The Vic. Legal Trades Union occupied
fully two days in admission business last meeting
—a record. After a long delay, it has decided to
allow a member of the Maoriland fraternity into
its fold. The chances of N.S.W. leeches are
doubtful; but S.A. suckers will probably get in
all_ right. Law-Professor Moore, an English bar-
risiter, is applying for admission, and may slip
through with the help of a curious section in the
Vie. Undesirable Immigrants to the Legal Pro-
fessions Act which flies in the face of the time-
honored gag that every man is presumed to know
the law. By-the-by, Matthew Davies appeared in
wig and gown to move the court to overlook some
flaws in one of his old articled clerks’ papers.

A Queen-st. (Brisb.) tailor has completed
three orders for Bloomer costumes. One of them
is to be seen on cloudy nights on the back of a
bicycle skimming along the suburban lanes. The
others write to the papers wanting to know why
somebody else doesn’t start the racket.

Dear Bulletin, — A suggestion to Gover-
nors on reduced salary. At Dunedin (M.L.) the
local Government House, on Ocean Beach, is now
used as a seaside hotel. Could not Brassey and
the rest make up deficiencies by running their
residences as respectable boarding-houses or high-
class hostelries for the genteel traveller ? I know
a brewer who would start Loch with a fair stock.
— Thorold D.

The whirligig of time. The head office of
the Federal Bank is now a jeweller’s shop ; that
of the City of Melbourne Building Society, a
licensed pawnbroker’s ; of the Premier Permanent,
a tailor’s shop; ot the Metropolitan Bank, an
insurance office; of the Cafe Anglais, a land-
agency.

There was great anxiety expressed during
the many hours the Melb. jury was shut up in the
Abrahams’ case. During the trial Purves had set
out alleged efforts of certain persons to “ get at ”
some of the jurymen, in much the fashion adopted
by a certain big Melb. money-lender some ten
years ago. That jury-squarer got four years for
“ embracery.”

The Vic. junior cadets are to be abolished.
The toy soldierlings had one grand chance of
distinguishing themselves —when they were told
off to keep the Hopetoun departure crowd back
to the kerb. They failed dismally and nearly got
trodden to death before being run over.

For the position of Sydney correspondent
to a country newspaper, advertised the other day,
there were over 100 applicants, mostly amateurs.
Among the list was a M. P. who has enjoyed
many pickings, and is reputedly worth a few
thousands sterling. Journalists hate the gratui-
tous contributor ; they likewise cherish little love
for the well-to-do intruders in their domain.

The Vic. Wesleyan Church has just been
receiving back a penitent clergyman, who had not
done any wrong beyond going over to another
denomination. The poor fellow has abjectly to
crawl in, on all fours, solemnly abjuring all title
to any interest in the beneficiary funds in which
he was formerly a participant. Christianity ?
* # *
The Wesleyan clergy have a deadly “down”
on the presence of laymen in Conference, and
have resisted it tooth-and-nail. At last Confer-
ence, in Hobart, lodging was provided for all the
clergy, but not for any laymen. A resolution was
passed that laymen are in future to have the same
privilege as “ the cloth.”
* # •
A Melbourne paper describes certain
J.’sP., who are alleged to have been “ packed ” on
to a suburban Bench recently, as “itinerant
magistrates.” Every police-court solicitor in
Melbourne knows that a solitary sovereign will at
any time command the attendance on any subur-
ban bench of half-a-dozen to a dozen busted and
boozy J.’sP., and it is a shameful fact that there
are at least a score of these gentry in and around
Melbourne available for packing purposes at from
2s. 6d. to ss. a time. In a recent very dirty case,
where defendant was acquitted, the Bench con-
sisted of two or three local J.’sP. and nine itiner-
ants (all of defendant’s “color”) imported
specially for the occasion. An honorary magis-
tracy prostitutes justice, and should be abolished.

A deputation will shortly wait upon the
N.S.W. Minister of Justice to lay before him the
brutal facts connected with the trial of the Mount
Rennie prisoners, and to enquire how Govt, pro-
poses to justify the further detention of men tried
in such a fashion. It is felt in a wide circle that
no more favorable opportunity than the present
could possibly be found for properly ventilating
the facts of this monstrous case, concerning which
the public of N.S. Wales, hopelessly blood-
drunken at the time of the trial, has never been
more than partially informed.

A woman up North got a set of teeth from
a dentist on time-payment. Her payments falling
behind, he called at the house, to find she was
just dead —and the woman’s husband gave him
the teeth back again, as he was not able to pay
for them. Next mouth, please !

Retrenchment has entered Yic. prisons,
and, for the future, prisoners are to support life
and the weariness of their position on shorter and
cheaper rations. The capitalistic press has not
accused the Government of breaking faith with
vucarcerated bankers and ex-saints.
FREDERICK VILLIERS, artist and war-
correspondent.
Frederic Yilliers, whose portrait is printed
on this page, is due in Sydney by the Warrimoo
tliis week. As war-artist he has a veteran’s
record. He was all through the China-Japanese
campaign, and it was his ninth. He was artist-
correspondent for the Graphic through the
Russo-Turkish war, was on H.M.S. Condor at
the bombardment of Alexandria, and represented
the same paper through the Afghan war, 1878-’BO,
the Nile Expedition for the relief of Gordon, the
Servian and Bulgarian war, and the Burmese
campaign. Yet he is still in the flesh, ever eager
for the fray; and although, thanks to the enterprise
of R. S. Smy the, he has come to Australia to relate
his experiences of the War of the Yellow Nations
before returning to England, and to exhibit his
pictures of the various battles and the taking of
Port Arthur, he a'ready, doubtless, like the war-
house, sniffs more battles not very far off. Thus
he is announced to give his opening lecture in
Sydney Centenary Hall next Wednesday ; but it
is quite possible that before then he will have re-
ceived instructions from Fleet-street to go to a
much bigger war than the last. Forbes, who is
not the most amiable of personages, was always a
staunch comrade of Yilliers. “Whenever,” says
Forbes, “the war-tocsin sounded, it was the
signal, too, of a call from Yilliers to know when I
was setting out; it went without saying that he
and I were to go together.”

On several coasting steamers, including
some in the Maoriland trade, men who are worked
all day loading and unloading cargo are obliged to
take the wheel at night, thus doing between 14
and 18 hours’ duty at a stretch,

A young lady has just entered a Melb.
attorney’s office as articled clerk.
The “authorities at the Chapter House”
have at last cut down the scrub, undergrowth and
weeds, which have for years choked the old Sand-
hills cemetery, at Belmore Park, Sydney, where
rest the bones of many N.S.W. pioneers, “free
and bond.” Amongst the tombstones brought to
light in the clearing-up are three noteworthy and
well-preserved, the first bearing the following
inscription:—
Erected by Henry and Glarind i Parkes to the memory
of their infant children, Mary Parkes, bom Feb. 16, died
Dec. 5, 1846, buried underneath. Thomas Campbell
Parkes, born April 18, died May 5, 1837, buried in St.
Paul’s churchyard, Birmingham. Clarinda Martha
Parkes, born June 23rd, died June 24, 1838, buried in St.
Paul’s churchyard, Birmingham.
The second :
In memory of John Sampson, Esq., sometime Ilia
Majesty’s Solicitor-General for the colony, who departed
this life, deeply regretted, on the 27th Oct., 1829, aged 32
years.
Mr. Sampson arrived as Solicitor-General in
March, 1828. The third tombstone is inscribed :
Sacred to the memory of John Putland, Esq., captain
H.M. ship Porpoise, chief magistrate throughout the
territory and aide-de-camp to his Excellency Governor
Bligh, departed this life Jan. 4, 1808, aged 27 years.
Capt. Putland was the husband of the plucky
lady, Gov. Bligh’s daughter, who stood between
the Governor and the soldiers when the latter
endeavored to depose her father. The captain’s
bones were in the George-st. cemetery, but were
removed to the Sandhills, with the headstone,
when the George-st. site was handed over to the
corporation.

Twenty-five per cent, of all pet parrots
suffer from tuberculosis. That’s the sort of thing
that sets one thinking. “Kiss pretty Joey!”
Yes, at the risk of your life.
One of the greatest frauds
is the pupil teacher dodge. It
is mostly worked in girls’schools,
on desperate women who have
no choice. They have to bake
classes all day, walk out with
pupils, and generally do all regu-
lar teacher’s work with this dif-
ference—that they get no salary;
a scamped half-hour from a \ bit-
ing music or drawing teacher
and board and lodging being
considered sufficient. In some
cases, this latter item is not
even allowed, for there are
fashionable colleges in Mel-
bourne that get all this work
from pupil-teachers and charge
them a week as well.
# * *
Dear Bulletin, Why
don’t men go to church ? T hey
do go. Some churches attract
more men than women ; some
more women than men ; some
attract the two sexes, about,
equally. Taking things all
round, however, there is little
to choose between the sexes.
As a general rule, where the
parson is mild and young and
has his hair parted in the
middle, the women predominate;
and where the parson is bald and
talks much about hell, there the
men are more numerous. The
balder be is, and the more he
talks about hell, the smaller the
female element in the congrega-
tion. —Yours, &0., T.S.

A young Maorilander was
visiting Bracti)<ir, in Scotland,
near Balmoral, and attended a
bazaar whereat the centre of
attraction was the stall of the
Princess Louise,- who has more
brains than most of her rela-
tions. The young man ap-
proached and asked the price of
a pretty sachet. “ Fifteen shil-
lings,’’ said Louise. Wbats
it for?” said the young man.
“ Oh, you buy it and give it to a
lady, She’ll know what it’s
for.'” “ But I’d like to know,”
persisted the inquisitive Maori-
lander. “ Well, whisper,” said
the Princess, leaning over and
whispering in his ear ; “ it’s a
lady’s nightgown case.” He
bought it.

OUT OF RESPECT TO THE GOVERNOR.
What a terrable fuss
There is just now in the bush
About tb u death off
The late Governor Duff.
My wife she’s got a bad atack.
And wanted me to buy some black
For her to wear on the wallaby l rack
Out of respect to the Governor.
I tried to persuade her
But it was no use
That people in the bush
Was though no more off tha i k n raroos,
But I had to give in
And buy her a pair of blacks - ■>•:<! igs
For her to wear on the wallaby t uck
Out of respect to the Governor.
How many in Sydney will we u- black
For which me and my mate-i
Will have to oontrihut a whack.
As for me I pick d up a old co i t
Which apeard to be black
I tore off a strip to make ms a I rip
To itch up my pants and wear it t iere
Out of respect to the Governor.
Our little one died
Before he could toddle
Perhaps no one was sorry
But me and its mother
There was one simple reason-
We did not wear black
Apd that was because we hadn't tiie cash
But that was no fault of the Governor.
Our parsons too of every creed
Will utter a prayer
And with much sympathy feel
Tho they preach and tell us
There is only one heaven
And with the man up above
We are all on a level
On a level with the Queen and the Governor.
But the man that dies on the track
What a different tale
He would be buryed more decent
If he had died in jail
They will bury him there
Without prays or coffin
They will tumble him in as if he was nothing
Without any respect for his Governor.
But how it makes me shudder
For we know he like Duff
Once had a mother
But what can we expect
When the Premier tells us
That a bad man not so good as a dog
So now I will finish my prattle
Without any disrespect for the Governor.

At Poverty Point.
Deoima Moore has cousins in Brighton (Vio.) who will
never have the pleasure of seeing their charming relative
act, as they consider the theatre very wicked indeed.
Miss Massey, of the Gaiety, is siniiularly like the Rose
Massey of 20 years ago, dame of Harry Montague, the
fashionable comedian, and complainant against him in a
breach-of-promise case. There used to be a photo, of her
as a basket of mischief, with her smiling face emerging
from a hamper.
The Melb. Y.M.O.A. liberalises. At its May Day con-
cert two actresses sang: Ida Osborne, and Isabel Webster,
who was in Coppin’s panto.
Pretty actress after rehearsal with one of the Firm’s
companies : “ Williamson says that I shall not he wanted
to do that dance in the second act now. Isn’t itashame?
And after I had got a new yellow silk petticoat to dance
it in, too. Never mind, I’ll take good care that the
people see I’ve got the petticoat, anyhow.” And no
doubt she did.
Maud Hobson never, on any account, gives the audience
a view of her legs in the “ Gaiety Girl.” In the bathing-
machine scene the performers wear cloaks which cover
bathing-suits, some fitting closely to the limbs, and
others which, as in Decima Moore’s case, are baggy
knickers. All the girls but the fair Hobson let the cloaks
flutter apart for a minute or two, but Maud keeps hers
tight round her limbs. As it is of soft, clinging muslin,
however,it does give a little comforting consolation to
the johnnies in the stalls, in the shape of the outline nf a
pair of substantial and shapely pillars, the upper regions
i f which are dimly seen’to be clothed with loose white
silk trouseretles. The Book of Revelations is onlyopened
in the new piece, “In Town.”
The contract between the Dacrea and Phil. Stuart,
which was for a year, has mutually been declared off.
Dacre and his wife, Amy Roselle, are arranging for short
seasons at Adelaide and Sydney, “on their own.” Then,
it is said, they will return to Melb. under “the firm’s”
management.
So far, the Gaiety Co. has returned a good profit to
“ the firm,” but nothing like so substantial an amount
as the Leslie-Lonnen co.’s.
The Comic Opera Co., now in Sydney, was the first co.
formed by Williamson and his partners, 15 years ago, and
it has been the most profitable and successful. It has
survived the competition of a dozen other similar com-
panies, long since dead and buried. Also, it has the
largest repertoire of operas in the world, some 30 in
number. During the eight weeks Sydney season 13 of
them will be produced.
The Melb. bald-heads, discussing the Gaiety girlhood,
plump unanimously for Maud Hobson as a beauty and
a woman ; Johnniedorn elects Grace Palotta, with her
fascinating lips and chic Parisian manners, as its queen ;
the “gods” vote for the leggy and olive-skinned Florence
Lloyd ; the d.c. maidens place their admiring faich in the
fragile, porcelain beauty of Blanche Massey and Decima
Moore; while everybody admits that so large and varie-
gated an assortment of feminine loveliness has never
before thrust itself on the gaze of the Melb. public.
J. O. Williamson, of “the Firm,” has played as a star
in every English-speaking city in the world.
Harry Rickards meditates a trip ’Ome in search of fresh
variety talent. The sprightly Alice Leamar is said to
have already decided to come out again under his manage-
ment.
Item omitted from Melb. version of “The Gaiety Girl. 1 ’
Lady Virginia: “Marriage is an ancient institution;
divorce is a mere mushroom.” Sir Lewis Grey (with a
leer): “ And, like a mushroom, it often springs up in a
single night.”
Charlie Brighton, Augustus Harris’ brother-in law, is
now “doing the colonies,” looking out for new talent for
their London Xmas show. Had he been a few weeks
earlier in Melbourne he might have had Will. Barnes’
Australian Bird Ballet to take back with him. Anyhow,
as the old country is coming to Australia for theatrical
stars it may just as well come for theatrical notions.
Dear Bulletin,— When will some live “variety”
manager rise to the occasion and banish the “serio-
comic ” from the stage for ever? The gallery like it, do
they? It would be interesting to have statistics of just
how many variety connoisseurs—the backbone of such
shows —are driven away from otherwise good combinations
by these hybrid monstrosities. Perhaps the introduction
into the “halls” of genuine, well-trained vocalists like
Norrie, Isabel Webster, &e., may eventually banish the
seriocomic. Another outrage is the “descriptive
vocalist,” who, though he doesn’t, fortunately, get a
show to play Hamlet., always goes as near it as oppor-
tunity allows. — Baldy.
Quite a rage has set in for photos, of pretty and shapely
Ethel Haydon in London. She has sat to nearly every
photographer of note, and some of the pictures of the
egg-shaped little woman which have reached these shores
are simply ravishing. Ethel has filled out and become a
great deal more womanly and “ grown-up’’-looking since
she went ’Ome.
Melba tells how she sang Siebel in “Faust” at a few
minutes’ notice under distressing circs. She hadn’t any
tights for the part, and had to fall back on an extra
capacious pair which the fat Italian tenor had in his
trunk. They were so roomy that the heels of the feet
reached to the fair warbler’s dimpled knees, and she had
to cover the creases up by pinning a long cloak round her
limbs.
Dear Bulletin, —ln his book, “My Musical Life,”
Pastor Haweis quite gives the reader to understand that
he was a very superior player. If I remember rightly, he
says: “ I know I always had the instinct of a virtuoso”;
in'fact, throughout the first portion of the book he makes
no secret of the fact that he was quite in the first flight.
I once asked a celebrated violinist if he knew anything of
Haweis’ playing. He replied : “ Well, he says he is a
great fiddler—and,” he added after an eloquent pause,

Elton’s first engagement lives fresh in his memory. It
occurred at a small town near Liverpool, when he was
quite a lad. He played for a fortnight, and after the
closing night’s performance he was called into the trea-
sury, where ss. in coppers and threepenny-bits was
placed in his hand. He had agreed for £2 10s., and, as he
had incurred two weeks’ hotel expenses, the unexpected
shrinkage fairly staggered him. In a deeply-depressed
state of mind, and pondering over the misfortunes of life,
he wandered out of the theatre so thoroughly immersed
in his troubles that he walked straight over the bank of
the local canal, and fell into the slush below. He managed
to struggle out of the 4ft. of mud and water, still holding
his heap of coin, not one being missing!

Ed. Bulletin,—Wilde's plays are incidental to his
“ epigrams ’’—not the-epitframs to the plays, which are
little more than settings for the superficial cynicisms
which Oscar kept in stock, and which he manufactured,
for the most part, by the simple mechanical process of
standing some common sociological axiom on its head.
Factory-made epigram, in a word—as distinguished from
that spontaneously whimsical correlation of ideas lor
which we have still to go to France. —M.
A strolling co. was in the Q. back-blocks and the actor
billed for the detective gave way to what he called dys-
pepsia. To fill the emergency the local policeman con-
sented to take his place. “ You have only to arrest him in
the usual way,” said the manager, encouragingly. The
cop. grabbed the heavy villain, knocked him down and
leaned on his chest. Then he tore his coat off, searched
it, ripped up his waistcoat and found nothing. After
running his fingers through the villain’s hair, he was pro-
ceeding to see if there was any stolen property in his shirt
when the curtain went down.

Mark Twain is coming to do Australia in the charge of
R. S. Smylhe. Mark will probably be run as a clergy-
man in keeping with Smythe’s joke—running recent
parsons as humorists. Talmage was described as “having
a good deal of Mark Twain humor, with none of his vul-
garity.” The people will find when the “Innocent
Abroad ” arrives that the humor of the Brooklyn pulpit
bruiser was really Twain’s. They already know that his
vulgarity was all his own.
There are now four juvenile minstrel co.’s touring
Melb. suburbs, in addition to six dramatic co.’s (inclusive
of Dan. Barry’s, Charlie Taylor’s, and a contingent from
the Bijou, which plays in St. Kilda). In Colling-
wood Town Hall alone Hayes’, Dan. Barry’s and Charlie
Taylor’s companies are billed to appear on separate nights
in the one week. Ail these co.’s, with the exception of
the Bijou contingent, play to “silver coin” audiences.
In short, the Melb. suburbs are theatrically glutted, and
it is a desperate fight to see which will hold out the
longest. This is “ sapping ” the theatres with a ven-
geance.
Letter penned to Fanny Brough, the entertaining
comedienne and sister of our Robert, by an embryo Aus-
tralian Leslie :—“ Dear Miss Brough,- The one who pens
these lines to you is an Australian youth (19 on the 26th
of this month). For twelve years he has set his thoughts
on the stage, not with the idea of it being a fairyland, but
knowing it to be the place where he knew he could make
a name for himself. Among many artists who have come
to Australia, and who I have seen (giving out pro-
grammes, &c., every night of the season), Leslie, Lonnen,
and Terry have been carefully watched by me. One can-
not do nor say half of what they would like to in any
number of letters, and it sounds very tall talk to say that
within six or seven months I would he able to give Lonnen
a start. Such, however, I feel to be the case. An outsider
like myself has little or no chance to get on the
Stage out here, where, if they want a corpse, they
would seek to get an order to open up the grave of one of
the stage hands’ grandmothers. 1 have not any parents,
some day I may have money, but in the meantime I am
unable to get £l4 14s. to get to England. Tickets can be
sent out to people so as to enable them to join their
friends at home. Could not you get up an art union and
have me for the prize, or, better still, if you send it your-
self. I have formed a high opinion of you from what I
have read of you, the proof of which is the letter. I will
look out for an answer when I think it is due. Wishing
you a happy New Year, &c., &o. P.S.—Trusting for an
early answer and your pardon, should anything be out
of place, as I was in a hurry to catch the overland mail-
train.”

When parson Haweis lectures on sacred subjects he
dons priest’s garb. Other times he sports faultless
evening dress, with a large ruby in his boiled shirt-front.
The sight of a very lovely woman in the stage-box is
sometimes rather distracting to the actors on the stage.
This was demonstrated the other night in a Southern
theatre, when one of the local belles entered the box with
a party of friends. The hero in the play fixed his eyes on
the lady, and addressed the rhapsodies intended for the
heroine directly at her. It was most embarrassing for
the beauteous one ; and when the audience began to
notice the too-evident admiration of the actor, the lady
retired behind the curtains of the box covered with con-
fusion, and so lost the two best acts and thrilling situation
in a capital play.
In the play of “Charlotte Corday,” Bellew and the
beauteous Potter’s vessel lately had a nasty contretemps
in America. She capsized Marat in the bath, showing
there was no water in it, and Marat was fully dressed.
What a chance to emulate the actor who blacked himself
all over for Othello !
That plucky seronaut, Miss Viola, lately found herself in
a trying situation at Gympie. The balloon caught fire
at the moment of ascent and the aeronaut rose into the
air like a veritable Elijah, off to the heavens in a
flaming chariot. During the ascent the lady calmly
waved her handkerchief to the crowd from her perch on
the trapeze until the balloon attained a height of some
2000 feet and commenced to descend. The parachute was
entangled and acted badly; but Miss V. eventually
alighted safe and sound.

Dear Bulletin,— Why do we tolerate applause in theatres
and yet eject the person who vocally expresses disappro-
bation? At the first glance, it would seem as reasonable
to hiss as to shout encore, “ but, when you come to think
of it,” the object of a play is to excite the emotions
pleasurably, and any conduct on the part of the spectators
generally conducive to this is permissible. To guy an
actor is sure to detract from his ability to please: an
organised claque, though a nuisance, is hound to encourage
him. Hence, following long-established custom, theatrical
entrepreneurs condone the demerits of the applause-giv-
ing system.— Junor.

The Wild-Cat Column.
One of the reconstructed banks takes its
deposit-receipts at their face value in payment of calls.
Therefore, the knowing shareholder, instead of paving
cash, buys deposits at a big reduction, and wipes off his
liability cheaply. There are a good many different
aspects to this case. If the bank got its calls in cash and
then bought deposits with the proceeds, it would make
25 per cent, profit for the shareholders, but it is a hard
business to get calls paid in cash, nowadays, so it lets the
shareholder annex the 25 per cent, instead. He pays up
promptly, too, because the deposits keep rising in price,
and he wants to get them while they are cheap, and his
purchases make them keep on rising, so that people think
confidence is really being restored. Also, the promptness
with which the calls come in makes the bank look
healthy, and further assists to restore confidence. And,
in any'case, the concern isn’t likely to pay divs. on its
ordinary capital for the nextseven years, and seven years’
interest saved on the deposit is over 30 per cent., so all
parties feel satisfied.
• * * *
Several banks which used to publish their
balance-sheets as great two-column advts. above the lead-
ing article, now crowd them into a half-column in an
obscure corner among the pills and the lost dogs. It is
cheaper, and, besides, the fewer people who see a balance-
sheet the better nowadays.
• * * *
According to the Australian Tory press, lying
dismally and deliberately in the face of every kind of
evidence, Maoriland, under the most democratic govern-
ment in Australasia, is a miserable and accursed place.
Population flees from it in shoals ; capital is driven away
by the graduated land and income-tax; there is to be an
immense Treasury deficit at an early date ; its financial
legislation outrages all economic maxims. Yet it is this
poverty-stricken province which has turned over a fresh
page in the book of Australasian finance, by floating suc-
cessfully a £1,500,000 loan at 3 per cent.—the lowest rate
at which Australasian securities have ever been accepted
on London market. The Conservative curses turn to
blessings under the enraged prophets’ eyes.
The loan was covered nearly four times, the
total subscription being £5,963,400, at an average price of
£94 Bs. 9d., or £4 Bs. 9d. more than the fixed minimum.
The province thus gets its money at about £3 3s. 3d. per
cent., or 11s. 3d. less than the price paid for the £2,700,000
loan of October last. This improvement in credit is in
the face of all the “disastrous legislation” which has
made the S.M. Herald and Melb. Argus wail so violently,
and in face, too, of the fact that the £1,500,000 is wanted
for the set purpose of advancing money to settlers, in
defiance of all Tory ideas of governmental rights and
duties.
The Maoriland Treasurer now proposes a
gradual conversion of the whole of the province’s debt
into 3 per cent, stock, thus aiming to do single-handed
what it was thought only the treasurer of the Australian
Commonwealth could accomplish. He may have to bonus
investors in order to achieve his object, but the annual
interest saving should more than make up. The market
is exceptionally favorable just now, and if Maoriland gets
over the stile there will be other provinces eager to follow
her.
These remarks apply to conversion schemes only,
and even conversion should be managed locally as tar as
possible. The most successful loan in London is really
a failure, and the more successful it is the bigger
is the failure; for the gain merely serves as an
inducement to these provinces to borrow more
violently than heretofore. The restoration of credit
in London is a curse ; and the best thing
that could happen to Australasia would be the
utter destruction of its British credit now and
for evermore. The Maoriland Government, with its
alleged national policy, should have done its bor-
rowings locally, and not gone back to wallow
in the mire of Vogelism. The new loan will probably
prove a paying investment, but even that does not fur-
nish any excuse for the folly of Seddon and Co. in having
recourse again to the British usurer. Moreover, there
was no real reason at present for having recourse to any-
body. The State’s credit is quite good enough to keep
afloat £1,000,000 worth of paper-money, and that sum
would at least have given the Credit Fonder a good start.

An Australian bank-manager, who is universally
allowed to be the “deoentest fellow going,” earns his
reputation by a simple device. _ He has a lot of letter-
paper imprinted with the heading of “Head Office, Lon-
don.” Assuming that a customer is getting too much
overdrawn, this philanthropic manager has a long letter
type-written in his own office to the effect that “ ths
(London) directors note with alarm that Mr. So-and-So is
far too much ahead, and that unless he can put up
additional security the account must be instantly re-
duced to £ Mr. So-and-So is sent for, and is shown
the letter. Mr. Manager commiserates with him, says it’s
a d d shame, and that so far as he (Mr. Manager) is
concerned he could have another couple of thon., but it’s
more than his position is worth, &c. Is sorry to say it
must he reduced so much. Hence his popularity, yet at
bottom he’s a perfect sneezer.
In these dull times, when money (with the excep-
tion of diva., of course) has almost double the purchas-
ing power it had a few years ago, the gigantic
Colonial Sugar Co. still manages to pay its 10
per cent, to the numberless widows and orphans and
ladies-in-reduced-circumstances, who are dependent on it.
The year’s net profit was £172,829, out of which 20 per
cent, could easily have been paid, but the enormous
amount of £87,830 was carried forward.

The unlucky Orient S.N. Co, made a profit of
£50,800 last half-year, but has to put it to reserve for
depreciation ; and the shareholders once more sigh for
the vanished div. The Chimborazo loss wiped out
£66,800; and the stranded Oroya will probably take five
figures out of this half’s profits. The reserve now stands
at £423,800. As the Orient Co. consistently employs
white labor, while its great rival, the P. and O. as con-
sistently employs black, The Bulletin’s sympathies are
all with the former: and it hopes that the luck will soon
take a turn.

During the past fortnight two reconstructed
banks down South have, by not being in a position to
afford certain of their customers larger facilities than
heretofore, lost at least 15 very choice accounts, w hich
have been eagerly snapped up by non-reconstructors.
The lean kine have only skin and bones left, whilst the
fat banks are getting such good biz. that they are shunt-
ing “weak” accounts altogether. So far as Victoria is
concerned, the “little man” will shortly have no bank at
ail to go to unless he pays for the privilege of trusting a
“ big ” bank with his money.
• ♦ * *
The Westralian loan of <£750,000 (half the
£1,500,000 “sop” which secured the triumph of the
Forrest Ministry at last year’s elections) was floated very
successfully at an average of £lO3 Is. 4d.—a price for 3J
per cent, stock rarely bettered by any Austra-
lian province. The total amount subscribed was
£2,860,000. With this addition the W.A. debt will stand
at £4,167,339 (including £185,085 Treasury Bills), in-
volving an annual charge on revenue for interest and
sinking-fund of £159,512. The sinking-fund accrued to
June 30, ’94, was £138,531 —not much ; but a good
deal, seeing that S.A., N.S.W., and Queensland have none
whatever. Taking the present Westralian population at
83,000, the public debt will now stand at about £52 per
head, about £4 over the Australasian average, but 120
below the magnificent figures of £72 which represent the
indebtedness of every Queenslander.

The gradual revival of Vic. trade has been
apparent in the real estate market for months past. Even
in suburbs such as Brunswick and Coburg, where houses
could have been had rent-free a little while ago, a decent
dwelling brings a fair rent —as rents go nowadays.
* * * *
The noxious supported-aecount system, respon-
sible in the past for the downfall of many a minor mer-
chant, and which was much curtailed during the past few
years, threatens, with the prospects of renewed trade, to
blossom again in Victoria. The big houses aln ady begin
to snap up any poor devil who may have a hundred or
two, and with glowing promises collar his " ready ” and
plant him in some decayed one-horse township with a
rotten and out-of-date stock not worth removal. But the
smaller city houses, agents, &0., who have been bitten in
the past, trade too cautiously now, and it will take years
before the “ big ” men will be able to work the “ sucked-
orange” game so successfully as of yore.
♦ * * «
A pitiful sight in Sydney just now is the terrible
competition between bum-bailiffs. There’s a boom in
their business, but the recruits added to their ranks have
made even this trading on the misery of others a constant
fight to a dead finish. Squads of these professionals
may be seen at set hours promenading opposite a money-
lending establishment. Suddenly Hooknose Eshkvire
appears at a door, and there is a rush and a scramble,
and a threatened battling for first place and employment.
N.S.VV. is turning the corner, and Mordeoohen means to
close down before it does turn it.

* * * *
The Guernsey market scheme of finance has
often been brought under notice of Australian Parlia-
ments, but as Fatman would rend Heaven with his cries,
were any attempt made to reduce the wages (interest) of
money, nothing has been done. The old bogie of gold
reserve would be trotted out, as though 90 per cent, of
business was not done without it. Interest is falling fast,
and if the Guernsey scheme were applied to works
assuredly reproductive here, it would compel capitalists
to lend to manufacturers at rates that would enable
them to establish industries which, under the present
rates of 10 and 124 per cent., are impossible.
* ♦ * •
Judge Molesworth, of Victoria, deserves sym-
pathy. He is called upon to administer two Acts-the
Insolvency (an ineffective thing of shreds and patches)
and the Licensing (the worst example of incomprehen
sibie muddle extant). In the Insolvency Court he haa
remedied abuses as far as possible under the circum-
stances, and “cronk ” trustees are being gradually wiped
out. Until a big boomer made a rush and got out by
way of the Full Court, he held the insolvents in fair
check, but now they are making a big race for the gap
made'by the aforesaid bonrjer. In the Licensing Couit
again he does his best, but is seriously hampered by th •
Act’s ineffectiveness and incomprehensibility. To have
to administer two such statutes is enough to kiil any
Judge’s reputation. You might as well tie a man’s arms
and ask him to sprint.

A scheme is afloat to amalgamate the leading
Trade Protection Societies of N.S. Wales, Maoriland,
Victoria, S.A., Queensland and Tasmania. The syndicate
(mostly Hebrews) are to be liable for £50,000 if it comes
off, and it’s really funny to see the frantic efforts each of
the societies the syndicate proposes to absorb is making
to ascertain whether the syndicators are individually good
enough lor their several liabilities.

Dr. Cockburn, S.A. Minister of Agricul-
ture, now runs a pig-farm at Mount Barker (S.A.).
The other day Lmut.-Gov. Way, walking along
an Adelaide terrace, ran against the Doc., who
seemed in deep thought. “ Hullo, Doctor,” said
little Sammy, “what are you worrying about
now ? Some new problem in statecraft ?” “ No ;

Henry Parkes, who has just completed his
41st year of political service, will be 80 years of
age on 27th inst.
Ex-Premier Dibbs has hurt his ankle.
Omen? Ex-Premier Parkes’ decline dated from
the day he hurt his ankle.

George Shenfcon as choir-master and “ Lady ”
Shenton as chief soprano.

liament a novel appeared with the title, “ Hogan,
M.P.” “ Real life imitates fiction.”

MICHAEL DAVITT.
Re nervous witnesses. A few years ago, a
N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge giving evidence m
a capital case was so nervous that the cross-exam-
ining counsel, “Jack’’Want, had to “give him
■time !”
Card. Moran will not go to Adelaide to
invest Archbishop O’Reilly with the pallium
which arrived in April. Dr. O’Reilly is poor, and
cannot stand the expenses incidental to entertain-
ing a large prelatical party.
Chancellor Brownless, of Melb. University,
who was sadly injured by a street-accident a
couple of years ago, has to travel in a bath-chair.
It is feared the veteran doctor has no chance of
recovering the use of his limbs.
Chief Justice Madden’s bar-income must
have been very heavy, as no consideration for the
junior bar ever induced him to let a guinea go by.
He refused “silk” (which would have deprived
him of the profit of drawing pleadings), and never
refused a pot-boiler.
Pastor Haweis much reminds the grey-
beards of Melb. of little Orion Horne, the poet.
Haweis wanted to give lectures two hours and
a-half long, but manager Smythe fixed the limit
at an hour and a-half, which limit Haweis always
exceeds, by the way.
Several of the Queen-st. (Bris.) photo-
graphers display pictures of Griffith, 0.J.,
in full robes, taken in half-a-dozen diiterenb
positions. Griffith also flashes his Court dress
on the smallest public provocation—and demon-
strates his pigfcontoedness.
Fitzgerald, ex-M.L.A. (N.S.W.), and
Louis Becke went to the big Maori meeting
at Maungakawa, in the Waikato. ihey were
the only Europeans present, and were invited by
Hone Heke, M.H.R., the sarcastic young Maori
antagonist of Premier Seddon.

In good dry Scotch he says : Wi the allowances
keepitback, an’ a bare seven thoosan , ye II no
get ony but a wealthy mon to tak it, an pmr s
sake ! I am no a wealthy mon.
Ex-Bishop Kennion writes to the Dean of
Adelaide that the diocese ought to thank God
that it has secured such a good diocesan as Mr.
Harmer. The poor S. A. clergy think Mr Harmer
ought to thank God that he has got a billet
worth .£llOO a year and perks.
A devout Melb. sharebroker lately re-
jected a photo, of himself, shown to him m
proof, on the ground that the book he carried in
his hand was so dimly outlined that one could not
easily tell that it was a prayer-book. Lhe
book,” he said, “must be as plainly recognisable
as my face.”
“Cheap Jack” Newton, of gorgeous-
equipage celebrity, well remembered in all the
Australias, is now at Johannesburg, Africa. He
owns the crack mansion of the place, for which he
paid ,£lB,OOO, and is living in most princely style.
He is said to have been simply minting money tor
some years past.
Australia has a Chinese squatter ; it also
had a full-blooded aboriginal squatter. He owned
» small station within 100 miles of Goondiwindi,
on the Macintyre river, and was married to a
white woman, who after his death married a
stockman. Two sons of the dark squatter are
now working on stations in the Dumaresq region.
The creeping years, writes a Bulletin
London correspondent, have, it seems, no influence
on the nice-nastiness of Mr. Stead. The great
author of “If Christ came to Chicago” lies back
daily upon a scented and silk-pillowed couch, in
his Arundel-sb. chambers, and interviews mixed
visitors on the great question of sexual relation-
ship.

JUDGE MOLESWORTH,
of Victoria, who thinks and

more or less subject to the

“Pat” Jennings, M.L.O. (N.S.W.), who
has long been very unwell, is lying in a rather
dangerous state at Petty’s Hotel, Sydney.
The world has, of late, not gone too well with the
erstwhile N.S.W. Premier, news of whose
physical condition will excite sympathy far and
wide, for, in his heyday, he was the most generous
of men.
Michael Davitt, the Irish patriot and demo-
crat, who sailed for Australia the other day,
has only one arm, and is not unlike John
Burns in ruggedness of countenance. Three
times he has seen the inside of prisons
for political offences, and the other day, in
evidence before the Prisons he
described penal servitude in England as “the
most rigorous, most heartless, and most scientifi-
cally refined system of punishment that the
wit of man could devise.” The extraordi-
nary thing about Davitt is the sincerity
of his convictions. During the Parnellite
split he left his Irish chief, and stuck to Glad-
stone, notwithstanding that it was the G.O.M.
who prosecuted him in 1870, and the same man
who sent him back to prison in 1881. Some
of the best years of his life have been spent
in gaol, and instead of nursing bitterness
and planning vengeance he was thinking out
schemes for the benefit of his fellow-country-
men. He professes the doctrines of Henry
George, and speaks with great fluency and
earnestness. More than any other Irishman he
has labored for the advancement and regenera-
tion of the working classes.
“ Speranza ” (Oscar Wilde’s mother) has
generally got the credit for hav-
ing, from the court gallery, con-
tradicted the prosecuting counsel
in the course of Duffy’s trial, when
it was sought to attribute to accused
the authorship of a certain article
which appeared in the Nation. It
was Mrs. Callan, editress of the
Nation during Duffy’s detention,
who actually gave the lie to the
lawyer’s assertion in the words,
“ That is not true. I wrote that
article.”
Prior to “Jack” Want’s appearance in
Dean’s case, “Bob” Wisdom was the last Attorney-
General to prosecute in person at Sydney Criminal
Sessions. The case was one of fire-raising, 15 years
ago. A man cold-bloodedly set fire to premises in
George-st. for the sake of the ss. reward given for
first information! and life was lost in the fire.
Justice Windeyer presided. Dunn got a sentence
of death, afterwards commuted to life. The con-
vict is now in charge of the wash-houses at Dar-
linghurst, and has repeatedly petitioned for a
further commutation without success.
According to ex-Premier Parkes there are
bub seven members of the old combined IN.B.
Wales Parliament now living : Henry Parkes,
Francis Lord, Charles Nicholson, Augustus
Morris. W. P. Faithful!, Saul Samuel, and Daniel
Cooper. And of these the youngest and most
energetic is Mr. Morris, now a N.S.W. official
assignee.
William Milne, ex-President of the 8. A.
Legislative Council, began his life in the province
in 1839 as a barefooted youth tailing cows in the
district which afterwards returned him to Parlia-
ment. He ran away with his employer s daughter
and married her, his golden wedding having been
celebrated three years ago. He was nob a bril-
liant man. but he was honest. He was reputedly
wealthy. , . . . ,
The death of that accomplished chemist
and metallurgist, Cosmo Newbery, is distressing.
Only 52 years old, he might have been fairly
booked for another 20 years’ work, but for the
Windsor railway smash which first discredited
Speight. Newbery invited his friend Dunn, a
clever professional rival, out to dinner, and they
embarked in the luckless train, which nearly killed
them both.
Justice Windeyer’s father was a leading
Liberal in his day, and was regarded as an
obnoxious Radical by many of his contemporaries.
During his canvas for a seat on the elective branch
of N.S.W. Legislative Council he was, though he
eventually won, bitterly denounced as a man of
dangerous and unconventional opinions. His
obstinacy in resisting a Government tax on one
occasion led to a sheriff’s execution on his domestic
property. .
General Hutton is refreshingly undiplo-
matic and natural. At a military inspection
soon after his arrival in N.S.W. he spoke a few
words of encouragement to the men, whereupon
the local field-marshal, probably a warlike and
aldermanic garter - merchant or sock-vendor,
thought he would congratulate the men too, and
straightway proceeded to do so. The Major-
General stood it with difficulty and raised his
eyebrows very high, but when another held-
marshal or general started to wag his jaw, and it
became evident that all hands, right down to the
little drummer-boy, were going to blather, then
the General arose and spoke in his wrath. He
said—but no matter.
There is one Australian Judge who is
rarely crushed by counsel, yet it does happen
sometimes. In a recent unsavory case the prisoner
was defended by Mr. 0., a barrister apparently
somewhat obnoxious to his Honor, and when that
learned counsel asked a question of which the
Judge did not approve, his Honor first of.all
affected an air of extreme amazement and then
spoke majestically, and in a very loud voice . i
had a long experience myself both at defending
prisoners and prosecuting, but I would never have
asked such a question as that.” “ Ah,’ replied Mr.
0 “other people besides your Honor have had
experience.” Then his Honor, finding that he could
not have his own way, became angry and undigni-
fied : “I tremble for prisoners,” he snapped, with
pointed emphasis, “ when I see them defended by
some counsel.” Whereupon the rude Mr. 0.,
fixing his Honor with a meaning look : ' 1 also
tremble for prisoners, your Honor —but for very
different reasons.” Then the learned Judge sank
back in silence, but he bore his reverse badly, and
in summing up alluded in lachrymose tones and
plaintive snorts to the way in which he had been
insulted when he offered a “most friendly sugges-
tion to counsel.”

Quisoolia, the Kanaka chief who figured
prominently in the recent William Manson cases,
has an island off the Malayta coast, with a village
fortified by coral rooks and wire-rope. irom
there he leads expeditions to the mainland in
quest of “recruits,” whom he fattens and hands
over to the Queensland labor vessels. He isbft.
high, with a grey head of towy hair a great old
cannibal warrior.
Antarctic explorer Borchgrevink tells of a
scientific magnate who tried hard to trip him up
at a meeting of the Melbourne learned, by asking
him conundrums. “ Hid you encounter insect-
life throughout your voyage ?” “ Oh, yes ! the
magnate was triumphant. “ I always understood
that no insect-life existed so far South? Well,
answered Borchgrevink, “ the unfortunate busi-
ness was that we had taken the troublesome
creatures with us.”
Bernard Hall’s picture of the late Vic.
Chief Justice (painted from photos, under great
disadvantages) has been aptly described by a man
about the courts as being neither one of ‘ the
Chief ” nor of “ George Higinbotham. The
statuesqueness of the great democrat is entirely
missing, and there is nothing to suggest that
wonderful mingling of strength and “sweetness
which charmed everyone who knew him. The
picture is stowed away at present in an upper
room of the Court Library, and it is to hoped
that it will remain there. “It’s not him ! ”
J. B. O’Hara, a Melb. University poet,
has a commission to supply a London firm with
material for an Australian birthday - album.
Someone should provide him with a spray
wattle-blossom and the carcase of a dead hush-
man. Apropos of which, Reginald Roe, head-
master of Brisbane Grammar school, joins
in saying “ there is no such thing a*
Australian poetry —it doesn’t exist, in fact.
Which is mere hypercritical hairsplitting, justified
by arguments which would prove also that there
is no American poetry, or no English poetry, or
no poetry at all.
Gregory Blaxlaud, whose exploring prowess
Mount Victorians—Vhforians, it should be—pro-
pose to honor with an obelisk, merits remembrance
for other and more far-reaching achievements.
He was the first to make Australian wines known
in England, obtaining a gold Ceres medal for
samples he submitted there in 1828 ; and he was
also the first in N.S. Wales to systematically
cultivate oaten hay. A Sydney daily last week
confounded Gregory with John Blaxland, whose
statue is found just inside the door of the local
Art Gallery, and who figured for many years as a
self-willed and independent member of N.S.W.
Legislative Council.
Capt. JOHNSON, of the warship Ringarooma
Adelaide believes that it has been left to it
to provide the Australian Rosa Bouheur, in the
person of Mrs. Lermitte, the clever wife of a
leading local medico. The lady quite suddenly
disclosed a talent for painting animals, and an
experienced sportsman said of her pictures of two
of his favorite horses: “She seems to have gob
under the skin, and to have found out all about
their anatomy.’’ Mrs. Lermitte appears to have
painted an excellent picture of Carbine (which
was bought at once fora good round sum); and she
has also most satisfactorily depicted the two
hunters ridden at every meet during the last
season by Godfrey and Miss Blanche Watson, son
and daughter of the veteran starter —Lord and
Lady Hopetoun’stwo inseparable hunting cronies.
It was to these two that the ex-Governor and his
wife made parting gifts of their own favorite
horses, Snowball and Goldtop, in token that they
would never ride with more companionable
friends.

Michael Davifcfc honestly says he is coming
to Australia to make money. He lost all he had
on the Labor World, the paper he started in
London, but which soon followed in the wake of
nearly all exclusively labor organs, and died. The
workman does not care for purely labor papers.
He prefers a paper that is of general interest,
whilst being sympathetic to his own cause.
