POLITICAL
POINTS
Victorian: “ I back Trenwith' at odds,
for Premier, within five years.”
Rumored that the Vic. railway and lunacy
departments are to be amalgamated.
In the N.S.W. member’s creed:- —“I
believe in the resurrection of the roads and bridges
member.”
Someone ought to write James M‘Cul-
loch’s biography, with the real Higinbotham as a
set-off to Morris’s wish-wash.
Of N. S. W. Parliamentarily-pledged Labor
party, all are members of some trades-union, ex-
cept Law, Loughnane, Kirkpatrick and Smailes.
A loudly-heralded Freetrade meeting, held
at Echuca, Vic., the other night, resulted in seven
orators and an audience of one. It was the most
unanimous meeting on record.
The puzzle is how Service came to be so
entirely out of the disputes in the Higinbotham-
cwm-M‘Culloch era. This gave him such a splen-
did free hand when he re-entered politics.
Q. Colonial Secretary George Washington
Tozer declared, up North, that there were only 11
unemployed in Brisbane. Next day the official
bureau returned the number as 640. The official
bureau is a gentleman.
Jenkin Coles, the wealthy S.A. Speaker,
is leading a National Ass campaign against free
education, payment of members, and direct land
taxation. Years ago, when the now knighted
Jinks was a policeman, he was a violent democrat.
Ex-Premier Patterson comes out of his
shell a bit, dodging round Chambers of Com-
merce, and those places, with tentative speeches.
Patterson is small, distinctly small. His koo-toos
to Turner have been too thin. He is a sort of
converted wolf.
Most desperate underground efforts were
made to have the Dean poisoning case inquired
into under the 383rd section of the N.S.W. Crimi-
nal Law Amendment Act, instead of by Com-
mission. But the public was emphatic, and the
legal straw-splitters went down.
An attempt was made to amend Griffith’s
motion—rejected by 23 votes to 21—against the
admission of aliens to N.S.W. public service, by
substituting “bacchanalians” for aliens, thus
showing the genial influence of Oliver Wendell
Holmes on the political desert.
After wasting over 200 words in a telegram
to S.A. Premier Kingston, in historical recapitu-
lation, comment, explanation and recrimination,
Premier Turner (Vic.) concluded: “I deem it
best to allow you to have the last word, and there-
fore simply acknowledge your letter.” A superb
bull!
Premier Turner, snorting over some Argus
reflections re his connection with the defendants
in the “ big gun case,” said, in his anger, that he
would rather have thrown over the Premiership
than have deserted his clients. Which is quite as
much justification as the Argus could possibly
wish for.
Wanted, a democratic member of every
Australasian Legislative Assembly to move the
following resolutions : “ (1) That, in view of the
intelligence that the British Premier intends to
introduce in the Commons a bill permitting
‘ colonial ’ Judges to sit upon the Judicial Com-
mittee of the Privy Council, this House resolves
that, so far as the province of Blank is concerned,
the proposal is unnecessary and unwise. (2) That
the Governor be requested to communicate the
foregoing resolution to the British Government.”
It is better, instead of waiting till the Jingo plot
is mature, to stifle it with firm fingers now.
The ironworkers of Mary-
borough (Q.) wished the Boodle-
wraith to compel all persons
erecting mills under the Sugar,
Works Guarantee Act to get
their machinery made in Q.
The Govt, is not willing, any-
how. The ironworkers logically
urge that they have as much right to
protection as the planters.
An Argus cable reports that the Times
“ compliments Mr. Howard Willoughby upon Ins
article dealing with the coming failure of State
socialism,” recently published in the Argus.
Howard Willoughby is a conspicuous ARGUS-man
—and isn’t he Melbourne correspondent for the
Times?
The defeat of N.S.W. Local Government
Bill gives Premier Reid an opportunity, which
some of his friends urge him to take, of so greasing
the constituencies with giftsof “roads and bridges”
that, in the first place, he will demoralise his
political enemies; and, in the second, arouse
public indignation against those who voted against
the Bill—even such as it was.
The Vic. Railway Commissioners con-
sulted Crown Solicitor Guinness as to whether
a prosecution would lie against an ex-M.P. who
was alleged to have illegally travelled on a mem-
ber’s pass. Gist of answer : “No, unless the ex-
member travelled on the pass with intent to
defraud.” Presumably the intent to defraud is
just as obvious as that of the man who travels on
r tqbacco-tag.
Gov, M‘Gregor asserts that the New
Guinea natives are not to be dispossessed of their
lands, and yet asseverates that they will prove
very useful to the European planter. Surely the
Gov. must know that a native settled on his tribal
lands will work regularly for nobody. By the
way, Minister Byrnes asserted the other day at
Mackay that the Queensland planters still look to
New Guinea for labor.
The quidnuncs say Premier Turner will
resign after next session, but nobody has the
faintest idea that he will be thrust out, after
financing the province so ably. He begins to see
that when he has squared off things he can retire,
make his pile in business, and come again when
he likes, as the man who saved the nation. But
Sir George and Lady Turner must come first.
S.A. returning-officers refuse to register
women as voters for the Leg. Council on the
strength of a claim as “joint tenants” or “joint
owners ” with their husbands or brothers of pro-
perty of sufficient annual, or fee-simple, value to
qualify both. Yet two men under similar circum-
stances would be registered without demur, and
the Act says that women shall receive the vote
on exactly the same terms as men.
Near Penola (S.A.) over 60 families make
good livings on land owned by Squatter Riddoch,
M. which five years ago was virgin soil,
looked after by one boundary-rider. Mr. Rid-
doch, fearing land taxation, had his run sub-
divided. The land consists of beautiful volcanic
soil, and was originally sold by S.A. Govt, for
10s. an acre—another illustration of the folly of
the State parting with the fee-simple.
‘ ‘ How do the Maori women use the fran-
chise?” asked Fitzgerald, ex-M.L.A. (N.S.W.), of
Hone Heke, M.H.R., the other day. “Splen-
didly,” said Heke ; “ every one of them votes.”
“For any candidate they choose?” “Oh, no;
just for whoever their chief nominates.” The
whole of Ngapuhi (Heke’s tribe) womanhood voted
solidly for him—but then he is very young, very
handsome, very clever, and very unmarried.
The removal from the Bench of an Aus-
tralian Supreme Court Judge is difficult, but not
the impossible thing which some people suppose.
Justice Willis, who opened the first Court in
Bourke-street, Melbourne, and whose rank was
that of second Puisne Judge, was complained
against as not possessing the confidence of the
public, his petulance being the chief objection, and
was, after investigation, formally removed from
the Bench. Though present conditions differ, the
precedent is valuable as showing that, when the
public has a will, it can always make a way.
The fine of £2O, lately imposed on the
Queanbeyan gaoler, for neglecting to supply
the Circuit Court with a full list of prisoners in
his charge, was on appeal confirmed by the Full
Court, but has now been, by an unexplained con-
junction of influences, wherein Lieut.-Gov. Darley
and O’Sullivan, M.P., take the lead, remitted.
N. Wales now possess so many different
kinds of legal tribunals—lay, ecclesiastical, and
military, with such varying and mystifying pro-
cedures, all topped by a star-chamber finale—that
it is impossible to say where any trial will
definitely end.

Sydney Telegraph : —“ Many Maclean
(N.S.W.) Freetraders are assisting in the move-
ment to oppose the abolition of the sugar-duty.”
Of course they are. The Maclean Freetrader
lives, directly or indirectly, on the sugar-industry,
and every Freetrader wants Protection for his own
industry. The only difference between him and
the Protectionist is that he doesn’t want protec-
tion for anybody else’s industry. To state that
the Maclean Freetrader wants the sugar-duty
retained is just as needless as to mention that the
Freetrade Telegraph, though it objects to subsi-
dising local industries in general, approves of
being subsidised itself by free postage.

Rosebery intends to convene another
Colonial “silken-bond” Conference in London
this year. The most recalcitrant Republican at
the Antipodes becomes quite tractable when
gently tickled at Windsor and battered at
Sandringham. The last big Conference was
that of ’87, which ruined Griffith ; and there
are at least two big men now needing to be
similarly bulldozed — Seddon and Kingston.
Wobbler Reid is of no account —his price can be
paid at any time if he lasts long enough to make
it worth while; but the others are more uncer-
tain. By all means, then, another “Conference,”
and let the Jingo spider smear her flies before
devouring them.

Under the N.S. W. Bankruptcy-act Amendment
Bill the Attorney-General will have power in certain cases
to make insolvent those imprisoned for debt who, finding
themselves comfortable in gaol, refuse to obtain their
freedom by voluntarily petitioning in bankruptcy.

A big mining sal© may be invalidated owing to
the quorum of the vending co.’s directors including an
individual who was, it is averred, illegally on the board.
The vending co.’s articles of association stipulate that a
director becomes ineligible if he becomes “ bankrupt or
compromises with his creditors.” This director is said
to have privately compromised, by arrangement, soma
time afo. There are other big transactions for other
companies, on which he has been a leading spirit since his
compromise, that could be challenged on aim bar grounds.
Thera was an apoplectic bailiff in possession of
the lower floor premises of a certain insurance co., the
other day. About 50 times a day he climbed up stairs to
get fresh air, but lie did so at such irregular intervals
that the clerks in the offices of the building that
f&es the insurance co. were engaged nearly all day on
time wafers as to the exact moment the hummer would
come up. One shrewd clerk, who endeavored to square
the bummer to arrive at preconcerted moments, lost over
J3lO in wagers, owing to the bailiff having a fit—possibly
on account of the co. paying him out.

The wholesale drug trade of Australia will very
shortly have an opponent less to compete against; but,
strangely enough, nobody will regret the collapse more
than the firm’s rivals, as the opening thus left is sure to
be filled by much more powerful opposition.

Lately judgment for a large amount was given
against a firm who, ere the successful plaintiffs could
reach out for their pound of flesh, sold their business and
transferred certain other properties. It looks as though
plaintiffs will get nothing, as defendants can go insolvent,
and set up that, although they sold all their estate for a
very trifle, that was fheir loss and their business. Also,
that the rather trifling sums they had sold very valuable
assets tor were all expended in feeding the lawyers who
defended them. ...

Hint to traders. Ere your travellers open up
with a suburban or town grocer who holds a wine-and-
spirit license, see that they report the brands of liquors
on sale. In nine cases out of ten this will give the key
as to who is actually running the show.

In the United Kingdom, at the end of 1893,
423 gas-works were owned by companies and 192 by
municipalities. Municipal ownership « steadily in-
creasing ami is an all-round success. In 1888 (the last
vear for which figures are available) municipalities
charged, on the average, 2s. lid. per 1000 feet against
Hs 4d4 charged by private enterprise ; they worked more
economically! their exp-nses being 2s. Id. for every 1000
feet manufactured against 2s. 3d. by private enterprise,
and they produced £1,400,000 profit, which went to relieve
the local rates. Yet the Australian Fat Man paper keeps
on rising to state the old, old lie that municipal enter-
prise has been a failure wherever attempted, and that the
bloated monopoly is the only true road to happiness.
Rumored in Melb. that the local big retail
drapery houses will shortly dispense with the Saturday
half-holiday and keep open until 6 p.m. If this happens
an extension to 10.30 p.m. will quickly follow.

Harcourt’s British Budget shows a surplus of
£750,000, which would be a big thing in Australia, but
doesn’t seem much in an income of 94J millions. The 6d.
per gal. beer-tax is to be reimposed to meet an expected
deficit of £319,000, and a decreasing consumption of
whisky will be checked, if possible, by a 6d. per gallon
duty reduction. t ,
F : “A phrase that is very popular with com-
batants in the currency tangle is “demonetisation of
silver.” It is used constantly, and with an emphasis that
is very convincing. But the statement that silver was
demonetised in 1873, or about that time, is altogether
misleading. Not an American dollar, or an Indian rupee,
or a French franc, has been demonetised. There is more
silver legal-tender money in the world than ever before.
I am reminded of the immoral use of this phrase by bear-
in'' the S.A. Treasurer in Melb. lecture to the unemployed
on how to increase the price of the necessaries of life.
* * ♦ •
The oableman alleges that
have a gold currency. The Czar coined £477,519 in gold
imperials and half-imperials during ’93-an inconsiderable
quantity. The cable news means either that he , lB *°
strike a new coin to his own glory, to restore the depre-
ciat. d paper rouble to par, to adopt gQ'd mcnMieta sm
or, more likely still, he is going to do nothing more in the
money line than spend the taxes. Russia has a State
hoard of about £100,009,000 sterling in gold, which is
generally reckoned as lost to the world’s currency.
Numerous wild guesses have been made at the reason for
this vast hoard. It is either a war fund or a fund to re-
establish the currency, say the knowing ones. But hard
cash can be used at a moment’s notice for the purpose of
the moment, and the Czars have mostly known enough to
keep money about them. Russia may be deciding for
gold monometallism, as Germany did 22 years ago, and
Austria, Chili, Afghanistan (!), and Japan have done
since. If Russia does this, the bi-metallists will have
something new to say, or will be expected by the other
side to say something new. In the meantime, Russia’s
population worries along with a paper rouble depreciated
about 30 per cent., the alleged money unit being the
silver rouble.

* • * *
How often is a distraint-warrant signed after it
is filled up? Perhaps in about one case in 20. And yet
on this ground a distraint could be declared void. A
warrant is supposedly signed on the date of issue; but it
is customary for many landlords to always leave half-a-
dozen blank (signed) distraint-forms in their agent’s
hands.

♦ * * „
Smith bought a milkman’s business, and, on
attempting to serve the customers, found that the vendor,
on a rival’s cart, had supplied them all an hour before.
By the time an injunction operates to remove vendor
from the rival's cart, the rival’s carter will know the
round, and the customers refuse to have anything to do
with a row where there is nothing to induce interest.

One of the most benevolent-looking old gentle-

prises. The burglar in want of an outfit obtains the
needful from the childlike and bland-smiling philanthro-
pist Also, three-card performers, schlenter bookmakers,
and others of the genus, all apply to him in their time of
financial need. Although the “bankers clients are
amongst the very worst of the local magsmen, he says
he has only lost £l5 in bad debts all the years he has
been at the game.

Westralia breeds suspicion prolifically. The
legend about the nine men who made a big find in the
Far North-West, and thereafter had their meals and camp
apart to avoid murder by poison, is an illustration to the
point. No man on that side willingly confesses to having
found gold. It is naught, it is naught, saith the digger,
but when he is gone he boasteth. Once home again the
digger boasts of the high quality of his gold. But
Westralian alluvial gold is only fair quality. The frequent
digger-story that the banks gave him £4 2s. fid. an ounce
is pure fiction. There are no reliable statistics published
of the value of gold on the various fields—but The Bul-
letin has some figures before it, obtained from a reliable
source, concerning gold from various fields.
It must be premised that the figures do nob
relate to gold in the rough. The characteristic red earth
that clings to the alluvial gold out yonder can only be
economically taken out in the melting-pot. The assayer
nuts the parcel, with the usual flux, into his crucible and
melts it. When the result has been poured and has cooled
it is assayed. This gives the value of the gold in a cleaned
state. The parcels “lose” variously, up to 5 per cent.
The “ loss ” is generally less in winter, or when water is
used. &0., &c., so the quotation of values gross would be
misleading. Here are the values (after melting) of cer-
tain parcels of gold actually assayed
Coolgardie, £3 19s 9d, £3 17s lid, £3 18s 6d
Coolgardie (40 miles north), £3 17s 2d
Hannan’s, £3 18s 5d
I O U, £3 15s lOd
Kurnalpi, £3 16s Id
Murchison, £3 19s 7d, £3 18s 9d, £3 18s lOd
White Feather, £3 17s Id, £3 16s fid, i 3 17s 7d
It must be remembered that the amount realised per oz.
on the digger’s parcel would be Is. to 3s. fid. less, accord-
ing to cleanness.

One grievous burden of Australian mining is
the cost of dynamite. It is made for 4d. a pound in Ger-
many and other parts of Europe, as well as in America,
where it is retailed at fid. ; but in Australia, where the
price is controlled by a convention, it varies only between
Is 8d and Is. lOd. This is an outrage. The consump-
tion in Australia must he at least 2000 tons a year which,
in round numbers, costs a quarter-million sterling too
much. , , * »
The veteran Lansell is preparing to sink the 180
shaft at Bendigo to 4000 feet-only awaiting a trial
of his new idea of lifting water bv compressed air, taking
the weight of the water off the valves every few hundred
feet Success is doubted by many practical miners, but
T.anaell is giving it great attention and, although he
never goes below, he knows all details and spends much
time at the mouth of the shaft.

Time and tonnage tell on small gold-reefs. The
West Volunteer (Tas.) shares have backed from 60s. to 30s.
Too late for many people, it was found they struck the
reef in one of the rich shoots. That noh 10-oz. crushing
could not be kept up, and now the mine wants a new
shaft, a new lift and more backs. Blessed are they who
know enough to sell at the top. A good mine, neverthe-
less, when the new manager gets ahead of his work.
The Volunteer, too, has nob enough ground
opened up to blow about, and proposes to take out some
crushings by means of an “ intermediate, dragging it
out per windlass rather than wait until the levels are
pushed ahead. In every other respect the mine is well
handled. ,

Dear Bulletin,—Re Tasmania G.M. ; your last
figures published are 0.K., but they are misleading. The
mine—ask “Billy” Jones-is better than she looks
in vour column. She is a really magnificent property,
but is “religiously” mismanaged, as is locally
well-known. Several years’ tailings from her batteries lie
distributed over a wide area in an arm of the River
Tamar, where they are so mixed with slurry as
not to be payable. The directors bless their
innocent little hearts-are now erecting a Luhng,
and patching up their old battery where neigh-
boring stamps once fell together as best they could,
the cams not having been properly adjusted. Beacons-
fleld rock is softer than Bendigo stone, yet a Bendigo
stamp crushes a third more than its Beaconsfleld-brother
in an equal time. There has been much waste of
time all through the mine-not. mind you,
that any one of the directors has ever been co f , ’ s^'°'J i 9 '7
extravagant to the extent of a sou. Heaven forefend!
The mine was bought for a song, and has been more or
less mismanaged to some tune almost ever since—prob-
aldv a hymn-tune. Fact is, some honest and well-inten-
tioned and even clever people know more about a gospel-
mill than a gold-mill. —Theta.

Leslie Norman, Coolgarrlie, writes
that Tub Bulletin “ occasionally does that Held an injus-
tice says there are 20 mines within a radius of 15 miles
the success of which may be regarded as certain. Bur-
bank’s, according to this authority, is a “ tidy little pro-
perty of great promise.” Tindall’s is one of the absolute

should turn up trumps under better management. The
latter statement quite upsets Leslie Norman. The mine

the writer, having seen the show, knows it to be a duffer,

Bur<>-ess mine?” The Bulletin is beginning to very
much doubt it. Like hundreds of Short-lived mines, it

* * * *
Marks, now in London reporting on Coolgardie
mines, has had persistent hard luck in gold-mining.
Israel generally has—how is it? He succeeded in winning
what was considered a valuable lease from George Lansell
on Bendigo, a few years ago. It proved worthless. At
Coolgardie he was one of the employes of the London-
derry owners, and was laid down with fever just when it
began to dawn upon him from their conduct that they
had something remarkably rich about the lease. The
dolly was at work about this time. After he came out of
the hospital its richness was disclosed. He arranged to
peg the West Block at daylight, and found that young
Carter and Dawson did not wait even for daylight. He
lost the chance of making thousands by waiting an hour.
Poor Marks ! Why is the mozzle of the tribes so per-
sistently “out” in gold-delving?

One of the most reliable of Australian mining

* * * *
How a well-seasoned Westralian sold his mine.
He first banked lOOoz. ; after a few weeks drew it out,
added 500 z., and banked the lot next day—each time
taking a receipt for the amount of gold which was only
left for safe-keeping, saying he intended leaving the held.
These tactics he pursued until his receipts represented
a good total, and a big bag of specimens was always handy
at the mine. In the end, a big cash sale.
• ♦ * •
Warden Wilshire, in granting an exemption
application to the Spa Hydraulic Mining Co., Shoalhaven,
said he had been pestered by legislators not to do so,
but he was convinced the field was useless for any other
system of mining. So far, he might have added that
hydraulic co.’s have made nothing out of it. The Spa
Co. to wit, have spent £50,000 and never paid a div.

Criticising “ Mortgaged Years,” an alleged
Australian story, by R. K. Dee, that weekly

find his plots in the police-reports of the Sydney

News has been received by the Californian
mail that such leading men of California as Alan
Gardner, Esq., M.D., of Santa Barbara; L. M.
D. Davies, Esq., M.D., Portland, Oregon; J. J.
Gingles, Esq.. M.D.. Portland, Oregon; W. P.
Bell Esq, M.D., San Francisco; P. Crowley,
Esq’, Chief of Police, San Francisco; have
unanimously given testimony of the toilet re-
quisite “Amykos,” which, besides all its good
qualities, is also an excellent preparation against
all the bad influences to which we are always ex-
posed during the cold and moist winter months.
Oscar Wilde’s trial resulted in a disagree-
ment of the jury, and he will be tried anew on 20th
May. Meantime, he is set free on an aggregate bail
of ,£5OOO. It is worth noting that British justice is
still, in some respects, superior to much of the
Australian variety. Wilde’s Judge did not hesi-
tate to remind the jury that the evidence against
him, after all, was only that of hlackmailers-and
how much was a blackmailer s oath worth . Of late
in Australia we have had a Judge who, instead of
laving stress on the fact that one of the witnesses
against a prisoner capitally arraigned was of note-
riously immoral character, seemed to rather seek
to minimise the fact, and seemed to grieve that
the prisoner’s counsel had the bad taste to refer to
such matters. Likewise, his Honor went out of
his way to discount the unquestionably good char-
acter enjoyed by the accused.
Some samples of alleged Vic. Govfc.-
stamped butter, supposed to be pure, have been
found, on analysis in England, to contain from 14
to 17 per cent, of foreign fat. This, however, was
no doubt added in England, where there are manu-
factories which turn out as much as 50 tons of
margarine apiece da.ly—the materials being beef
suet, milk, and nut oil. English purchasers of
Australian fowls complain, too, that a number of
alleged Vic. “ chickens ” were merely tough old
birds with the spurs cut off. And only the other
day a Tasmanian fruit-exporting association had
to complain of one of its members packing off
miserably small apples, to the detriment o the
general trade.
The Earl of Pembroke, who only the other
day wrote the preface of Louis Becke’s “ By Reef
and Palm,” is dead, age.! 45. He was the joint;
authorof that charming book, “South Seaßubbles,
and took high rank in Europe as a naturalist.
The Ear), who was extremely rich and good-
natured, had for some time been very ill,
apparently with a gastritic trouble; but in a
recent letter to Sydney he hoped for better things
than so sudden an ending.
THE HISTORY OF A SCAR.
On the bank if my right hand —just about the
middle of it—there is a small scar, half as big as
a threepenny piece perhaps. You would never
notice it unless I showed it to you, and even then
you would have to look sharp to see it. But it s
there, all the same, and will be until t e hand is
returned to dust. It dates back forty years, that
scar does. Going home from school one day
another boy and I quarrelled and fought. Strange
to say, it wasn’t about a girl either. Anyway he
drew his jack-knife and stabbed me in the back ot
the rig n t hand. The wound, I remember, was
slow to heal. It was sore and inflamed for month?,
and hardly a day passed but something hit it, or I
knocked it against something, and so made it
worse. My whole available body appeared to be
concentrated in that sore. You know how such
things are. They are like boils, and if th-reis any
comfortable spot to have a boil there are loads of
money waiting for the fellow who discovers it.
Lately we have received two letters, both con-
taining an identical expression, namely, this:
“ Every thing seemed a trouble to me." Now, none
of us are so tough as to be proof against trouble,
but when everything is a trouble to a man the very
heart inside of him must be tender and touchy.
And, mercy knows, it is so often enough. Grief
will do it, worry will do it, and illness too.
One of these letters, or, at least, the writer of it,
goes on to say;—“lt was in March, 1880, that I
felt tired and languid, and without my usual
energy. Before that I had always been strong
and active. Now I was low-spirited and melan-
choly ; everything seemed a trouble to me. At
first I had a bad taste in the mouth, a poor
appetite, and all I ate gave me great pain in the
chest and sides. Even fish and poultry gave me
as much distress as more solid _ food. I was
constantly spitting up a sour, acid fluid which
caused a miserable feeling in my throat and
mouth. , ,
“After I had been for some time in this con-
dition I was attacked with gout in ray hands and
feet, which confined me to the house now and
again for a week or two at a time. The parts
became inflamed and swollen, and gave me excru-
ciating agony. I could not bear anything to touch
them. For four years I was subject to these
attacks. The doctor who attended me was able
to ease me temporarily, but I was soon as bad as
ever. _ , ,
“ In the middle of April, 1888, I read that cases
like mine had been cured by Mother Seigel s Cura-
tive Syrup, and I thought I would try it. I hadn t
taken more than half a bottle when I felt relieved.
This encouraged me to keep on with the Syrup,
and in a comparatively short time all symptoms of
the disease left me. Since then I have enjoyed
excellent health, and whenever I feel a twinge of
my old enemy a dose or two of Mother Seigel
soon sets me right.—Yours truly, (Signed) M.
Leahy, 49 Grosvenor Terrace, Grosvenor Park,
Camberwell, London, September 22nd, 1892.’’
So much for the facts as Mr. Leahy felt them.
What has science to offer in explanation of them ?
This. Gout, rheumatism, and biliousness are three
complaints arising indirectly from an overworked
liver, or, more properly, from indigestion and
dyspepsia. The poisons so engendered may lie
hidden and unfelt for a long time, and then be
suddenly rendered active by mental worry,
exposure, over eating, wrong eating, or any of a
dozen other causes. The kidneys fail (following
the stomach and liver), the acid poison remains in
the blood and sets up inflammation in the joints,
and the retained fluids produce dropsy. All sorts
of disturbances are apt to go this condition
of things, every one less a disease in itself than a
symptom of the one cause— indigestion and
dyspepsia. The heart and lungs are often attacked
in sympathy. Cure the torpid digestion, and an
all round improvement at once succeeds. To do
this is in the power of Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup—as, perhaps, in the power of nothing else.
It was most unfortunate that this gentleman
suffered for eight years; and no wonder, mean-
while, that his mind was sensitive as his body to
every approach. Knowing what he now knows he
feels safe. There is no darkness but ignorance, and
the German Nurse shows “The way out.”
London, October, 1892.
J.M.P.

FROST & SHIPHAM, 474 George Street, SYDNEY.
Some of the Sydney nobodies bid, at the
late Govmentouse auction-sale, nearly twice as
much for effects as they cost when new.
Boy newspaper-runners combined, a few
nights ago, to drive girl newspaper-runners off the
Sydney streets, contending that a girl’s proper
sphere is the home and that it demoralises her to
enter into competition with boys. The ‘‘com-
bine,” which lasted about an hour, was supported
by all John Haynes’ and several other arguments
against women’s rights.
An unknown foreigner was run in to the
Sydney Central lock-up the other day, charged
with being drunk. He gabbled some gibberish,
but was cast into a cell. At 3 a.m., something
about him struck the officials as an out-of-the-way
phase of intoxication, and lie was carried off to
Sydney Infirmary. The authorities there declined
to receive him, and he was hawked back to the
cells. At 6.30 a.m., he was in such unmistakeable
extremity that he was taken again to the hospital
and admitted. He died within an hour. And
no inquest was deemed necessary !
A new amalgam has been discovered
which is a wonderful substitute for gold. It con-
sists of 94 parts of copper to six parts of antimony.
The copper is melted and the antimony is added.
Once the two metals are sufficiently fused, a little
magnesium and carbonate of lime are added to in-
crease the density of the material. The product
can be drawn, wrought, and soldered, just like
gold, which it almost exactly resembles when
polished. Even when exposed to the action of
ammoniacal salts or nitrous vapors it preserves its
color. The cost of making it is about a shilling a
pound avoirdupois.

Dear Bulletin, — Re critic Craig's recent
allusion to “ prose tales told in verse.” What are
the best poems in the English language but prose
tales told in verse? Most readers will agree that
a good story dressed in rhyme is like a pretty
woman in a well-made gown—all the better for it.
-E.J.B.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

The Australian explosive Ripp-Lene has
proved a success in India. Mr. Harold Boyd, the
inventor, has now gone to Egypt to clear the
track on the new Syrian railway, and arrange for
placing Ripp-Lene in the tunnel to be cut through
the Nubian mountains as a water-way for the Nile.
It is alleged that ex-Premier Stambouloff,
of Bulgaria, is the only politician who ever rang
up his monarch on the telephone. Stambouloff
was a genius, but he was also a hulking person
with no manners, and his sovereign—who has fifty
pairs of embroidered pants, and several hundred
garish waiseoats, and belongs to the cult which
languishes over the lily—sacked him as a con-
sequence.

The last turn of the rack, in Melb., is the
P.O. bells excruciating “Sweet Marie I Sweet
Marie!”
It is said that what appears to be an elderly
invalid gentleman, driven once a week by a foot-
man in by the back way to a Melb. hotel, is really
a figure filled with illicit spirit.
A burglar lately broke into a Melb.
Chinese store, and got away with what appeared
to be the strong box. On opening it at home, he
found—a complete skeleton! No beans, only
bones !
Melb. matrimonial agencies are evidently
profitable concerns, judging from the fact that
one leading matchmaker has expended .£3OOO in
new premises, gorgeous furniture, &c., for the ac-
commodation of clients.
What the recent local meeting of the Aus-
tralasian Association for the Advancement of
Science did for Brisbane. Young lady, leaving
hall at end of session ; “ Well, I am glad I
attended those science-lectures. I met two of the
most fascinating men there I ever saw in my life.”
At the Melb. Easter Monday sham-fight.
Captain Buggins coming out from behind fence,
waving white handkerchief to enemy: “Will
Captain Jobson oblige with a few hundred rounds
of cartridge ? If not, we must retire.” Cartridges
sent, and, to the amusement of the crowd, the
firing was renewed.
N.S.W. Marine Board’s annual lighthouse
picnic cost last time .£450. So far as can %e
gathered, there were 8 unofficial and unnecessary
guests to 0 unnecessary or official. A special
steamer was engaged, and tilings were done in
style —as they always are. So the province’s
money is muddled away. Three men—an engineer,
a sailor, and an architect—could perform the in-
spection in one of the Board’s own boats at a cost
of ,£2O in addition to their regular salaries as civil
servants. But as the country has to bleed, why
shouldn’t the Marine Board bleed it as well as
others?
An enterprising Newcastle man wanted to
reach Westralia, but hadn’t money enough to
carry him through. He stowed away on a Sydney-
trading collier, and was subsequently gaoled for
three weeks in Darlinghurst. He adopted similar
methods to get right round, and reached Perth
after a. three months’ journey, mainly spent in
working out a fortnight in Melb. gaol and a
month’s durance in the Holy City. Finally he
s rved six weeks in Perth, as an example. Being
s'ill idle, and likely to remain so, the enterprising
Cornstalk occasionally deplores his numerous
transhipments.
Raymond Blathwayt, the English press-
man who has come here to lecture, may be a good
interviewer, but as an interviewee—well, it is
plain he doesn’t yet know his Australia. From
what he poured into the note-book of a Sydney
D.T. man, the other day, Mr. R. B. apparently
imagines that this beastly rubbish-dump is pre-
pared to worship even the mere names of the
British alleged aristocracy. “Lord Beresford is
a personal friend of mine and I often stay at his
place;” “ Some of my introductions are from
Lord Winohelsea ” —or words to that effect. Let
him say, or be reported as saying, anything like
that again, and Australia will earthquake and
chasm right across from Cape York to Cape Otway
in order that it may vomit freely enough to ex-
press its sentiments.

Dear Bulletin, — Be “P. M.’s” assertion
that the vegetable Chow more often than not
buys his goods before he sells them. If the
vegetable Chow’s Australian operations were con-
fined exclusively to the largest cities, “ P. M’s.”
statement would be partially correct, as in
Melbourne, for instance, Chinamen do to some
extent buy in the open market, although the same
market is partly supplied by Chinese. Unfor-
tunately, the Chow’s ramifications are not con-
fined to" the largest cities, but extend throughout the
length and breadth of Australia. In Victoria,
especially, he has taken such a hold upon almost
every country town and village, that his price is
“ the price,” all white rivalry being annihilated.
In country places, yellow men with their co-oper-
ative gardens hold an impregnable position. For
instance, the Chinese of Minjip, a Wimmera
town, supply customers within a radius of 30
miles, hardly a white person within that limit
being able to grow for sale any sort of vegetable
whatever. Before the Chinese advent two white
men were growing vegetables at Minjip, but did
not, and could not, compete with Bung Hi, either
in price or quality. Now, these Chows do not buy
their goods before selling, neither do the Chinamen
in other districts outside of Melbourne. Further,
“P. M.” says the Chinaman “is never cheap.”
I did not say he was, but that he had the ability to
sell cheaply or fix his prices to suit himself. Now,
at Minjip, Ballarat, and scores of other places in
Victoria, the co-operative garden system, which
makes each Chow more or less of a principal, is the
very means to the end of cheap production. Every
man working with a will for Ids own, if not for his
partners’, benefit, saves all middleman waste.
Then the men who grow the vegetables hawk and
sell them, being thus able to sell at “ absolutely
cost price.”—P.B.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Interprovincial Lawn Tennis Tournament
began at Sj dney Cricket Ground last Saturday and was
columned all through the week. Lawn-tennis is not a
game of strength or heroism ; it is not so dangerous as
football, nor so reckless as steeplechasing ; hut it needs
accuracy and quickness of eye, and the nimbleness and
long-windedness of a hare, A lawn-tennis player, too, is
all the more popular for good calves—and just here it is
fitting to remai k that Colquhoun might easily find a buyer
for a shapely pair among the London Gaiety dancers.
The tennis was well-patronised by society, and
many of the local ladies, who shake hands high up in the
air, hoisted their parasols so that the men standing behind
their chairs saw no tennis whatever. Twelve single-handed
matches were played ; Victoria won the first and the last;
N.S. VV. the intervening 10; and the day closed with 27
sets to N.S. \V., and 9 to Victoria. The surprise of the
day was the brilliancy shown by Dewhurst in beating
Green, the crack Victorian ; but undoubtedly Dewhurst,
at his best, is the pick of the present bunch of contestants.
The matches were of a sameness; the Victorians
frequently made the mistake of following a weak service
up to the net, and they lost twice as many points as they
won by repeatedly trying for the volley. The last match
of the day, between the old opponents Webb and Green,
warmed interest into enthusiasm. For Green had been
beaten by Dewhurst, and Webb had won his contest
against Waters; and yet in past matches Green had
invaiiably beaten Webb two sets out of three, and Webb
as invariably had won three sets out of five. And this
was a three-set contest.
The crowd stood three and four deep around
the ropes, and Judge Manning, now happily much
improved in health, presided. Webb played with
his accustomed coolness and adroitness, settling
himself to win the game by his skilful placing of the
ball, and Green responded by flying hither and thither
about the court, springing in and leaping out, and work-
ing himself into a fever-heat, ending in baths of per-
spiration which he frequently mopped up with a towel.
Webb won the first set, and Green the second, and both
men gave exhibitions at times which made even stiff
society matrons applaud. But in the third set Green
re-doubled his efforts and won the rubber rather easily by
six games to two.
• • * *
Australian cyclists look forward with ardour to
the coming of the unbeaten flyer, the famous Zimmerman.
He will be here in three months or so, and then Megson,
the Sydney champion, must set his strong jaw. Megson
is now on an overland trip, vid the back country, from
Sydney to Brisbane. Bennett, who looks after Megson
upon the race-track, does not approve of his man’s trip—
says that delving at a heavy machine along roads and
carrying blney into the bargain, is training a racehorse
by putting him to hard graft in dray-shafts. A big con-
sideration is that Zimmerman has done his wonders upon
concrete and asphalt tracks, and he may, therefore, find
Megson upon a grass track an unusually tough customer.
It was not Beach, but the Parramatta River, that beat
Hanlan—so Hanlan always said.
* » * *
Many of the cricket powers are urging that the
next Australian team for England be composed of teeto-
tallers. “We have had enough of the heavy drinker and
small scorer; we want the small drinker and heavy,
scorer.”
* • • *
The hungry Vic. footballers “ killed the goose.”
Last season they gave no passes out, and sold hogwash at
sixpence a glass, while pubs, opposite were vending it at
3d. per pint. Children with their parents were charged
full price. Yet the very grounds they play on belong to
the public.
• ♦ » ♦
Victorian football will seriously suffer this year
owing to the many other forms of” winter sport now
indulged in. Cross-country running is growing in popu-
larity; as instanced by the recent opening run at Malvern
(Vic.), in which 239 harriers appeared in full costume.
In addition to thfS.the Rugby game is making good head-
way down South, while lacrosse and baseball also have
their partisans.
» » * »
Rugby football is going to have a good time this
year in Melb. An admirer named Nash has presented
the Vic. Union with a 20-guinea cup for competition
during the season, while an offer has been received from
Sydney for a team to visit N.S. Wales next year, the
N.S. W. Union paying all expenses. Needless to say the
offer was accepted, and so the matches will take place in
August next. Besides all which visits are expected from
Maoriland and English teams.
♦ • • •
Essendon’s 8 goals 17 behinds against Richmond
an Saturday eloquently demonstrates the loss of Thur-
good, last year’s champion goal-kicker.
• * * *
There is a glut in the market for football um-
pires. In Adelaide alone there are at least six Melb.
umpires seeking billets at 30s. per match—a fair remu-
neration for a Saturday afternoon’s work. When tie
Australian footballer Icfees his youth, is discarded by his
jlub, and misses his £2 per week expenses, he takes to
umpiring.
• * * *
When are the football associations playing the
Australian game going to legalise professionalism ! At
present every footballer is hypocritically called an
‘‘amateur,” whereas the majority are out-and-out
“pros.,” making a direct living from the game. Cricket
profits, although not nearly so large as football profits, go
in improvements to the plaving grounds, but the enor-
mous “ gates ” taken at football matches seem to be
poured into the bottomless sink of professionalism. Yet
the footballer must not be called anything but an “ ama-
teur.”
» ♦ * »
Sydney footballers are going to have a ground
of their own. As this move is the outcome of a difference
with the Cricket-ground authorities, and as the new site
will be on the old rifle-range, exactly adjacent to the
Cricket-ground itself, the latter’s management will
gnaw its nether lip when the football next door clashes
with cyclists’ and other meetings.
• • * *
A Johannesburg correspondent tells The Bui,-
tiBTIN that “the fight between Burge and Murphy was
the toughest thing since the Heenan-Sayeis match. The
two Jims had been matched for a 35-round glove-fight,
and the day before the event it was said that they had
arranged matters very comfortably. Each accused the
other; dirty linen was washed ad nauseam-, and each
wanted to demolish the other. So one fine, sunny Sunday
morning as wicked and gory a fight took place as has been
seen in this or any other country. *No amphitheatre and
no gloves for me,’were Burge’s sentiments, and ‘Right
you are,’ quoth Murphy. Out. on the veldt, under a
blazing sun, they put in 2hrs. lOmin. working their way
through 35 rounds in a way that would have delighted
Jem Mace. Murphy very nearly went out in the second
round, when the old ’un fell on him with his elbow in Ins
stomach. After that Burge took his gruel with all the
oid-time indifference. Had they not made it a draw, I
believe it would have ended like the Kilkermy-cat episode
—there would have been nothing left of either. Murphy
is now off to England after Valentine’s scalp ; and plenty
of Rand money will be on him, for, rightly or wrongly, the
opinion is pretty general here that he has no equal at his
weight.”
» * * »
Yankee papers tell how Griffo gave the mighty
" Jem " Corbett bis quietus with a right-footer on the
point of the jaw. Griffo having annoyed Corbett in a bar,
Corbett knocked the little ’un down with his open hand,
and then stooped over him, whereupon Griff, landed the
heel of bis boot on the jaw of Mr. Corbett, who fell
back and split his head open against an iron pillar. Griff,
and his party then made tracks.
And, by the way, Griffo is credited with having
the toughest crowd to be found in the States at his back-
* * • »
Steve O’Donnell has again shown, in his eight-
round go with the old-timer Jake Kilrain, (hat his forte
is boxing, not fighting. OD. landed as often as he liked
on K.'s face. At the end of the eight rounds nothing like
a knock-out was recorded. O’Donnell is to meet Peter
Maher on May 15, and the Irishman is just about good
enough to prove conclusively what Steve is made of.
♦ » * •
The inclusion of Dawson (Mercantile R.C.) in
the Vic. interprovincial eight practically amounts to the
Vic. Association throwing down the gauntlet to N.S.W.
A month nr two hack the Sydneyites announced that they
would insist on the strict interpretation of the amateur
rule, evidently having th< ir eye upon Dawson, who
was a “cash amateur” cyclist at the time. Now that
the selectors of the Vies, have refused to listen to the
Northerners’ protest and put Dawson in, everybody is
waiting to see the next move. At any rate, the N.S.
Welshmen are consistent, while the Vies, are acting in
absolute defiance of their own constitution. Both asso-
ciations, at the present, rule that a professional in one
sport is a professional in everv sport, yet the Mel-
bournians, though well aware that Dawson has pedalled
for cash prizes, pretend to accept the cyclists' gag—
“ cash amateur.”
» * * *
Coursing flourishes in Maoriland. Every centre
has its club, and rich stakes are offered for competition.
In N.S.VV. the sport is almost entirely neglected. Among
the 69 dogs left in the Dunedin Challenge Stakes, to be
decided this month, are Vic. and N.S.VV. owned animals.
* * * *
Boats seem to he as great a drug in the market
as racehorses. Golding sold, the other day, one of the
best built 24-footers in Sydney Harbor, in apple-pie
order, for £29. She goes to Newcastle for use at the
oyster-beds.
* • * •
Miller’s good horse. The Indian, won his last
hurdle-race in Victoria on Saturday last. He has been
bought by Uphill, “ the Indian buyer,” who now justifies
his title. Uphill was not to take delivery till after Caul-
field, so that, despite his big weight (12st. 71b.), he was
well backed by his party, and won meritoriously. Cer-
tainly Mr. Sep. Miller made the most of his opportunities
with this horse, who is ab nit the most consistent prad
running. The much-talked-of Captain Webb started
favorite, but didn’t get even a place.
♦ » * *
Mr. Palfrey’s mare, Rita, trotted the final heat
of the Champion Stakes mile, at Christchurch, M.L., in
2.30. It was hoped by lovers of the sport that Sydney
Agricultural Show’s trot would have given the game a
fillip locally, hut nothing has come of it. Pastime, one
of Sydney’s best, has now gone over to M.L., and is not
likely to come back.
♦ * * ♦
“Pony races.” Ye gods ! The winners at last
Moorefield meeting were by Cheviot, Martini-Henry,
Trenton, First King, and Somnus respectively.
♦ * * *
The new watch-tower at Randwick must surely
be in a bad position, as the stewards mounted therein
were, the other day, unable to see what was patent to
pretty well every racing man on the course—that a certain
jockey had all his work cut out to stop his mount from
winning.
* * * *
Eiridspord’s (third prize in the St. Alban’s art
union) dam, Sonsie Queen, is a Musket mare, got by that
great gun before the English breeders knew the value of
the blood. Eiridspord’s sire, Isonomy (sire of the cham-
pion Isinglass) was perhaps the bestmodelled thorough-
bred ever seen in England. It is no wonder Mr. Wilson’s
horse has been a success at the stud—giving us Eirids-
ford, Challenger, Alveary and The Moor in so short a time.
* * * *
The best of the seven M.L, racers now due in
Sydney from Auckland is, of course, St. Hippo, Maori-
land’s crack horse, albeit he»has not raced for a s ason or
two. His three-year-old career was remarkably brilliant,
but when taken to Melb. he was palpably off. A season
at the stud has done him no harm.
* * ♦ »
In the appointment of their new handicapper,
people discerned an inclination on the part of the A.J.C.
to countenance pony-racing. But there has yet been no
active sign of the expected change.
* * » *
Judging by recent events, the cableman will soon
be giving us details of Carbine’s amours.
* * ♦ *
The St. Alban’s people, as The Bulletin hinted,
are scarcely wise in putting Trenton iu a “raffle” when
so big a price could undoubtedly be secured for him in
England. On the turf he was scarcely inferior to
Cirhine. Dan O’Brien, who brought both across-front
MaoriUnd, according to an interviewer, stated that
Trenton was, in his opinion, the better. Trenton has
proved a very successful sire, and the same can hardly be
said of Carbine, so far. The fact that the great Ormonde
begot Orme, and nothing more, is sure to influence
breeders.

The Dairy Exports of N.S.W.
For imported butter, Great Britain pays annually
£13,000,000, of which Sweden and Denmark get
£7,000,000, and Denmark’s share gives her £8 10s.
a year per head of her population.
• * •
N.S.W. is hardly felt in the competition, her
portion of the total last year being but £150,000.
Victoria sends five times as much farui produce to
London as N.S.W. does, yet she beg.m but nine
years ago, whilst the older province exported
frozen mutton in ’7B and frozen
butter in ’B4. South Australia, which
began the year before last, is fast
catching N.S.W. in the competi-
tion. Maoriland is far ahead ; and
Queensland, possessing, as she does,
three times as many cattle, is taking
the lead in frozen beef. N.S.W.
holds her own in mutton ; some
850,000 frozen sheep were exported
last year. Of these, over 500,000
were frozen by the N.S.W. Fresh
Food and Ice Co.
* » •
In addition to their great butter
trade, Denmark and Sweden send to
Great Britain annually pork and
bacon to the value of £2,300,000.
These two countries act in alliance in
bidding for the spoils of the British
market. Their system is scrupulous,
comprehensive, and progressive, con-
trolled and directed scientifically by
wise Governments. They hold dairy
shows, at which either country may
compete, every fortnight throughout
the year, and at the end of the year
the factory securing the highest total
of points is declared the winner;
the prizes amount to £1350. The
experts of the respective Agri-
cultural Departments are the judges
at these shows, and if anything radi-
cally wrong is at any time dis-
covered in a factory’s butter, a Govt,
expert is on the spot atonce to cope
with the evil. Besides this there are
efficiently trained agents stationed over
Great Britain to look after the trade there. And
thus N.S.W., and Australia generally, in sending
butter to the London market, have to compete
with countries whose long-established Government
control ensures the excellence of their exports, and
whose agents are familiar with every phase of the
British market, and every peculiarity of the
British palate. All these portentous facta were
carefully studied and noted by Mr. Pateson, the
active and far-seeing manager of the N.S.W.
Fresh Food and Ice Co., during his recent eight
months’ business tour through Europe.
• * •
“And,” says Mr. Pateson, “N.S.W. has abso-
lutely no system ; her farmers plod on in ignor-
ance of the scientific methods of the day, and her
uon-Legislative Assembly makes no effort to
instruct or help them on this subject of vital
importance. The other provinces will
soon leave her last in the race. There
is a fairly-managed Governmental system
in Victoria, and through that aid the
farmers there are more energetic- more
up to date than those of N.S.W. It is
no use blinking that fact,” Mr. Pateson
is as patriotic as he is vigorous, and he
insists that if a Governmental plan were
instituted on the efficient principle of
the Danes and Swedes the local butter
would soon rival that of those older and
successful countries. “ But we must have
experts, not " inspectors ; ” intellects, not
politicians’ friends and relatives.”
* * *
The Fresh Food and Ice Co., which
was founded by the patriotic T. S. Mort,
will not keep abreast of the times ; it
will lead the way. A new creamery,
on the latest principles, is to be erected
at Grafton at a cost of .£5OOO, and
thence the butter will be exported to
London. At this new creamery, for the
first time in N.S.W., the cream will be
raised in temperature to 180, and then
cooled down to 40 for the purpose of
destroying all possible noxious germs.
* * *
“In dairy produce,” says Mr. Pate-
son, “ the English people will have every-
thing mild and first-class in quality.
They want butter exceptionally good,
with a minimum of salt. Even in the mining
districts they are strict about quality, although
they do not object to a little more salt. A good
deal of execrable stuff has been sent from here to
England, and the senders have lost money upon it
as they deserved to do. When I went to London
I took with me samples of all Australian produce,
and I found that our mild butter of the best
quality, with but one per cent, of salt, was in first-
class condition after being kept ten weeks.
* * *
“And the English people want cheese so mild
that they can eat it by the yard. They will posi-
i\vely not buy strong cheese, and that fact cannot
tie too deeply impressed upon the Australian pro-
ducer. The Bodalla Cheese and Bacon Co. is
making cheese on the Canadian Cheddar principle,
which yields the mildest cheese known, and this
cheese will become very popular on the English
market. This Company is taking a strong lead.
The late T. S, Mort spent =£30,000 upon it before
one cheese was made, and close upon .£200,000 has
been spent upon the estate. But good dividends
are in store.
# * *
“The Fresh Food and Ice Co. is sending
beef mutton, hares, rabbits, butter, cheese, and
honey to England, and it will take the lead now
by sending pork, veal, and poultry. It wi 1 not
pay us to send game. Poultry, like everything
else for the English market, must be the very best:
good young roosters, fattened up to perfection by
artificial means. After being killed, they must
he plucked only, not drawn, before being placed
in the refrigerating chambers ; this method keepr-
the flesh moister.
“When the late T. S. Mort began sending
frozen mutton to England, freights were from 2d.
to 2£d. per lb. ; now they are from three-farthings
to fifteen-sixteenths of a penny. Ido not think
that much success awaits the venture in chilled
meat, but I feel confident that Nelson’s defrosting
process will revolutionise the trade in frozen meat.
After frozen carcases have been thawed hy this
process it is impossible to tell that they have ever
been in the freezing chamber.
• • •
“There should be no prejudices against ‘colo-
nial ’ products in England : our beef is among the
best they get, and our mutton is excellent. But
in dairy produce we are woefully behind the pre-
ST. ALBAN’S ESTATE (Geelong). View of front garden from house.
Photo. by Talma.
sent day, and the N.S.W. people and Government
will have to wake up thoroughly and work un-

then a splendid success will reward us,”
The St. Albans Estate, situated within
about three miles of Geelong (Vic.), is by far the
most charming country 7 property in the Southern
province, and is more suggestive of the wealth,
taste, and leisure which enable the privileged
classes of Great Britain to make even the desert
smile and the wilderness blossom like the rose,
than of anything characteristically Australian. A
couple of illustrations showing the house and a
view taken in the garden appear in this issue.
The house, a single-storied brick building, concedes
everything to comfort, and little or nothing to
vanity. But while it is absolutely unpretentious
it forms a very fitting centre to the spacious and
admirably arranged grounds which surround it, and
is, in fine, a gentleman’s residence perfect in all its
ST. ALBAN'S ESTATE (Geelong). Front view of house.
Photo. by Talma.
appointments, and adequate in every respect to
the requirements of modern life. During the
hospitable regime of the present owner (Mr.
W. R. Wilson), St. Alban’s has fre piently
entertained large parties of guests, and the
housekeeper is now and again disconcerted by the
sudden arrival of 20 or 30 of Mr. Wilson s
friends for a few days’ stay. The estate itself
consists of 842 acres, divided into 30 paddocks,
separated by growing hedge fences. The land is
extremely fertile and sound, nearly all the pad-
docks being laid down in English grasses. The
Barwon River (2 chains wide and 20 feet deep)
intersects the property, thus ensuring a never-fail-
ing supply of fresh water, for irrigation and domestic
purposes. The system for the former is perfect,
the drains being most extensive ; and the steam
pumping plant has a capacity of 120,000 gallons
per hour. The rainfall is, however, so good, that
it is rarely necessary to irrigate more than two or
three paddocks during a summer. The plantations
and hedges, of which several miles exist, add to the
usefulness and beauty of the estate in a marked de-
gree. As a breeding and racing establishment it is
Questionable if any more complete place exists in
Europe; certainty it has no rival in Australia.
Originally selected and laid out by Mr. James
Wilson, one of the keenest Australian judges,
money has continued to be freely emended onim-
proving and perfecting it by Mr. W. R. Wilson,
its present owner, so that it is now one of the sn
places of Victoria. The property as f ' vho '®’
represents a capital investment of £U)S,wu. pi.
Alban’s is being offered to the public oni the dis-
tribution system by Mr, George Adams ( latter-
sail ”), 125,000 shares of £1 each, the estate being
the firpt prize, and 120 other prizes T racehorses.
brood mares, celebrated sires, a dairy herd, a
flock of sheep, &c. all valuable and easily realis-
able effects. The fact that Mr. George Adams is
conducting the distribution should Ai
tralians that everything will be conducted in
honorable and straightforward manner which has
characterised all his transactions with the public.
The shares are going off rapidly.
The April number of Cosmos is much the
best vet issued. The articles on Marcus Clarke,
by Ciias. Bright; on “An Australian School of
Literature,” by E. A. Badham ; on Horne and
Deniehy, by G. B. Barton ; and on Charles Brad-
laugh, by Ernest Besant-Scott, are particularly
interesting, but by no means exhaust the interest.
“ A Man of Method,” by “ Samuel Oliall White,”
is a slight but entertaining story ; and the
“poetry ”is beneath notice. One bard (Scottish,
of course) begins—
An upturned boat on the beach lay dry,
And the little waves tickled the shells thereby.
“ Tickled ! ” “ Thereby ! ” Eh, mon ! but yon’s
juist awfu’.
Mitchell’s “ Newspaper Press Directory ”
—the best publication of its kind with which The
Bulletin is acquainted—reaches, with 1805, b
50th year of issue. The jubilee is celebrated, so
far as Australasia is concerned, by interesting
historical articles on the press of each province,
well written and rarely inaccurate. The “Direc-
tory ” is up-to-date and reliable, and does credit
to its editor and his helpers.
Many ladies who favor that most becoming
fashion of tailor-made gowns have lost sight of the
King of Ladies’ Tailors in Australasia—Mr. B.
King, late of Coll ins-street, and, we are sure, will
be pleased to discover him again. We are able to
give the information. Wo. now find him with
Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson and Sons, the successors
of the old-established firm, Moubray, Rowan and
Hicks, who, under his guidance, have made a
special department for Ladies’ Tailoring—a branch
which it is not surprising to see daily increases in
favor. It is to Mr. King that ladies have to be
grateful for a new patent riding-skirt, which
minimises the danger of a fall.

A number of Chows were fined =£10 and costs
at Melb., the other day, for running ticket-lotteries. Tne
profits of the business will, however, stand a lot of such
hues. A clerk in one of the most religious Melb. houses
was engaged in the nefarious occupation of “ marking a
ticket ” in one of the branches of the lottery-bank, the
other day, when a policeman appeared in the doorway.
Police-court publicity meant certain dismissal, and there
was no opportunity for flight; so, great was the relief of
the when the Celestial welcomed the minion of
the law in a familiar and friendly manner with: ‘‘Well
you blank, dash, crimson, red-headed Ilishman, what you
want? ’ And the latter replied in a similar vein, winding
up with a demand for a ticket. Later that evening the
raid xvas made.

London papers to hand announce the death and
burial of W. H. Sprague’s buli-dawg, Don Pedro. The
obsequies were of an imposing character. Deceased went
to his last home in a polished oak coffin, covered with the
Union Jack and a mass of floral tributes, whilst one of ins
sons, dressed in black canine garments for the occasion
acted as chief mourner. The coffin’s brass plate bore the
inscription, “Don Pedro, an old English bull dog ; born
August, 1882 ; died February, 1895.’’ There is something
very touching about cockney reverence for bull-dogs,
which are popularly regarded as the representatives of
‘ true British pluck” among the animal creation. The
smaller and flabbier the cockney the greater his belief in
British pluck.
* * * *
At Lake Macquarie, N.S.W., not long ago, the
crews of three fishing-boats were wuicning, with nets for
the incoming mullet-schools at the narrow channel con-
necting lake and ocean, and when the record school
came along the fishers spliced together all their nets
making a total length of about a mile. For three weeks
the three boats were carrying the fish to market. The
fish, during their captivity, were held in the nets and fed
—which could only he accomplished in a place like Lake
Macquarie, where the tide rises and falls just about 18in.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin —In Victoria I’ll concede the
palm to no one as a fish-liar, but Tasmanian talent drives
me out of the big. Whilst fishing at St. George’s Bay
St. Helens, during the holidays, I had the rare luck to’
catch a genuine English mackerel (i.e., identical in every
respect with the mackerel caught in English waters) The
friends who were with me regarded it as a rara places
and it was much admired in the local pub. by everyone
except the local “know-all,” who assured us that it was a
very common fish in St. George’s Bay indeed, he
himself had, with a rod and line, only a week before com-
pletely filled this same boat with the same fish in’three
houis. The carrying capacity of that boat was seven and
a halj tons.— Moorite.

Melb. football clubs’final rehearsals on Saturday
week were mainly devoted to settling the claims of new'
talent. The chief match, Esiendon v. Combined Juniors,
showed that last year’s premiers have some remarkably
good men to replace losses, Norman Waugh, Jones, and a
darkie named Johnson being top-sawyers. In fact, it is
doubtful if Essendon will not he stronger than last year.
In any case, this year’s premiership going to Melb. F.C.
is by no means a foregone conclusion.

Living Pictures of the Warl
After the taking of Port Arthur(wliich virtually
finished the war), Mr. Villiers was on the point of
starting for England, when he was specially invited
by Mr. R. S. Smythe (by cable) to come to Aus-
tralia to relate the moving incidents by flood and
field of that exciting contest between China and
Japan, in which modern ships and armaments
were used for the first time with such astounding
results. Mr. Villiers’ lectures will be illustrated by
Wonderful Realistic Sketches
ASB
Illuminated Snapshot Photographs
of the various battles, including the great massacre
at Port Arthur, of which his photographs of those
thrilling scenes were the only ones taken.

DIRECTORS.
(To be appointed at First Meeting of Shareholders.)
BANKERS.
The BANK OF AUSTRALASIA, MELBOURNE, and
Branches.
THIS Company is formed to acquire Fifteen auriferous sections, immediately adjacent to the cele-
brated Harris Reward Cold Mine, at Mount Jukes, West Tasmania, and to work or re-sell all
or any of the same. Ihe sections are known or numbered as follows on the accompanying plan of the
district: Nos. 7 (otherwise Sailor Jack), 9, 12, 14, 34. 36, 39, 44, and A, B, C, D, E, F and G. The
sections comprise an area of about 140 acres.
As will be seen from the plan, what is believed to be Harris’ Reward rich reef, has been found on
section No. 9. An auriferous reef has also been located on section B, whilst a third reef is proved on
section No. 7 (Sailor Jack).
The developments now taking place on Harris’ Reward Claim prove the existence of an enormous
body of highly payable quartz, the reef being from 8 to 12 feet wide, and in the opinion of the Manager
(Mr. Nugent) good for 6to 7oz. per ton. In his report of 16th April on the Harris claim, Mr. Nugent
states :‘ 1 have extended Harris’ trench 6 feet further South. There is a splendid show in it The
reef is 12 feet wide, and every stone a good specimen. I sunk a shaft 28 feet deep on the Western
wall, at the extreme Northern end. There is a grand reef in sight, very rich, and a 6-inch leader
extremely rich.
aurif * W llH ' l,BVec * t,iafc ever y Ol >e of the sections proposed to be acquired by the Company will prove
S . i ..portant are the developments on the Harris Reward Mine (which was only discovered and
taken up last N-w Year’s Day) considered by the Government of Tasmania that despite the depleted
condition of the treasury, the Government are constructing a road thereto at a cost of .£450—,£300 to
be paid by the Government of Tasmania, £5O by the Harris’ Reward Gold Mine, and .£lOO by this
proprietary. J
The intention of the proprietary is, as developments proceed on the Harris’ Reward, and on the
section! belonging to the proprietary, to re-sell to subsidiary Companies on favorable terms a portion
oral of the proprietary sections. It will easily be understood that a considerable profit may be
den ved from the re-sale of these sections.
There is no doubt that the rich alluvial gold, worked in the immediate neighbourhood of Mount
Juk es, was derived from the line of reef traversing these sections.
As an evidence of their belief in the mine, the Vendors are content to take a large portion of the
purchase consideration in contributing and other shares.
If no allotment, the purchase money will be returned in full; in the case of allotted shares, if the
allotment-money is not paid within eight days of the allotment, the allotment may be cancelled and
the application deposit forfeited to the Company,

"The Bulletin's" Scrap-Book.
THE LITTLE ABBEY OF CARENNAC.
(On the Dordogne).
jjere—in God’s house of the open dome—
Vigil is kept by the pilgrim breeze ;
Here, from its sun-illumined tome,
Labor intones its litanies.
For discipline, here is the chastening rain ;
For burden, the fruit of the bending tree ;
The thorn of the rose for a pleasant pain,
And palm for a costless victory.
Oh ! if my vow but bound to these,
. Twere long ere this laggard step grew slack.
O, that the wilful world would please
To leave me my flocks, my birds, and bees,
My ivied stall and my hours of ease,
And my little Abbey of Carennac !
Far from the city’s guarded gate,
Free from the crush of its silken crowds,
I see the sun in his purple state,
And,the changing face of the courtier-clouds.
My thoughts are mine when my task is sped ;
My head aches not, and my heart is full ;
And the laurels that cumber my careless tread
Are the only ones that I choose to pull.
Away from my friends I love them best;
Away from my books no lore I lack :
H-re—no longer a flying guest,
With wavering foot that finds no rest—
Truth comes home to this lonely breast
In the little Abbey of Oareimac.
Thus, half-hid from the smile of Spring
Under the bough of a blossomed tree,
My single wish is the grace to sing
The praise of a spot where a bard should be.
Sounding clear as the forest call—
Wakening man in the monarch’s breast,
Many-voiced as the waters fail—
Speaking to every soul’s unrest.
My song should seize with a minstrel sway
Yon green twin-isles and their busy bac,
The hamlet white and the convent grey,
And the lodge for the wanderer on his way,
And thus tn mv France in n.v lit.fle lay
Give my little Abbey of Carennac.
To journey again o'er the hard highway ;
To enter a garrulous, troublous train ;
Uncalled to come, and unbid obey,
To feign it pleasure, and feel it pain.
To float—a straw on an idle stream ;
To glitter—a mote by the sunbeam sought;
To walk—a shade in a waking dream ;
To strive for nothings where all is naught.
An iron tongue to summon away,
And a rope of sand to hold me back,
Are the call to go, and the will to stay—
Clamorous duty and still delay ;
0 gilded gloom ! O green and gay
Of my little Abbey of Carennac !
Fields that teem with the fruits of peace,
Let your reapers reap, and your binders bind,
1 cannot flee for a fond caprice
Yon stony spot to my hand assigned.
To me are numbered the seeds that grow ;
Not mine the loss of the perished grain,
If working I watch for the time to sow,
And waiting pray for the sun and rain.
My day to God and the King I lend ;
The wish of my heart will bring me back
A few last, lightsome hours to spend,
And to pass with my lifelong looked-for friend,
Through a quiet night and a perfect end,
From my little Abbey of Carennac.
FENELON.
The name of the translator of Fthielon’s mo-t charming
poem is unknown to The Bulletin. What Goldsmith in
his “Vicar of Wakefield” and “Deserted Village” was
to his countrymen, so F6nelon, in compositions such as
the foregoing, was to Frenchmen. For purity of senti-
ment and delicate beauty of language, F&ielon is un-
rivalled in any tongue.
