HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
Prince of Wales
On a Wet Day.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is raining heavily and hopelessly in a very
small township among the mountains some seventy
miles from Sydney, The landlord of the big,
empty hotel Jeans over the counter of the empty
bar, and looks out speechlessly at the muddy road
which leads both ways to nowhere.
Presently two bright young Australians enter
and demand whisky. The landlord apparently
knows them well, for he says, curtly : “ Got the
money ?”
They haven’t got the money, but one of them
opines that the landlord might trust a blanky
bloke.
The landlord responds, briefly : “ I’m off you.”
The pair take the rebuff in good part. They
remark, in a friendly manner, that the landlord is
“a blanky hard case, blanked if he isn’t.” Then
they wander to the door and look out at the thick
atmosphere, which is full of strange noises like
those a cow makes trying to get its foot out of the
mud, and at a forlorn bullock-team going past,
and at a wet hen scudding under bare poles for a
place of shelter.
After a while one of them says, in an abstracted
fashion; “Gor’ strike me blind,” and the other
one silently acquiesces.
By-and-bye, eight road-men tramp in heavily
and remark that it is a blanky sort of a day, and
call for whisky. The landlord regards them sus-
piciously, but fills the order. The spokesman puts
down some money and offers to owe him the other
sixpence.
The land lord e vid ently disapproves of this arrange-
ment, but the customers remind liim that five of
them have had the blanky horrors in his house
within the last month, also that they had all spent
all their blanky cheques there, and they state that
he might trust a crimson bloke, and one of them
offers to fight him or any other blanky hiatus for
the dashed sixpence, and at last peace is restored.
Then there is an interval of silence until the
man in the corner lifts up his voice in a monologue
and utters eight oaths and five indecent expres-
sions. He addresses himself to nobody in par-
ticular and nobody answers him. After he has
finished off with a specially bright and exhilarating
curse there is more silence.
Suddenly, at one end of the room, a great and
violent shindy arises out of nothing.
The man with the patched pants is holding forth
at a great rate to the man with the torn hat.
“I can fight the best blanky man in this room,”
he says, enthusiastically. “I can fight the best
blanky man in the town. I’ll fight the best
blanky man in Australia an’ knock him out in
three blanky rounds. I’ll fight you now for a
blanky quid.”
The man with the torn hat simply says : “ Yer
a blanky cow,” but the smallest man in the room
sails in with a yell; “ I’ll fight -you for a hundred
blanky quid an’ put the money down.”
Then there is chaos and pandemonium. One
after another drifts into the riot until eight men
have agreed to fight each other on the spot for an
aggregate of .£lOOO or so. In the course of the
discussion each man has called every other one a
blanky liar at least twelve times, also each one
has called the rest nearly every indecent expres-
sion he knows of. The yells and vociferations of
the company can be heard all over the township.
Meanwhile the landlord looks on in sleepy unoon-
ctrn, and the wet hen, who has evidently beard
it all before, steps in out of the rain.
When the parties have all agreed to fight imme-
diately for enormous amounts they sib down or
lean against the wall, and the subject drops.
Apparently any person who proposed to fight in
real earnest would be scouted as a lunatic. No
one hits another. No one offers to put up any one
of the vast sums that are to be fought for. No
one even seriously resents being called a liar and
various other things as well. The fiercest-looking
desperado in the crowd steps out to look at the
weather and comes back disgusted.
After a while one of the party asks another to
sing some thing or other. The general con-
sensus of opinion is that the person in question
can’t sing worth a cent. He settles the point
by lifting up his voice in a rough but pathetic
melody with the refrain : “ Do you ever think of
mother.” It is tolerably well sung, and the com-
pany stops swearing till it is finished. Then one
person, who seems almost affected, states that it
is a good song. He also adds, approvingly,
that the melodist is a blanky hog at singing.
Two more men drop in. One is very wet; the
other partially drunk and cross. The former
shouts for the crowd; the latter states that the
audience are all —— cows.
Someone asks the wet intruder if he has heard
from Dick.
A voice from the corner interposes the state-
ment that Dick is a cow.
Someone else remarks irrelevantly that Dick can
jump all right, and asks, in a large and general
fashion, that Providence may strike him pink.
Then the drunken intruder says that Dick can’t
jump over a blanky egg, and a shambling person
says that he can jump sft. lOin, standing, and the
man with the worst hat says he can jump six
blanky feet, and another person offers to jump the
best flaming man in New South Wales for fifty
flaming quid, and everybody talks at once, and
calls every other person a liar with embellish-
ments, and the old pandemonium starts all over
again.
And, in the midst of it, a peaoeful-looking man
who is sitting in the corner lifts up his voice on
his own account and altogether apart from the
question at issue, and says : “ !! 1
,” and much more to the same effect. The
most striking feature about this statement is that
there isn’t one publishable expression in three
sentences. Nobody takes any notice of him. By
this time a tall, slab-sided man is holding his belt
at a height of seven feet above the floor and offer-
ing to jump over it there and then for a hundred
blanky quid. Everybody agrees to jump imme-
diately for a hundred blanky quid and put the
money down. Then the subject drops. Nobody
puts down a single copper and nobody jumps.
A cracked bell rings, and a few who are ap-
parently more solvent than the rest go in to
dinner. They ask each other to pass various
things, and blaspheme cheerfully all round. The
landlord’s wife and a rather pretty girl who waits
at the table take it all as a matter of course. After
dinner one sneaks away into the kitchen and
makes a matrimonial proposal to the waiting-
maid. It transpires that he is aconstant admirer,
and proposes ineffectually at every visit. On wet
days he hangs about the hotel and proposes more
or less all day long. As I pass the kitchen-door
some scraps of his wooing are wafted out along
with the odour of hash and other things. He is
telling the girl that he will blanky well marry her
any blanky time she will have him. He also
expresses a wish that Providence may strike him
blue. As he is struck considerably blue already,
with four days’ stubble on his face, this seems
superfluous.
It still rains dismally. The landlord lounges
about and refuses to be negotiated with, and the
damp hen cruises round dejectedly.
All through the long, dank afternoon the men
sib around and lie. There is a great and dreadful
sameness about their lies. One of them once
owned a dashed horse that was faster than any
other blanky horse in the country. Some of
the others call him a liar with many choice varia-
tions, and the rest all owned another dashed horse
which was much faster still. Another of them
had licked the most formidable pugilist ever known
in Australia, and the rest had licked a much more
formidable one. A small, lank-haired man had
run 100 blanky yards in nine seconds, and it pre-
sently transpires that almost all the rest had done
the same blanky distance in eight seconds. They
had all ridden fabulous distances in the shortest
time on record, and had jumped quite unprece-
dented heights, and each one had utterly extin-
guished everybody he met in every possible line of
industry. They are all prepared to bet fifty
blanky quid that they did all these things, and a
hundred blanky quid that they will do them
again, on the spot, at that very instant if required.
Most of them call each other many different kinds
of liars, and almost all of them bet a hundred
blanky quid that they can do all these things better
and more effectually than any one else present.
Nothing happens.
About five o’clock the two young Australians
have a disagreement, and undertake to tear each
other’s liver out for twopence. Nobody is
injured, however.
The lying, and the disagreement between the
two young Australians, and much plug tobacco,
fill in time somehow till tea. Then the bell rings
again, and the same crowd go in and blaspheme in
the presence of the pretty waiting-maid as before.
Also, the same individual drifts into the kitchen
afterwards, and doubtless renews his overtures in
the same form of language as heretofore.
Then they fill in the long, wet evening with
more plug tobacco, and tell all the old lies over
again without variations, and get up the same old
rows, and agree in the same old language to fight
each other for a hundred quid, and to run
each other for a hundred quid, and to jump
each other for a hundred quid, and to do
all manner of things for the same large and pro-
fane sum of money. Yet nobody fights, or jumps,
or runs, or does anything worth mentioning.
At nine o’clock I go to bed, but the voices of
the company rise up from below and keep me
awake, and the same old horse-lie comes up again
and again, and so do all the other lies and curses,
and until eleven o’clock or later a dissonant voice
is heard every few minutes agreeing to fight some-
body else for a hundred blanky quid. Then there
is silence, and more rain, and a holy calm.
Next morning it is still raining, and the same
crowd, or another just like it, is there again in
the middle of the same old performance, so I
decide to go on my way. And as I depart I hear
the voice of somebody who is telling his weary
horse-lie over again for the fiftieth time, and
somebody else who is relating how he licked a
champion pugilist in three rounds, and another
man who has jumped sft. 10in., or more, stand-
ing, and they are all backing themselves to do it
again on the spot for more blanky quid than any-
body can remember.
If I ever meet any of these men again in this
world I will know them by that dismal horse-lie
they are always telling. And if I ever meet them
in the next world I will know them, for I will
hear a discordant voice offering to jump'Gabriel
the Archangel for a hundred blanky quid."
TITUS SALT.
Face-massage—not the good old style of
treatment to be obtained for the asking in Mick
Nathan’s saloon—is becoming popular in Mel-
bourne amongst the few who can afford to pay for
it. The art is, however, as yet in its infancy.
There is a fortune waiting for the man who dis-
covers how to restore red noses to their original
color.
Panton, P.M. (Melb.), complains bitterly
that prisoners are too well-fed in the local gaol,
and offenders often let him see when he is sen-
tencing them to three months that they take it as
a kindness. The crying need of the city, it seems,
is a damp, cold, slimy prison for winter male-
factors, with long waits between meals, and un-
stinted lumbago and cold in the head for all
boarders. The magistrate, full of dignity, who
thinks to strike terror to the heart of a vagrant
with an eight-weeks’ sentence, and is pathetically
requested to “make it three months, couldn’t
you, please, guv’ner?” feels that he has outlived
his usefulness and is only an empty show.
It is said, on apparently good authority,
that one of the Syme clan, is chewing over plans
for t'u* establishment of a second evening paper in
Melbourne. There is a feeling in press circles that
a rival to the Herald is inevitable shortly.
Meanwhile, the only evening paper in the
Southern capital is about to shift its quarters.
It is leaving Collins-street, and taking up its abode
at the old De Graves bond building in Flinders-
street.
For rheumatism use Colemane and Sons
Eucalypte Special Oil.
At Bathurst (N.S.W.), the other day, the
“ first Convention of local preachers in connection
with the Wesleyan Church ” was presided over
by Mr. J. 0. White, who has resided in the dis-
trict for over 50 years. Mr. White has a unique
record. He was one of the earliest settlers in
South Australia in the time of Hindmarsh and
Light, and built the first Wesleyan Church at the
Port with his own hands. The building still
stands but is now used as a printing-office. Mr.
White was one of the pioneers of the press over
the Blue Mountains, having for many years owned
the Bathurst Free Press newspaper, which he
bought from the founder, Mr. Farrer.
In Maoriland a sweet, gushing maid of
16 has just been married to a gilded old bachelor
of 69. “ They’d marry auld Nick if he’d keep
them aye braw.”
In London authors make a hit
By writing as they best think fib
Of bushmen on the wallaby
That stab and shoot and drink and die
And show true Anglo-Saxon grit.
The critics never see the skit
Where print and picture doubly lie, .
The libel never brings a writ
In London.
Oh, when will someone here commit
A like atrocity, to wit—
A guide to London, written by
A resident of Gundagai ?
Oh, why are bonfires never lib
In London ?

Our Pipes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The moon rose away out on the edge of a smoky
plain, seen through a sort of tunnel or arch in the
fringe of mulga behind which we were camped—
Jack Mitchell and I. The “timber ” proper was
just behind us, very thick and very dark. The
moon looked like a big new copper-boiler set on
edge on the horizon of the plain, with the top
turned towards us and a lot of old rags and straw
burning inside.
We had tramped 25 miles on a dry stretch on a
hot day—swagmen know what- that means. We
reached the water about two hours “after dark ”
—swagmen know what that means. We didn’t
sit down at once and rest —we hadn’t rested for
the last ten miles. We knew that if we sat down
we wouldn't Van t to get up again in a hurry—
that, if we did, our leg-sinews, especially those of
our calves, would “draw” like red-hot wires.
You see, we hadn’t been long on the track this
time—it was only our third day out. Swagmen
will understand.
We got the billy boiled first, and some leaves
laid down for our beds and the swags rolled out.
We thanked the Lord that we had some cooked
meat and a few “johnny-cakes” left—for we
didn’t feel equal to cooking. We put the billy of
tea and our tucker-bags between the heads of our
beds, and the pipes and tobacco in the crown of
an old hat, where we could reach them without
having to get up. Then we lay down on our
stomachs'and had a feed. We didn’t eat much—
we were too tired for that—but we drank a lot of
tea. We gave our calves time to tone down a bit;
then we lit up and began to answer each other.
It got to be pretty comfortable, so long as we kept
those unfortunate legs of ours straight, and didn’t
move round much.
We cursed society because we weren’t rich men,
and then we felt better and conversation drifted
lazily round various subjects and ended in that of
smoking.
“ How I came to start smoking?”said Mitchell.
“Let’s see.” (Mitchell reflected.) “ I started
smoking first when I was about 14 or 15. I smoked
some sort of weed—l forget the name of it—but it
wasn’t tobacco ; and then I smoked cigarettes,
not the ones we get now, for those cost a penny
each. Then I reckoned that, if I could smoke
them, I could smoke a pipe.”
Mitchell reflected.
“ We lived in Sydney then —Surry Hills. Those
were different times—the place was nearly all
sand. The old folks were alive then, and we were
all at home, ’cepting Tom.”
Mitchell reflected.
“ Ah, well! . . . Well, one evening I was
playing marbles out in front of our house when a
chap we knew gave me his pipe to mind while he
went into a church-meeting. The little church
was opposite—a 1 chapel ’ they called it.”
Mitchell reflected.
“The pipe was alight. It was a clay pipe and
nigger-head tobacco. Mother was at work out in
the kitchen at the back, washing up the tea-things,
and, when I went in, she said : ‘ You’ve been
smoking!’
“Well, I couldn’t deny it —I was too sick to do
so, or care much, anyway.
“ ‘ Give me that pipe ! ’ she said.
“I said I hadn’t got it.
“ 1 Give—me — that — pipe ! ’ she said,
“ I said I hadn’t got it.
“ ‘ Where is it ? ’ ” she said.
“Jim Brown’s got it,” I said, “ it’s his.”
“ ‘ Then I’ll give it to Jim Brown,’she said, and
she did ; though it wasn’t Jim’s fault, for he only
gave it to me to mind. I didn’t smoke the pipe
so much because I wanted to smoke a pipe so bad
just then, but more because I had such a great
admiration for Jim.”
Mitchell reflected, and took a look at the moon.
It had risen clear and had got small and cold and
pure-looking, and had floated away back out
amongst the stars.
“I felt better towards morning, but it didn’t
cure me—being sick and nearly dead all night, I
mean. I got a clay pipe and tobacco, and the old
lady found it and put it in the stove. Then I got
another pipe and tobacco, and she ‘laid for it,’
and found it out at last; but she didn’t put
the tobacco in the stove this time—she’d got
experience. I don’t know what she did
with it. I tried to find it, but couldn’t. I fancy
the old man got hold of it, for I saw him with a
plug that looked very much like mine.”
Mitchell reflected.
“ But I wouldn’t be done. I got a cherry pipe.
I thought it wouldn’t be so easy to break if she
found it. I used to plant the bowl in one place
and the stem in another because I reckoned that
if she found one she mightn’t find the other. It
don’t look much of an idea now, but it seemed
like an inspiration then. Kids get rum ideas.”
Mitchell reflected.
“ Well, one day I was having a smoke out at the
back, when I heard her coming, and I pulled out
the stem in a hurry and put the bowl behind the
water-butt and the stem under the house. Mother
was coming round for a dipper of water. I got
Out of her way quick, for I hadn’t time to look
innocent; but the bowl of the pipe was hot and she
got a whiff of it. She went sniffing round, first
on one side of the cask and then on the other,
until she got on the scent and followed it up and
found the bowl. Then I had only the stem left.
She looked for that, but she couldn’t scent it. But
I couldn’t get much comfort out of that. Have
you got the matches?
“ Then I gave it best for a time and smoked
cigars. They were the safest and most satisfac-
tory under the circumstances, but they cost me
two shillings a week, and I couldn’t stand it, so I
started a pipe again and then mother gave in at
last. God bless her, and God forgive me, and us
all—we deserve it. She’s been at rest these 17
long years.”
Mitchell reflected.
“ And what did your old man do when he found
out that you were smoking? ” I asked Mitchell.
“The old man ? ”
Mitchell reflected.
“ Well, he seemed to brighten up at first. You
see, he was a sort of pensioned-off by mother and
she kept him pretty well inside his income. . . .
Well, he seemed to sort of brighten up—liven up
when he found out that I was smoking.”
“Did he? So did my old man, and he livened
me up, too. But what did your old man do—what
did he say ?”
“ Well,” said Mitchell, very slowly, “ about the
first thing he did was to ask me for a fill.”
Mitchell reflected.
“ Ah ! many a solemn, thoughtful old smoke wo
had together on the quiet—the old man and me.”
Mitchell reflected.
“Is your old man dead, Mitchell?” I asked,
softly.
“Long ago—these twelve years,” said Mitchell.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Duchess of Albany is 34. A London
weekly observes : “ Her Royal Highness looks
very fit and well, and it is a pity, if it is true as
alleged, that the Queen will not yet allow the
Duchess to marry again.” A fit-and-well widow
prohibited from matrimony is indeed a subject for
compassion.
At East Brighton (Vic.), a little while ago,
a gardener found a black snake and out it in two
with his scythe. The top half escaped, but the
tail end remained and was killed. Later on, the
family cat found the upper end and brought it on
the verandah, and a few hours afterwards the
half-snake bit the gardener’s little daughter and
killed her.
Charles Gavan Daffy’s autobiography is on
the point of issue in London. In his treatment of
his Mel bourne experiences O’Shanassy must surely
loom up. When this very big man joined in the
effusive welcome to Duffy, which included the
presentation to him of a house and land at Haw-
thorn, the Melb. suburb, total cost, .£2OOO, all
raised by subscription amid the “rebels to the
backbone and spinal marrow,” ’cute critics saw
the rift, because two such Hibernian lions could
not both roar along one shore. Sure enough, they
had a fine old row, because Duffy thought the
great O’Shan, conspired, as Premier, with Attor-
ney-General Dick Ireland, to make the Duffy Land
Act abortive.
Bernard Shaw, Tas. Commissioner of
Police, is also acting P.M. for Hobart, and, in the
former capacity, investigates a case one day which
he has to hear in the latter capacity the next!
When he first mounted the Bench, he departed
considerably from the policy of his predecessor
(who hung tenaciously to the practices of the old
days—fining a first-offending drunk ss, with the
option of seven days, and other offences pro rata),
by letting the aforesaid first-offenders off with the
option of 24 hours’ gaol, and exhibiting similar
leniency all round. A man lias now got a much
better chance against the policeman than in the
days of the late P.M. Shaw has also dispensed with
the old laborious process of taking evidence in the
numerous trivial cases which crop up in police-
courts all over the world.
Rev. J. D. Langley, appointed to be
Archdeacon of Cumberland (N.S.W.), was, like
temperance-advocate Boyce and several other
Anglican clerical lights, a bank-clerk and manager
in early life, alternating the computing of interest
with the interpreting of texts in Newcastle
(N.S.W.) for many years. His last church, St.
Phillip’s, Sydney, was, in its first stage of exist-
ence, burnt down by convicts who hated to be
dragooned to prayers there every Sunday, and it
is worth noting in illustration of the logic of that
far-off period that the Governor, feeling satisfied
that the incendiary convicts desired a lazy Sun-
day, got even with them by ordering all the work-
ing gangs to be occupied on succeeding Sundays
in the building of a new church. Pastor Langley,
while at St. Phillip’s, fiddled at philanthropy,
and was a species of civilised Rev. Phillip Moses.
The N.S.W. Civil Service Inquiry Com-
mission has been posting complaints about the
enormities committed under claims for “ex-
penses.” ’T was ever thus inmost grades of life.
The entity travelling for himself and the entity
travelling for a firm are two distinct and separate
creatures. In the “good old” ante-telephonic
and ante-country-correspondentdays of journalism
in Melbourne, the “ex’s” often overtopped the
salary. David Syme, of the Age, was fairly
liberal, as things went, but once ho got a
“ scorcher ” from a “ rantin’, roarin’ ” reporter. It
was Gov. Bowen’s last public function in Victoria
—the opening of the Stawell-Horsham railway, at
Horsham—a three-days’ absence from Melbourne,
The rail went round by Geelong in those
days, the more direct Ballarat-Melbourne line
not having been then constructed. A special
carriage and refreshments were provided for the
press, so that transit ex.’s were actually nil. The
little township of Horsham, when reached, was
so crowded that a bed couldn’t be had for love or
money. So that (given the banquet), beyond break-
fast next morning, and a late bowl of bread and
milk at Craig’s Hotel, Ballarat, on the return
journey (that was Peter Lalor’s suggestion), and a
tip to Craig’s night-waiter, the expenses were still
nought. But the press boys played Nap., and the
Age man lost <£B or so. Quo’ Pugh (now dead,
poor fellow), of the Argus, drily—“ Stick it on tc
your ex.’s.” “I can’t ; there are so few ex.’s this
trip.” “Ah! but it’s a bad precedent on your paper
not to have ex.’s ; or they’ll expect the next man
on a really costly journey to do it cheap.” Pugh,
of course, was only “guying,” but the thought
stuck. The Age man did put it in his ex.’s,
lumped up baldly as—“ Three days, Stawell to
Horsham, £9.” That bill stuck so long on
David Syme’s file that at last the bold author of
it bearded the boss and asked after its welfare.
“Aw don’t onnerstan’ it,” said Syme. “Simple
enough —expensive trip—three days away.” “Yes,
but ye had free railway.” “Of course, bub the
night at Horsham—township crowded—exorbitant
charges—and, of course, I had to represent the Age
worthily.” “ Weel, that may be; but gie me
items.” “ Oh, it’s items you want. Certainly !”
The reporter spoke gaily, but his heart was
beneath his diaphragm as he went to concoct
trie items. Presently he returned with a formid-
able list. “ I find, Mr. Syme, I’ve been cheating
myself. I lumped it at'£9, but I find, by the
items, that it’s <£9 Bs. 4d.” And he handed over
the account. He had overshot the mark in his
imaginative itemisation, but was too exhausted
with excitement to go through the trouble a second
time. Syme commenced reading half-aloud -
“Special cab, 6 a.m., from Emerald Hill to
Spencer-street station, 7s. 6d.; Age, Argus (3d.),
Daily Telegraph, 6d.; cup of coffee and a bun,
Is.; book to read on journey, 2s. fid.; flask of
brandy, 3s. fid.; mutton chop and a bottle of
porter at Geelong, 2s. fid.; bottle of porter for
friend, Is. fid. ” “There, there,” bursting out
laughing and appending the magic initials “D. 5.,”
which made the bill cash downstairs, “I’m satis-
fied !” So was the rep.

"THE BULLETIN."
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly-Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Js the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy,
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
H be elected by constituencies'as at present;
tiie other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushrnah of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
po[)ular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent Bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than an
other paper, because its vast mass of original an
specially written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
